CURED BY Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure.
Those who have taken this medicine are amazed
at its wonderful influence. Sufferers from any
form of bronchitis, distressing cough, difficulty of
breathing, hoarseness, pain or soreness in the
chest, experience delightful and immediate relief,
and to those who are subject to colds on the chest
it is invaluable, as it effects a complete cure. It
is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure is certain.
“Mr. VV. Q. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —Your medicine has
cured me of bronchitis and asthma, from which I suffered
for upwards of seven years, during which period I was
scarcely ever free from coughs, and frequently the diffi-
culty of breathing was so distressing that for nights in
succession I had to sit up. 1 write you this acknowledg-
ment from a sense of duty, as in my case every other
treatment had failed. For a year previous I had been
getting very much worse, and at the time I obtained your
medicine 1 was confined to bed suffering from a most
violent cough, expectorating blood and matter, and
apparently beyond hope of recovery. The first dose of
the medicine gave me welcome relief, and I steadily
improved as I continued the treatment, until I became,
as I am now, quite well.—Yours sincerely, If. WALKER,
Balmain, Sydney."

Dr. aiBBONS,
Who is trebly-qualified, and registered in London
and the Colonies as a Physician and a Surgeon,
received his education in the English and Scottish
universities, and completed his studies in the lead-
ing hospitals of Europe. He has made a life-long
study of
PRIVATE, NERVOUS & SKIN DISEASES.
He has, by diligent research, investigated their
origin and course, and has discovered the quickest
and surest means of thoroughly curing them. He
has, by his unequalled success, during a period of
many years, obtained the highest position in his
profession. The various diseases embraced in his
speciality are those which have hitherto caused
more misery and suffering than any other kind of
trouble that affects the human frame.
Young Men and Those Who
Wish to Marry
who, as the result of early follies, or excesses of
more mature age, suffer from any of the follow-
ing symptoms, namely :—Frequent headaches,
giddiness, drowsiness in the daytime, irregularity
of the bowels, feeling of fullness after eating,
specks before the eyes, halos or circles round a
candle, cold hands and feet, pains in the back or
under the shoulder-blade, tenderness of the breast-
bone on pressure, yellow discharge from the
mouth on to the pillow during sleep, palpitation
of the heart, unpleasant dreams, clammy hands,
an aversion to society, a whitish or cloudy sedi-
ment in the urine after standing, fear of something
dreadful about to happen, impure blood, spots or
pimples on the back, chest, legs, or face, failing
memory, hacking cough, falling of the hair, &e.,
&c., should consult him, for he is now enabled
quickly and effectually, to remove the cause, and
so prevent life-long misery.

vvmich hays paid ad valorem or
OTHER oe THE repealed duties,
>\fEL BE OFFERED AT REDUCED
1 RICES, so as to bring them in

things.
The Amount of the Reductions
Is fixed by the amount of duty paid. All goods
on which TEN PER CENT, has been paid will
be reduced TEN PER CENT., while goods
bearing FIFTEEN PER CENT, duty will be
reduced FIFTEEN PER CENT. This is an
edict, from which no manner of departure will be
allowed.
The Thoroughness of the
Reductions
Cannot be cpiestioned. When the whole of the
re-marking is done, every article will show on its
ticket, or in some other way, the OLD PRICE
and the NEW PRICE at which the line is offered.
The Effect of the Red net ions
Must be a great loss to ourselves. We desire to
be candid, and we repeat it will be a great loss.
But we are not distressed at that. W owe the
people a DEBT OF GRATITJDE WE CAN
NEVER DISCHARGE, but the change in the
national policy gives us an opportunity, and we
hasten to pay SOMETHING-ON ACCOUNT.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A SOUTHERN SHORE.
Here the hollows are wave-enchanted,
Here are the winds that, scourge. 1 thru’ z .lies
Hlow with trumpets an anthem haunted,
Hreams of coral and dead men’s bones.
Over the bars, the foam, the thunder,
-rurple delights and sluiced lagoons,
Over the reefs that shatter asunder
-h lery waves in million moons ;
Over the ships that move untiring,
Lovely as floating madrepores,
a soul desiring
ultimate slumber on the shores,
Here away from the world’s endeavor
I, the gull, and the cloud are three,
i wo the same for ever and ever,
Inaccessible unto me.
They, from dawning of time, have floated
Careless over the earth and sea,
Ne er like mortals by darkness moated,
■Dungeoned spirits that would be free.
Bird, like music, and love, and morning,
Day’s forerunner, eternal joy,
T, ‘o" and all wings that beat are scorning
Man too heavy with sad alloy.
.Though the cycles of ages gather,
i 1 hou i’ the waves’ aerial snow
Fee lest rapture in every feather,
Ceaseless ecstacy thou dost go !
Thee no sorrow each age is bringing,
Joy for ever from bowy lips
Throws thee kisses, and ocean’s surging
Ihrills thy pennon’s tremulous lips.
Happy thou that Art ever flying,
Bold with a keen desire of life,
And, when death is upon thee, dying
Not with a soul’s despairing strife.
Thou art the same as birds that -fold. -d
Wings on the waves of a winsome world
Th ine delirious motion moulded
Under the winds at dawning hurled.
And for ever thy path is Fancy’s,
Seas, the sun, and the clouds thy peers,
And, like echoes of old romances,
Thou recallest our careless years.
Wellington, M.L.
HUBERT CHURCH.
A NOCTURNE.
The lamp of night is poised above the ocean ;
The long, bright billows swamp the sinuous
caves
Which mock their music; then with wild com-
motion
Rush out again to meet the braver waves,
To plunge with them once more through echoing
gloom,
Dread as the hollow darkness of the tomb.
Wet sands and dripping rocks in moonlight
glimmer ;
Crisp sea-froth flutters in the hurrying breeze ;
Far out from shore peaked sails like satin shimmer,
And one drops down to those enchanted seas
Below the ocean line—strange seas which lave
Mysterious shores with amber wave on wave.
All night the ocean pulses rising, falling
Will whiten the curved beach and fill with
sound
Moon-mellowed space and all the caverns calling
Like giants chained in darkness underground.
The moon will set ere dawn ; then twilight gray
Will break the golden night-dream of the bay.
N.S.W.
RICHARD DOWNEY.
POSING.
There’s a rage among the cultured to know how
the people feel,
If poverty has maddened,
If toil perpetual sours ;
The famine dire of Demos gives the journalist a
meal ;
The artist’s home is gladdened
By the sordidness of ours,
When we swarm beyond our hovels in a fierce and
ghastly tribe,
We are “posing ” for the painter and the poet and
the scribe.
And the parson learns the patter; it was heard, a
quaint conceit,
The world was made past healing,
Lost in wickedness and blind,
That the glory of the Christ might shine in con-
trast more complete.
This touched the artist-feeling,
This pleased the poet mind,
Foils forever ’gainst a brightness that can never be
our own,
Are we “copy ” for the godhead ? Must we
“{K>se ” before the Throne?
M.L.
C. CLYDE.
A LIFE.
Long time he drifted ’mid the surging flow
Of intermingling men ; they bustled by,
Sparing no thought to him who lingered nigh,
And knew him not—nor did they wish to know.
So the wan, creeping days stole to and fro
Like grey ghosts summoned earthward by a
sigh, \
And, whilst he craved men’s love and sympathy,
He lived his life apart, in silent woe.
He grew into a wrinkled man who zoned
Himself with fancied griefs, and loathed his
strange
Empeopled solitude ; who oft bemoaned
Men’s apathy, and welcomed an exchange
Of Life for Death, wherein no ghosts were rife
Like those which haunted all his Death in Life.
Sydney, ’95.
THOROLD DALRYMPLE.
TAREEL DOOL.
In our great Desert’s heart of hearts there lies
A place of graves, grown round with grey-brown
reeds
That shiver without stirring, like stout weeds
Touched by sad zephyrs at the morning rise.
A sullen sea of salt moans out his sighs
Into the heavy air—sounding his needs
To the red blood-filled sun—who drips and bleeds
Flame-bearing tear-drops from his sightless eyes.
Once, in a kindly winter’s deepest cool,
I journeyed thither. Trembling by the edge
Of that grim shore, I watched the monsters feast,
And gazed on skeletons of man and beast
Gathered by hundreds in the briny sedge
That guards the confines of the Tareel Dool.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 25, 1896.
The Federal Capital.
Nbw that a certain amount of progress has
been made with the rudimentary work of
Federation, the struggle for the possession
of the Federal capital may be expected to
break out again at any moment. In fact, it
is a cause of surprise that it has not broken
out already. Each province of the Federa-
tipn has hopes, more or less vague, that its
capital shall be made the seat of the Federal
Government; the smaller cities only desire
it as one who yearns after the impossible,
but Sydney and Melbourne are prepared to
claw after the distinction to the bitter end.
The struggle is not merely for the honor of
possession, for the ownership of the Federal
City means a great deal in the way of solid
cash advantage. The Legislature of the
Commonwealth will be situated there ; so
vvill the principal Government offices, the
head-quarters of the railway department
and the military forces ; and innumerable
casual hangers-on of the State. The prin-
cipal railway workshops will probably gravi-
tate there in course of time ; also the State
arsenal, arms and ammunition factories, the
State Bank and the State Insurance Depart-
ment when these come into existence. When
this country begins to recover from its de-
pendence on the British money-lender, and
to conduct its own financial business and
rely on its own resources, the Federal
city will become one of the principal
financial centres. If either Melbourne or
Sydney is selected as the seat of Govern-
ment, the head offices of private banks and
companies will gravitate towards the Federal
City, and its rival will become, in a financial
sense, a place of secondary importance. To
become the capital of Australia would pro-
bably mean an increase of 50,000 or 100,000
population in a very few years ; a great in-
crease in rents and land-values ; a glorious
boom ; and a permanent accession of wealth
and importance. Therefore the possession
of the capital is something worth struggling
for, and, as the scramble must begin shortly,
The Bulletin takes this opportunity to
suggest a possible site for the seat of Govern-
ment and to advance its reasons for selecting
the same.
This paper’s theory is that the Federal
capital should be built—not taken over
ready-made. As one possible location it
suggests the shores of the Darling River in
the west of New South Wales, somewhere in
the gruesome neighborhood of Menindie ;
and as another it desires to draw attention
to the sultry regions of far Western Queens-
land among the head waters of the Gregory
River. The central parts of Australia may
apparently be set aside as a hopeless locality
by reason of the deficient water-supply, but
possibly as good a site as any could be
found in the Northern Territory at the
back of the desert, somewhere about the
beginnings of the Victoria River. Also,
there is a promising situation on the
Murray just where the three provinces of
New South Wales, Victoria and South Aus-
tralia meet- -a position which would gratify
three provinces and could not aggravate
more than four. The Bulletin makes
these suggestions in a perfectly serious
spirit, and for many good reasons. Austra-
lia is a vast country, thinly peopled and
weakly defended, and, despite its good for-
tune in the past, it must reckon, like every
other country, upon possibilities of invasion
in the future. One of the most important
duties of the Federal Government will be to
provide for national defence, and with this
end in view it should not be planted either
at Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane, Adelaide,
Perth, or any other seaport, where the first
successful inroad by a foreign enemy would
scatter the Government into the bush, and
make the whole machinery of the State run
for the foothills. Such a disaster would
probably involve the abandonment of the
State papers and records, the dispersal of
the Legislature to the four winds, the cap-
ture of the principal military stores, and the
dislocation of everything, just at the time
when the State machine most wanted to be
in good working order. Moreover, it will
be absolutely necessary for the Federal
Government sooner or later to set up its own
arms and ammunition factories and its own
arsenals ; no country can safely depend on
importations in these matters, for the time
when munitions will be most wanted will be
when its ports are blockaded by an
enemy and supplies are unobtainable, and
when a general European war has created
such a demand that foreign makers cannot
keep pace with it. In other words, the only
time when Australia can confidently depend
on a foreign supply of munitions is when
they are not wanted, and the only time when
the supply is certain to stop short
is when it is wanted most of all. The natural
site for the Federal arsenals and ammunition
factories is at or near the capital, so that they
will be under the immediate control of the
authorities, and the natural site for the capi-
tal is as remote as possible from the coast,
so that some enemy arriving on a raid won’t
capture these essentials at the very beginning
of the difficulty, and leave the country prac-
tically defenceless.
Wherever the capital is located, there will
necessarily be a great increase of population.
A large array of State departments will be
concentrated there ; also the Legislature ; a
part, at least, of the military pomp and cir-
cumstance, and many other things ; and a
great number of shopkeepers and miscel-
laneous tradesmen will rise up to live on this
access of population ; and other people will
rise up in due course to live on them in their
turn. In Melbourne, Brisbane, Sydney,
Adelaide, or any other existing town or city,
this would simply mean a great increase
in rents and land-values for the benefit
of the private monopolist, and it would
also mean the purchase at inflated
values from the private monopolist of land
whereon to build new offices to accommodate
the concentrated machinery of Government.
But if the Federal Government creates a new
capital in a wholly new locality it can begin
by acquiring a few hundred square miles of
country from the provincial authorities at
prairie value, if, indeed, the province is not
prepared to cede the required space gratis in
consideration of the benefits it would
indirectly derive from the establishment of a
large population on its empty borders.
Within this territory the Federal Govern-
ment could be the sole ground landlord for
ever. As population increased throughout
Australia the Federal city would grow in
size and importance, and the ownership of
the soil would become a magnificent source
of revenue with unlimited possibilities of
expansion. The building of the capital is
the best opportunity Australia ever had, or
probably ever will have, to try an experi-
ment at Land Nationalisation on a large
scale, and work that experiment for all it is
worth ; and if it fails to seize upon the
opportunity through apathy, or a slavish
respect for precedent, or an inability to
comprehend the greatness of the oppor-
tunity, it will have cause to regret its neglect
for ever afterwards. /
* * * *
There are also many other reasons why
the new seat of Government should be
built at the further edge of nowhere on
the road to the setting sun—or something
equally vague. Its establishment, wherever it
may be, will, as already mentioned, create a
large population, and the dim interior wants
population badly. The coastal cities, on the
other hand, have already far more than their
share. The capital of United Australia will
naturally become an important railway
centre, and if it is planted on the furthest
end of the existing railway system, or beyond
the present furthest end, it will create new
activity where it is much needed. At present
no railway has penetrated the interim of A us
tralia because there was no place for 1 °S°
to save a blank spot on the map, no popula-
tion to serve, and no goods or passengers to
carry. Moreover, the only great market
is in the coastal cities, and even if the rail-
way had readied the far interior, and had
found something to carry from there, the
immense distances and heavy freights would
make agriculture hopelessly unprohta >e.
If a city arose in this wild region, agricul-
tural and pastoral industries would grow up
around it for the supply of its inhabitants,
and irrigation would make it an oasis in the
wilderness. It would be a case of bringing
a large population alongside the far-out pio-
ducer instead of placing it on the coast 10U
miles away from him, and thereby it would
give the interior lands the start for want ot
which they have always been languishing.
Also, it would bring the eye of the
Government-Jo bear directly on a region
which badly heeds its fostering care and
supervision. A Government located on the
coast knows practically nothing about the
necessities for irrigation and artesian well-
sinking, for locking rivers and conserving
water, which are the great troubles of three-
fourths of this continent. It can only learn
about these things in any satisfactory fashion
by personal observation, and as the pro-
blems can’t be brought to it for personal in-
spection the next best thing is to plant it
alongside the problems, wdiere they can t
possibly escape its attention.
* * * *
The establishment of a big centre in the
interior would help to create a scientific
railway system throughout Australia. All
attempts at constructing a transcontinental
line to open up the interior have failed,
greatly for want of something to rest upon
towards the centre. The population of the
interior has increased at a miserably slow rate
compared with that of the cities on the coast,
and the transcontinental lines had no place
to make for save the other side of the con-
tinent. All the great lines that strike
directly inland start from some town or city
beside the sea, and die away for want of
nutriment somewhere far inland, like an
over-long fishing-rod which gives way at the
thin end through being too far away from its
only base of support. The politicians who
discourse so much about the opening up
of the interior should consider this view
of the case ; the only way to open-up
the interior is to accumulate population
there, and one chance to create a big centre
of population here presents itself. The same
argument applies to the other stock saying
about opening up the mineral resources of
the country. As an example, the Northern
Territory, one of the richest and best-
watered regions of Australia, is rich in
mineral wealth, which, so far, has come to
little or nothing, mainly because it is 1000
miles from any place in particular. If it
became only 500 miles or less from some
place in particular, that empty land would
probably begin a new lease of life and start
on an altogether novel burst of prosperity.
There would be a new centre from which all
manner of industrial prosperity could start;
it would be a case of carrying civilisation
further out than it has yet been, and even
far outside the Federal district there would
necessarily be a considei’able increase of
population, which would be a source of
revenue to the adjacent provinces.
/ * * * *
All this, of course, would have to be gained
at the expense of much temporary discomfort
to the Federal Government. If the site of
the new capital was beyond the present
limits of railway construction a line would
have to be extended in the first place to
enable the Government to reach the scene of
its labors, and when it got there it would
have to live among the primitive surround-
ings of its partially unbuilt city until the
great experiment took shape through the
expenditure of much toil and money and
perspiration. But, after all, it is only the
same experiment which the United States
Government successfully carried out when it
built its capital city of Washington, and it
undertook that work with a smaller popula-
tion than Australia now possesses, with
little money, no credit worth speaking of,
and at a time when railways were still
unheard of and communication was slow and
difficult. And, whatever may be thought of
the other alleged advantages of the proposed
location, it offers sundry benefits which are
beyond dispute. It places the machinery of
Government, the Treasury, and the State
arsenals where they can’t be captured or
scattered to the winds at the first landing of
a possible invader. It solves a problem
which is bound to arise out of the rivalries of
the various provinces each desirous that its
own capital shall be the seat of the Federal
Government. It affords a fine opportunity
for an experiment in Land Nationalisation
on a large scale. And, incidentally, it will
afford much employment—of a profitable
character, in The Bulletin’s opinion—and
it will tend to diminish the enormous con-
gestion of population in the already over-
large coastal cities. All of which are matters
which call for much hard consideration. y
PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Ignorant Rag.
Few papers fake ’ and misrepresent statistics
with quite such a glaring disregard of truth or
probability as the Melbourne Foreign trade
and Single-tax organ, the Beacon. In i ts
issue of January 1, that paper strikes a new
theory about the horrors of Protection. Th<
people of New South Wales, it alleges, ar<
better fed than those of Victoria ; each Corn*
stalk consumes annually bushels of wheat,
1270 z. tea, 1121bs. sugar, llloz. currants and
raisins, over twenty gallons spirits, and 460 z.
tobacco ; while the Victorian gets only 5}
bushels wheat, llOoz. tea, 9‘2£lbs. sugar, 98oz.
currants and raisins, 18& gallons spirits, and
35-1, oz. tobacco. Then the lying organ
whoops :
It follows, therefore, that New South Wales, because it
is more prosperous than Victoria, can import more goods
and yet manufacture more goods locally than \ ictoria
can. ' The same increase in consumption, only very much
larger was witnessed in England after the abolition of
the protective system. Protection, by increasing prices,
and at the same time reducing wages, reduces consump-
tion wherever it is adopted.
The Beacon’s figures are partially correct so
far as they go—most of the wilful misrepresen-
tation comes in when they stop’going. To finish
the story (as told on page 300 of Coghlan’s
“ Wealth and Progress of N.S.W.”) the Vic-
torian consumes annually 971bs. more potatoes,
rather more coffee, and when wine, beer and
spirits are reckoned—not spirits alone (page
306) —one-tenth of a gallon more alcohol than
the cornstalk. Also, when all the different
varieties of eatables are added up (except
salt) the Victorian gets 10031bs. -of solid
food per annum against the N.S. Welsh-
man’s 9861b5. Also, by the same table the
cornstalk is worse fed than the Queenslander,
Tasmanian or Maorilander. Further, the wheat
statistics are a gross exaggeration, and the sugar
item is pure invention, the balance there being in
favor of the Victorian. Therefore, the Beacon
stands convicted of being a stupid and clumsy
per verier of the truth, and its statistics are of
the same value as Foreign-trade statistics in
general. The Beacon, by the way, is generally
supposed to be in some way connected with
Max Hirsch, the author of the “ Fiscal
Superstition,” a work which, as The Bulletin
had the pleasure of demonstrating when it
appeared, had hardly one reliable fact or figure
in its whole composition. And a hand which
looks very like that of Max sticks out in the
Beacon’s statistics, where each cornstalk is set
down as consuming 20 gallons of spirits alone
(per year, presumably, the other items being
for the year) whereas he consumes less than one
gallon, and the Victorian is credited with
drinking 18 gallons instead of less than three-
quarters of a gallon. If the Beacon’s figures
were correct these provinces would be one long,
wild, awful delirium tremens ; and as they are
they stamp that paper as the most pitiably
illiterate rag in Australasia.
“Federation Critics.”
Mr. Arthur Rae replies to a recent Bulletin
criticism as follows :
Dear Bulletin, —In the “Plain English” columns ot
your issue of the 11th my letter published in the Daily
Telegraph of the 21st December is subjected to a severe
keel-hauling. Your article, as a whole, is most unfair.
Firstly, although destruction may be more easy and
simple and look more beautiful than construction, as you
allege, and while the destructive critic may look more
powerful and eloquent, and so forth, than the'other fellow,
yet that has not been the case so far as Australian Federa-
tion is concerned. On the contrary, that great and im-
portant question has been glorified and gushed and slob-
bered over by every greasy groveller and tuft-liunting toady
in Australia, since Federation was first seriously mooted.
It isn’t the “ easy ” and “ simple ” work of the destructive
critic which has delayed Federal Union in Australia, but
the hypocritical snuffle, the effusive “loyalty” and pas-
sionate yearning to get further “ under the Crown,” on
the part of the average Federationist, as well as the
general National Ass principles on which they proposed
to construct the Federal Constitution which has hitherto,
disgusted many Australians and sent the rest to sleep.
Secondly, I must inform you that I don’t object to Federa-
tion “ because there is no provision for putting a Foreign-
trade minority on top.” A Freetrader might logically
object to secure the certain defeat of his own fiscal prin-
ciple by handing it over to a majority who held different
views. On that point, however, any costly and complete
system of Federation would, to be successful, fall far short
of either the Protectionist or Freetrade ideals, as a big
revenue tariff, which is neither one thing nor the other,
is inevitable to meet the Federal expenditure, plus direct
taxation for State purposes and more direct taxation for
the smaller form of Local Government. Where will the
saving come in? If it gives us cheaper loans, then
the curse of living on borrowed money is only
strengthened. If, as The Bulletin urges, it is
cheaper to have one Australian Governor than seven,
and easier to abolish the one than the seven, then
where does the beauty of a Federal-system come in which
carefully maintains the seven provincial vice-royalties,
with all their attendant “society” crawlsomeness, and
adds another, bigger, more costly and .more crawlsome to
the existing seven ? You say, “There is no remedy sug-
gested for this obvious difficulty,” except waiting for the
backward provinces to catch up. So far as the Federal
franchise is concerned, it seems to me the one tHin<>- for
which we should wait, as, on the character of the fran-
chise depends the possibility of future progress. If, how-
ever, the Convention provides for, and the several pro-
vinces accept, a uniform single-vote system for the
election of the Federal Parliament, then, personally I
don’t care a fig what franchise the local State elections
are held under, as that will be their own individual
funeral. Re the name of “ Senate ” or “ State Council,”
that may be a small matter,-but the mode of its appoint-
ment is of great importance. You say that I suggest no
remedy for the difficulties in the way of securin'*-
an ideal system of Federation. Well, what is the
matter with the idea of liberalising and extending
the scope of the present Federal Council, now dormant
and always nearly useless throligh the mother province
declining to join in V That council might have made Aus-
tralian Federation an almost perfect evolutionary growth
from the primitive to the complex. If that course is now
impossible, surely a time-limit for the proposed constitu-
tion would provide a simple and effective means for
reconsidering the position, and, if necessary recasting
the terms of union. Anyway, while admitting that Growth
and improvement will always be necessary, I should not
be accused of enmity to the principle in endeavoring to
render that development possible and easy by having the
foundations laid both broad and deep. h

could it be any easier to develope the
pre-tent Federal Council into a Parliament than
to make a new Federal Parliament? The first

Moving Them On.
An English religious paper called the British
Weekly has started out to incite the miser-
able English sewing-girl to embark for the
Promised Land of New South Wales. The
following is a fine slab :
I had an interesting conversation some days ago with
Miss G. A. Edwards, of Sydney, who is well-known in
New South Wales as the founder of the Sydney Working
and Factory Girls’ Club. Miss Edwards probably knows
more than any other woman of the conditions of the Aus-
tralian labor market for women. She mentioned that
many of the factory girls marry at l(i. One of them re-
marked to her : “ We’re all on the shelf at IS.” There
are in Sydney over 4000 girls employed in factories, and
as the colony cannot yet provide a sufficient number of its
own workers, there is a considerable field for immigra-
tion. “ Since I came home,” said Miss Edwards, “ I have
seen much of the factories and business houses of Loud >n,
and when I find girls earning Is. Bd. for making an
elaborate blouse which will sell in the West End for three
or four guineas, or earning 4Jd. for covering a picture hat
which will ultimately be sold at 305., I cannot help asking,
Why should they not emigrate ? From the first our
club has had the warm support of the Governors of
the colony and their wives. Lady Carinotox was
our first friend; Lady Jersey and Lady Duff have
also shown the warmest interest in the girls. Many
factory girls in Sydney spend their evenings in dancing
saloons, and in walking about the streets with doubtful
companions. At the club they have a comfortable home
provided for them at the cost of from 7s. to 10s. weekly.
Wages in Sydney are much higher than in England. A
good tailoress can earn from two to three pounds weekly.
Less efficient workwomen earn in the great tweed factories
from 15s. to 355., while the very lowest sum paid to
beginners is 10s. a week. The factory girl’s day begins at
6.3 J. They go to work, unlike our London ’Arriets, with-
out their hats. Not that the Sydney girl despises feathers,
but she keeps them for more festive occasions. Half an
hour is allowed for breakfast, and an hour for dinner,
and the working day ends at half-past 5. The long even-
ing, so much brighter in the Australian climate than ours,
is thus at the girl’s disposal, and it is one of the most
serious problem of Sydney life how to make the best use
of these hours of leisure.”
This is a fine sample of the mendacity of the
British religious paper. There are not
over 4000 girls employed in factories in
Sydney ; including Sydney and the adjacent
districts, and reckoning girls, women, old
women, very old women, and toothless bel-
dames in general, the whole number of
female factory workers, according to last
census, is only 2900. The alleged 4000
factory girls don’t habitually marry at 16;
out of the half-million females in N.S.
Wales, the whole number of married women
under 17 at the latest count was 127. Also,
the Sydney tailoress doesn’t earn, as a rule,
more than one-half of the alleged sum ; and the
beginner gets nothing, or 2s. 64. or ss. per week.
If she gets much over ss. it generally includes
being hugged by her male employer. There are
no “ great tweed factories ” in Sydney. And the
picture of the girl who gets up at 5 a.m. so as
to be at work at 6.30, and gets off at 5.30 in the
evening, not knowing what to do with her
superabundant leisure, is a wild vision. What
does anybody who works 11 hours a day do with
his or her leisure ? If Miss Edwards is a real
individual, and if she really told these awful
taradiddles to the British Weekly to induce
the British workwoman to come out to this
land of hard toil, low wages, scant work, sweat-
ing, and depression, then she has a lot to answer
for. The Briton has one standing remedy for
all kinds of suffering—to move the sufferer on
to somebody else’s premises, and drop him
gently on somebody else’s doorstep, and let him
starve somewhere where somebody else will be
anuoyed by his emaciated remains and have to
pay for the inquest. Meanwhile, the wild
assertions attributed to the alleged Miss
Edwards, of Sydney, are interesting reading.
The Protected Foreigner.
An embarrassing aspect of the New South Wales
Income-tax turned up last week and threatened
to make trouble between the GoD-fearing
Ministry and the importing interest. Accord-
ing to the Act, when any foreign merchant or
producer sends goods to N.S. Wales for dis-
posal by his agent, it is to be assumed that 5 per
cent, of the proceeds represents profit. Possibly
the real profits may sometimes be 25 per cent,
instead of five, and the figure assumed by the
Act is therefore about as moderate as anybody
could desire. This 5 per cent., being income
earned in New South Wales, is to be subject to
Income-tax at the usual rate of 6d. in the £, so
that foreign goods sent to Sydney for sale will
be subject to a duty, through the Income-tax
Department, of half-a-crown for each £IOO
value. An agitated deputation of importers
pointed out to Premier Reid that this impost
was “calculated to restrict trade,” and
Reid agreed that it was so and promised that
the law in this particular respect would not
be enforced. One inference to be drawn
from this is that the law is a thing to be en-
forced or not just as the Premier, or his boss,
the great aggregate importer, pleases. But the
most interesting point is the tender care exer-
cised over the smallest interests of the foreign
dealer. If a Sydney merchant imports goods
and sells them, or if a New South Wales
manufacturer makes goods and sells them, or if
a farmer or pastoralisb drags produce out of the
ground and sells or exports it, the profits made
thereby are subject to income-tax, in the usual
way. But if a foreigner sends goods to the
province for disposal through his agent, his
profits are exempt from even this small impost,
and if he lives and spends his money in a
country where there is no income-tax, he, there-
fore, gets so far ahead of the local man.
Just now there are 25,000 tons, or there-
abouts, of wheat en route from California to
Sydney, and the profits made by the sale of it
will, according to the new reading of the Act,
pay no tax whatever to the N.S. W. Treasury !!
But the profits made by the cornstalk farmer
out of his grain will pay the usual per cent,
income-tax, and the land on which he
grows his produce will pay Id. in the £
per annum on its capital value, and if he
is located far back, with many miles of
railway carriage between him and the capital,
it will cost him a heavier freight to land his
produce in Sydney than it costs to carry the
American farmer’s grain from San Francisco to
the same city !! ! Under all these conditions,
Premier Reid’s anxiety to exempt the foreign
consignor from the humble impost of 2£ per
cent, on the profits of his New South Wales
trade is a touching sentiment. The amount of
the exemption may be small, but, at all events,
it is all the exemption that the circumstances
admitted of. And the arrangement which pro-
tects the foreigner who lives and spends his
money abroad, and merely consigns goods to
New South Wales, against the local importer
who, at all events, spends his profits in the
country, is a case of carrying the Foreign-trade
principle to the very last extremity. Even the
local Foreign-trader is discriminated against
nowadays because he isn’t foreign enough.
No-Liability Companies.
The Bulletin lately called attention to the
damage the No-Liability Act does to legitimate
mining. What is wanted, it was contended, is
a provision declaring that a certain percentage
—say 20, 30, or 40—of the company’s capital
shall be actually available for prospecting and
mining. At the opening of the year several
lists which had been prepared were published
with the purpose of indicating “ Lhe marvellous
growth of Westralian trade.” From one of
these lists a table of deadly significance has
been compiled, as follows :
It will be interesting to note the direction in
which some of these companies spend their
£2OO or £5OO in a country where water is from
6d. to 9d. a gallon, and where the bicycle
expressman’s fee for posting the engineer’s
report runs up to half-a-crown. Indeed, it is
interesting to speculate how much of the
£2OO or £5OO is left after brokers’ commission
and “printing and advertising prospectus,
printing scrip,” &c., are paid. Here are a
couple of companies, each with a capital of
£120,000, the shareholders in which have
actually paid up £375. It was so little
that it was not worth putting to the
credit of the co., and so the vendors took
the lot. Wherefore these £120,000 mining
co’s set out to develop their (of course) excel-
lent prospects upon—nothing. True, in these
two cases all the shares have to contribute (or
forfeit); but in some others the vendors’ shares
are fully paid up, so that the few contributing
shareholders have to carry the whole of the
burdens. For a certainty most of them neither
can nor will pay calls ; they only buy the shares
on the strength of a possible rise, and if the
shares don’t rise they simply forfeit. And they
call this mining ! Presently (speaking of the
system, not of the individual mine) when the
grocer calls round, the shareholder who has not
been able to sell curses “mining” as loudly as
his broken voice will allow. Admittedly mining
is a risky business, but that surely is no reason
why Legislatures should give men facilities for
making it the pretext for a mighty gamble. In
fact, if mining is risky, but if nevertheless it is
a thing to be encouraged, the sensible course, it
would seem, would be to reduce the risk as
much as possible by weeding out gambling
concerns which are obviously only masquerading
as mining companies.
That Irish Regiment.
Cardinal Moran, in achieving the nucleus of
an exclusively Irish military corps in New
South Wales, is true to an early prepossession.
While imperial, like his uncle, he is in practice
insularly patriotic. He believes in saints, but
in Irish saints in particular ; in orators, but in
Irish orators particularly ; in poets, but par-
ticularly in Irish poets. The denominational
schools of Ireland were very well managed for
over half a century by what were known as
Christian Brothers. The order was a French
one, but it was manned by Irishmen, and was
generally considered to be racy of the
soil. The Cardinal, however, at that time
a humble ecclesiastic, objected to have
Ireland instructed from a French source,
and as soon as he obtained episcopal power he
created an Irish order of the same brotherhood
and established their schools in his Irish diocese.
Thus, owing to him, there are two distinct
orders known to the world as Christian
Brothers. To what good ? One order in which
all nations might blend would to many seem
preferable, just as would one army in which all
patriots would coalesce. Differentiation is some-
times good, but historically it is a bad principle
when applied to nations. The isolation of
Ireland has been a fault, and if perpetuated
will be a curse. The setting-up of an ex-
clusively Irish regiment in New South Wales,
notwithstanding that similar regiments exist
in Queensland, Victoria, and elsewhere in
Australia, is in a small wav doing with Irish-
men what in a large way English rule has per-
sistently done. And the result has been that
the Irish soldier doesn’t know the English one
or the Scotch one, and they don’t know him,
and, when ordered, they blow his brains out as
being something worse than a wooden-shoed
foreigner. Nothing is clearer to the thinking
man than that the salvation of the world
depends upon the working-classes of the world
—whether the bearers of scythes or swords—
having an intimate knowledge of one another’s
sufferings. With this knowledge under his cap
Fritz won’t kill Jacques, Jacques won't kill
Paddy, however loudly crowned despots yell or
partisan prelates bless or damn. Rather, they
will stand together and, clubbing their rifles,
batter the brains out of despot and prelate.
Anything, therefore, which makes for isolation
of nationality in Australia should be laughed
down as a hoax, and, if found serious, be con-
demned as a crime.
Jingo.
Melbourne Punch : “ Were the Empire in
danger to-morrow not a colony of the many
that owe their birth to England but would
freely and gladly strip itself, if need be, of
men and money, to beat back the mother
country’s enemies.” Here the query naturally
arises—what men and whose money is the
Punch man willing to strip Victoria of in the
good cause of Jingoism? Does he propose to
borrow the money so that this generation will
get all the whoop and the glory, and the tem-
porary boom which would arise through the
influx of coin, while the long, dreary bill is left
to posterity ; or is he willing to see the income-
tax put up to ss. in the £ with no exemp-
tion, and a land-tax of, say, 3d. in the £
levied, also without exemptions, and perhaps a
poll-tax of 10s. per head per annum piled on
top of it all, so that the people who get the
medals and the knighthoods and the glory in
the papers will also pay for the proceedings ?
And, as regards the men, does he propose that
other people should go to the gory front while
he stays behind and is paid for writing articles
about their doings, or is he going himself? And,
in the latter ease, how' does he like the prospect
of giving up his billet in the city, and the
salary, and the theatres, and the iced-drinks,
and going out to carry a heavy rifle and knap-
sack on long marches in some arid land, and
camping out under the damp, rheumatic stars,
and coming back a cripple —if he comes back
at all —with his writing arm shot off, and find-
ing that someone else has got his billet, and
there is no demand for a half-man anywhere,
and getting a medal and possibly an allowance
of 2s. 6d. a day from his grateful country ? In
short, is he going to sacrifice himself and his
money for the glorious empire, or is he only
willing to float a loan and sacrifice someone
else ? This question is the great Jingo problem
in a nutshell.
The conflict of opinion between the Sydney
police and stipendiary-magistrate Whipping-
dale Johnson over cabman Darwin’s method
of disciplining his refractory child merely
touches the fringe of what is a really great
question. The tendency of the age is unques-
tionably in the direction of saving children
from corporal punishment. Several countries
have specifically abolished the use of the cane
in schbols; under the Board in England
assistant-teachers are not permitted to
cane, and head - masters and mistresses
who inflict such punishment must make
an official entry of the fact, and record
the principal circumstances of the case.
An almost identical rule prevails in the State
schools of several of the Australias, while all
over the world the teacher who can control his
pupils without an appeal to corporal punish-
ment is set up as an example for his class to
follow. With this tendency so clearly marked
where trained officers of the State are con-
cerned, it is matter for surprise to find an ex-
perienced magistrate declaring in open court
that the police were morbidly sympathetic in
prosecuting a father for chaining by the legs
and welting with a belt-buckle his nine-year-
old child. School-masters, like policemen,
are expected to possess control over temper ; if
they do not possess it to a high degree they
stand small chance of promotion to the sole
charge of children. On the other hand, any
man, practically speaking, is permitted by law
to become a father. Thus we have the genius
of the time taking the power of inflicting cor-
poral punishment, even the mildest hand-caning,
away from trained officials, while law-adminis-
trators of the Wiiipfingdale Johnson type
endow the man who achieves a child, though lie
has given no proof of control of temper or dis-
ciplinary training, with the right to corporally
punish it to an extent which, as in the Darwin
case, the police and medical evidence testified
to be excessive.
The first witness called by Vic.’s Fat Man
Council was a bootmaker, who declared, in ’94,
that if other makers paid less than statement
prices he would at once tackle his wages sheet
and see he got his work done at the same
prices. He now declares that a law-fixed wage
must be unfair, and that to display wages rates
in legible characters on the walls of every
factory (as prescribed in the Assembly Bill)
would be equal to disclosing his whole business.
Which is a serious hint that the whole business
of a manufacturer is to “ tackle his wages
sheet.” That bootmaker is a protected manufac-
turer, encouraged by the State on the innocent
supposition that he and his pals would do the
decent thing by his employes. Now that the
law that called him into existence as a manu-
facturer proposes to regulate him as a manu-
facturer, he squeals. Neither sandshifting,
alias Freetrade, nor Protection, is much good
without the Minimum-Wage.
What are the Commercial Principles that are
belauded down South as the secret for big State
undertakings ? Commercial principles are sup-
posed to oust patronage and jobbery in appoint-
ments and promotions. Can anyone name a
bank, or a warehouse, or a shop in Australia
run with or without commercial principles
where patronage and influence are not essential
to appointments and promotions ? One Mel-
bourne bank keeps a billet or two for good
cricketers, because the manager is a crank about
the game. Put that manager on the Railway
Trust and he will instinctively, and with the
most honest intentions in the world, proceed to
keep an eye open on behalf of some rising bat
in a junior team who would play for the M.C.C.
if —•. Take any man in Australia and give him
the bestowal of billets, and his friends will find
his weak spot —it may be his church, or his club,
or his position in society—but weak spot he
has, and somebody will touch it. This is true
of private as well as publio fat billets—only it
is probably more so with the private dispenser
of patronage. There is hardly a grocer or
draper in Australia who has not handicapped
his business by making a manager of his incom-
petent son or his weak-kneed nephew-
If Australia takes in the smiling Japanese as
per schedule of proposed treaty, numbers of
frayed goody-goodies will be able to grab hand-
fuls of ripe evangelical employment. Without
naming the nastinesses goody-goodies like fin-
gering, there is a wealth of open sin about the
brown man quite exhilarating to an average
evangelical maid. For instance, Koko knows
nothing about a Sabbath. Never once did his
nation hear of it—except as a barbarian obser-
vance. His mills work on Sunday, his shops
stay open, his workmen labor. From 12 to 17
hours his nation toils daily, and the idea of
idling all in a body on one particular day of the
week strikes him as an absurdity as great as if
a nation went drunk on one day in seven.
This is a clean prejudice, but it will take Aus-
tralian tabbies years to uproot it.
The Boer is again triumphant. The battle of
Kruger’s Dorp was won by straight shooting ;
“dour fanaticism” and “deep religious feel-
ing ” are all frill. Likewise, any resemblance
which may exist between Oom Paul’s braves
and Cromwell’s Ironsides has little bearing
upon the matter. To Australians this Boer
business conveys two lessons. First, that the
most necessary men in bush fighting are men
who can shoot straight; and, secondly, that
Australia hasn’t got them, or, at least, not
enough of them. The defence forces are large
enough for a church-parade or a midnight-
tattoo, but not to protect our enormous coast-
line, even if they were equal in skill to the
Ivanoffs or Fritzes, or even t,he little
monkey-men of Japan. The young Australian
rides, pulls, and pedals in pursuit of fame and
trophies, and, given sufficient inducement, he
will take to rifle-shooting as a duck takes to
water. Let the various Govts, foster rifle
clubs and offer prizes for good markmanship,
and in a short space of time we shall have a
nation of sharpshooters who could effectually
oppose the invading forces of any power likely
to land on our shores. The art of war for
Young Australia is chiefly contained in three
words, “ Learn to shoot.”
The Yic. law cast a humorous light upon itself
the other day in a matter concerning a humble
sneak-thief and a fiddle. The fiddle was an old,
commonplace article, and it was pawned for
ss. When the thief was convicted, the pawn-
broker pleaded that he shouldn’t lose the ss.
advanced, as he had acted in good faith, but a
Melbourne bench of magistrates ordered him to
hand it over, and told him sternly that he
should have known better, as the prisoner
“ didn’t look the sort of man who would be in
honest possession of a fiddle.” Then, in its
usual illogical fashion, it stopped short,
in order to conceal its ignorance. If the
bench knew how a man looked when he
looked like a man who wasn’t likely to own
a fiddle, it must also have known how he looked
w r hen he looked as if he was likely to own
such an article ; it stands to reason that it
couldn’t know that he didn’t look like a man
with a fiddle unless it knew how a man with a
fiddle could be expected to look. And when it
knew how a man looked when he owned a
fiddle it should have given a description of him
so that future thieves might be detected. If
the miserable pawnbroker had been equal to
the occasion he would promptly have asked
how a man ought to look when he owned a
fiddle, and then the bench would probably have
discovered all of a sudden that it was only an
ass trying to look intelligent, and have covered
its confusion by having him run in for con-
tempt.
Ed. Bulletin, —Industrialism has slain feudal-
ism, but the dying giant left a Nessus’-shirt,
which, if not soon destroyed, will prove the con-
queror’s bane. It is steeped in the bad blood of the
old monster. We call it the principle of inherit-
The man who accumulates wealth is,
generally (at least, if we believe the polito-
nomists), whether he will or no, a great bene-
factor of man. The man who inherits it is
usually dangerous to man. Why should the
principle of “ consideration ” not be applied to
the transfer of property by will and in
intestacy, as to other transfers ? Kill inherit-
ance and you strip capitalism of most of its
evils, you effectually prevent the formation of
dangerously large landed estates, you remove
enormous premiums on incapacity, you burn
the roots of undesirable caste-mangroves, you
make the marriage-problem soluble, and you
make it possible that the survival of the fittest
shall mean the survival of the best. Inherit-
ance is the nucleus of the germ-plasm of caste.
And that Democracy may be possible, Carthago
delencla eat. —D.
A milking machine, tried at Cave Hill,
Vic., milked a set of 12 cows dry in 10 min.
A Pimpinio, Vic., farmer, other day,
found in the stomach of a dead cow over 60 pieces
of bone, from one to five inches long.
Dear Bulletin, — I am prepared to main-
tain that on W.A. goldfields no decent woman
can live without either losing, to a great extent,
her own self-respect or her sense of respect for the
opposite sex. She will not be interfered with—
true; but she will be simply ignored. The
filthiest expressions are commonly used, despite
the presence of one, two, or 20 women. Ordinary
bad language might be passed over; but it is
impossible to pass over one particular word known
as the “W.A. adjective.” Scores of “ t’other-
siders,” as well as “ natives,” who wouldn’t use
this expression in Little Bourke-street, use it
freely in their conversation in streets and public-
houses over here. Certainly, it is comparatively a
few men who offend, but there are quite enough of
them to make the goldfields —I speak for the Mur-
chison as well—unfit for decent women to live
and work in. Coolgardie itself is improving,
although the barmaids, for instance, are st ’ prac-
tically unprotected. In all other centres a few
low whites can wag filthy jaws as long as they
like.—D., Coolgardie.

Alexandra “ The Roll of the Drum.”
“The Work Girl,” at Sydney Royal,
belongs to the common or hunky variety of melo-
drama in five acts. It opens with a bell-topper
rolling in a cellar, the owner thereof having been
killed by the evening-dress villain and dropped
down a trap-door into the river, while his hat
bounds on the damp floor as a silent witness
of the tragedy. The rest is mainly a new and
damaged version of the “Silver King,” the hat in
the cellar being a temporary departure into
originality. The hero is brought to grief at the
same old Derby, by the same old schemer who
was mashed on his wife before he married her.
The variations are that he gets into gaol instead
of into Colorado, and comes back stone-broke to
warm himself at a baked-potato can on a bridge ;
and as his wife has died in the limelight he is
only looking for his daughter. But Scot Inglis
dresses the character as an exact copy of the good
old “King,” with the traditional long coat and
white hair, and the handkerchief round his neck,
and borrows a shilling off his lost daughter on
the bridge with a wild expression in his eye.
There is something that is intended to be
humorous, but which is only coarse, in a scene
where six girls tear Sweeney’s pants off; and
there is something that is probably intended for
dramatic intensity in another scene, where a
fashionable lady tries to horsewhip the pathetic
Harrie Ireland—the lost girl, who has been fished
out of the river and has hardly had time to dry.
* * • »
Except in the last act, which has a lot of
go in it, and some vigorous assault and battery
on a stair, “ The Work Girl ” is a drama of bitter
commonplace. There is very little humor in it,
for the bulgy Jew leprosy who cries out “mum-
bling Moses !” in moments of excitement is not
funny, though he is possibly meant to be.
Besides, he is a weak and futile Jew, who is con-
stantly swindled in the most infantile fashion by
everybody. The airiest person in the show is
Westmacott’s larrikin. Harry Plimmer, who has
improved greatly of late, does good work as a de-
cayed person with a secret locked up in his hairy
chest; Scot Inglis is a fine, pathetic prodigal;
and Hazlett is an artistic villain, though the bell-
topper in the cellar finally bowls him out. Also,
Misses Harrie Ireland and Florrie Forde are all
that this paper’s fancy painted them. The rest of
the. cast is an innumerable caravan of plain non-
entities. The drama is otherwise adorned with
the most utterly repulsive young female who ever
wore ap expensive silk dress on the stage, and
tried to,cane a distressed sister; also, by a fruit-
barrow and a high-kicking ballet; and there is a
scene in a sweater’s workroom —only, as nobody
seems to work, the moral gets mislaid. Still, Miss'
Josephine Thynne comes to apply foi a billet, and
when she doesn’t get it ? she smacks the bloated
proprietor severely—which is something.
* * * *
“ The Passport,” supposed to present some
vague resemblance to Savage’s ‘‘Official Wife,”
and which really does resemble it just as much
as a curate resembles an Armenian massacre,
Opened a promising career at Sydney Lyceum on
Saturday. As to the humor of it there is no
manner of doubt; the first night was a con-
tinuous howl of laughter, and the large-nosed
female in the d. 0., who explains the play to her
deaf aunt as it goes along, broke herself up in her
efforts to make things understandable to that
afflicted relative by 9.30. There are many things
in “ The Passport ” to be thankful for. One is
that the Russian police do not undress Brough on
the stage, after all, to see if he is disguised in
what they candidly mention as “a false stomach;”
they rush him several times with the obvious
intention of undressing him, but their attention is
fortunately diverted each time, and 1 ’.rough is finally
undressed in an adjacent room. And when Miss
Watt-Tanner, as a weeping mother-i i 1 -law, beseech es
Cecil Ward to make Miss Hilda Spong change
her stockings whenever she gets i er dainty feet
wet, Ward judiciously takes it for granted that
her feet are dry up to date. And when another
lady hands back to her jealous husband the jewels
he gave her, and makes a suggestion about also
handing back the clothes she received from him,
and which she has on at the moment, th jy tor-
tunately beoome reconciled in time to preveii t this
alarming sacrifice. “The Passport” is b ised
principally on a grave suspicion that Cecil Ward
has committed bigamy with Miss Spong; that
Mrs. Brough has done the same with Boucicault,
and that her first husband is not really “ slumber-
ing in the thingumbob ; ” and that Miss Hardy
had been a lady of bad character in Russia, and
Ward’s first wife —a lady of very loose morals—
isn’t really dead. And when all these matters are
cleared up without the Russian police undressing
Brough in public, the result is a big success all
round.
* * * *
Dot Boucicault, in a very small character,
is the most artistic spot in the drama. Boucicault
is undoubtedly the most versatile and most finished
actor in Australia, and even the smallest
character, in his hands, becomes a thing worth
remembering. Also, he is the only man who has
a different smile every time. Brough and Cecil
Ward are sufficiently successful in the two biggest
parts, and Mrs. Brough is exceedingly good as a
beautifully-dressed lady who rests under grave
suspicions. Dorrington, as a lengthy idiot, does
well enough, and all the other ladies are quite satis-
factory, Miss Spong only excepted. The fair
Hilda has not learnt the art of weeping success-
fully, and her habit of putting on her stage com-
plexion in a large blob under each eye is dis-
appointing ; altogether her performance falls con-
siderably below some of her previous efforts. But,
taking the 00, all round, there is little to complain
of, and the fact that Brough isn’t undressed by
Policeman Majeroni would cover almost anything
if need be.
* * -* *
A lot of new songs and new music have
been worked into the pantomime at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney), and it is rather better for the change.
A pantomine is a thing that developes as it goes
along, and when it gets into its fifth week it is so
utterly unlike what it was in its first week that
only the name holds the two productions together.
Miss Luella and Mr. “Bobby” Watson —it is
truly awful to belong to a profession where a
grown-up citizen must call himself “ Bobby ” for
humor—-have added a lot to their parts, and im-
proved things generally. Also, by way of further
variety, Walter Rivers manfully played Saturday
the other night with six stitches in his head, he
having fallen down somewhere, or over some
place, or something of the kind, and broken him-
self in three pieces just before the performance.
* * * *
Sydney Music Hall has been closed re-
cently, on account of the “ heat-wave ” or some-
thing, but promises to re-open to-night (Thursday).
J. Driscoll Foley, who left a good record behind
him as the crawlsome Irish attorney in “ Pat,” at
Sydney Royal, will appear as “ the celebrated
monologue entertainer, ” and if he monologues as
well as he crawls he should be a person of value.
* * * *
Two more of Rickards’ new artists, the
Cosmans, dropped in at Sydney Tivoli last Satur-
day, and a lot of the old ones, notably W in ton,
the ventriloquist, did some good work. There
seems to be no end to Rickards’ new importations;
the head of the procession arrived in Australia
long ago, while the tail-end isn’t yet at Suez, or
anywhere near it.
* * * *
Another co. proposes to try its luck at
Sydney Criterion next Saturday, when proceed-
ings will begin with the comedy “Hide and Seek.”
Wilfred Shine and “New Boy” Ralph Roberts
are two lights of the new venture, and the ghosts
of the last two enterprises which tempted fortune
at the Cri. will be round on Saturday night to
wish them all manner of luck.
* * *• *
Melb. Alexandra, under Dan Barry’s
management, has struggled back to life rather
sooner than was expected. On Saturday the alert
campaigner hoisted the British Flag for all that
sacred emblem is worth at Haleck prices, and the
result justified his daring. Previously Daniel had
excited the populace by advertising for 100 hideous
supers, to impersonate Boers. The 100, or more or
less, ugly men rolled up and were engaged to fall
in heaps of slain as often as the band played
“Rule Britannia,” which they did in a praise-
worthy manner. The scene of the Flag’s triumph
is laid in the Johannesburg district (according to
the programme), and it is vaguely understood
that Tommy Atkins wipes out the combined
armies of France and Russia, in addition to
decimating the Boers. Altogether, “ The Roll
of the Drum,” is a fine moral military drama.
The enemies of Great Britain seem to be a
thoroughly bad, deceitful lot—especially Augustus
Glover—whilst the Queen’s gallant troops are
persons to feel proud of. Miss Dora Mostyn, as a
lion-hearted drummer-boy, and Wilson Forbes, as
an unyielding colonel, do wonders for Hingland in
the hour of need ; but Glover and other evil-dis-
posed foreigners chiefly help to push along the
smoky story. “Roll of the Drum” contains a
good deal of plot, as well as battle and noise and
stage-management, and if Emperor William will
kindly resume his bellicose attitude of a fortnight
ago Dan Barry’s drama will run for the next six
months. Much depends on Emperor William and
the cable-liar.
* * * *
No change in “ Djin-Djin ” stock at Melb.
Princess’s. Several large parcels of seats have
been placed during the past few days, and small
buyers are rather more eager than ever. In fact,
the present output of eight performances per
week is barely sufficient to meet the demand,
therefore rumors of “Djin-Djm’s” early ship-
ment to Sydney may be ignored. The healthy
tone of the chorus is maintained, also the
beauteous ballets are quoted in high terms of
admiration, and although the earthquake always
falls heavily it recovers its former position every
time, and will be a Firm attraction until further
notice. The Bulletin’s blessing—which passeth
all understanding, and which neither moth nor
rust can corrupt—rests upon “Djin-Djin ” as
usual.
# # * *
The Royal pantomime was doomed to dis-
appear on Wednesday of this week, and the ver-
dict is death from starvation. A cheerful trans-
■formation scene, a merry little leading lady, a
satisfactory ballad songstress, and an acrobatic
dog, were about the only popular features of
George Coppin’s hungry show. The other features
represented a reduction of economical principles
down to the “ one straw a day ” policy which slew
the farmer’s steed, in the old fable. Last Satur-
day, manager Coppin told his audience that the
>anto. he assisted to produce at the Royal, 50
years ago, didn’t cost more than =£4o a week,
whereas, nowadays, one might easily achieve a
nightly loss of that amount. But the veteran’s
little story of olden times had nothing to do
with the case. He himself produced a costly
pantomime four or five years back, and made
money by it, although the same show wouldn’t
have paid expenses when he was half a century
younger. But no matter. Somebody from St.
ivilda won a gold watch with his coupon on Satur-
day. This jubilee souvenir presented by the
early father of Melbourne pantomimes, and in-
scribed accordingly, deserves to be laid carefully
aside until it becomes a famous theatrical relic.
* * * *
At the Opera House crowded houses are
now saluting Alice Leamar’s bicycle and cheering
their beloved Rickards with considerable fervor.
Melbourne’s drain-germs attacked the perenniai
Henry as soon as he returned from Hingland, yet
the fact that he feels pretty bad before and after
his performance makes no appreciable difference
in his form on the stage. J. F. Robin, from
Amurka, seems to be on good terms with
multitude, and the rest of the bill gets a favorab
reception.
*# * *
Leonore Orelli, the Firm’s latest recruit
from the amateur opera brigade, is unfavorab y
contrasted with Juliet Wray by such people as
would draw comparisons between Titheradge ana
Elton. The abnormally self-confident Juliet is
a dasher, well suited to French comedy par s -
She has scored in “ Angot, ’ “ Nitouche, Mas-
cotte” and “La Belle Therese.” Her style is
bold, her enunciation strong, distinct and harsh,
and her singing method is bad, so bad that it will
knock her thin voice to bits if not speedily im-
proved. And probably it is beyond hope of cure.
The Orelli is quite a different case. VY hen she
and Juliet played Josephine (“ Pinafore ) ( one
night apiece, at Melb. Princess’s she was the
quiet one,’’ whereas her rival, in bright blue satin,
was “ the cheeky one.” Orelli was distinctly the
more promising girl for sentimental heroine
characters. Her personal style, voice and vocal
ability will bring her to the front if her health
permits, as a Patience, Marjorie, Princess Ida,
Bohemian Giurl, or anything in the same ime or
business. Already she is a more graceful debu-
tante than the original Florence Young, likewise
a much more intelligent elocutionist. Her speak-
ing tones ought to grow loud enough in a little
while, and then the only objection to Miss O. will
be her curious enunciation. She doesn’t exactly
lisp, or slur her words, but her mannerism of
talking is odd and foreign to the ear. Yet it isn t
unpleasing. If she gets along well as a singer and
actress this peculiarity will find professional
imitators. At present it has the negative virtue
of uniqueness.

E. PAYNE, the N.S.W. Cyclist.
At Poverty Point .
P.: When is a pantomime to arise without a male old-
woman part? The sausages, geese, and hot pokers of old
pantomime hive practically disappeared, but the vulgar
man in white socks and petticoats stays on—a drivelling
anachronism. No one thinks the pantomime old woman
is funny—many people think their neighbors think it
funny. It is not the she-male’s hideous vulgarity that is
most awful—it is its criminal want of humor.
Christchurch is the city most dreaded by pros, visiting
Maoriland. Approval there means prosperity right
through. Disapproval spells damnation. Most com-
panies nave been damned in Christchurch.
Eth i Haydon, writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, makes but a poor Shop Girl. Her acting is
indifferent and her voice small, whilst she is lacking in
the spontaneous vivacity absolutely necessary in burlesque.
If she had only the “ go ” of a Cissy Loftus she would
with her undeniably good looks, be quite a star of the
first magnitude. The Gaiety is not, by any means, a good
school for a pretty girl, and it will take all mamma’s
vigilance to keep the johnnies (and Georges, too for that
matter) at arm’s length. It is to be hoped that the report
that Ethel has captured, and is about to annex in the
holy bonds, a South African millionaire is true.
London Sketch : “It is a pity that pretty Miss Ethel
Haydon, who sings charmingly, has not greater skill in
taking advantage of her beauty.”
The stately Geraldine Oliffe, late of the B and B Co
is now playing in London in “Mrs. Ponderbury’s Past”
Thus George Bernard Shaw, in the Saturday Review ■-
A Miss Oliffe, whom I do not remember to have seen
be ore, conies very near making an artistic success in the
title-part, only missing it by a few unhappy lapses into
clowning at the crucial passages, according to the trad -
tion of the English stage, where people are always so
carefully taught by the stage-manager to force the fun
and spoil it. If Miss Oliffe would only try the effect of
playing the part with absolute sincerity throughout, and
without slackening her grip, absolutely refuse to g"ve
away her handsome style at any moment for the saki of
raising a silly heehaw by a grimace or an ugly sound or
gesture, she would distinguish herself considerably ”

Fathoms Deep” fame, after a chequered career in Aus-
tralia, died of dropsy at Sydney Alfred Hospital, the

the principal hoys are Melbournites. The only exception

Three of the local milita-ry, high up, were admitted
behind the scenes of a big Sydney theatre the other night,
as a privilege, you know. The supers waited till the
heroes were interested in an actress or two —then they
chalked the martial hoofs. The dauntless three were
each enclosed in a chalk circle, bearing the inscription 2s.
Cd. Soon the supers spoke in stage-whispers to each
other, “A oaser! a caser ! my kingdom for a caser!”
The answer came, “ ’Sdeath, Humphy ! Yon treacherous
dog, borrowing my caser to bury his baby, and the
grizzling monster has put it down his hissing neck!”
After five minutes of this, the soldiers barked. Then the
actresses mollified them with the good old legend, “ I
feel hj nervous when you are watching me and every-
body was satisfied. “Never offend the army,” Farren
use dto say. “ They are generous when they have it, and
they are always harmless.”
The veteran actor Gladstone, a relic of theG. Y. Brooke
era, has taken up his residence in the Dramatic Home at
Northcote (Melb.). Mr. Gladstone was a subscriber to the
funds since the inception of the institution.
Kenneth Mackay, M.L.A. for Burrowa (N.S.W.), has, in
conjunction with Alfred Dampier, completed a drama en-
titled “ The Golden West,” which will shortly be produced
at one of the Sydney theatres.
Harry Payne, the Drury Lane clown, died with £6OOO to
his credit. One bequest in Harry’s will was funnier than
any of his old Xmas jokes. It provided the sum of £lOOO
in trust for a certain Mrs. Sam May, “ with power of
appointment thereof in favor of all, or any one or more, of
her children, or reputed children.” In theatrical circles,
seemingly, it is a wise child that knows its own mother,
apart from the everlasting doubt as to papa’s identity.
R. S. Smythe regards Holy Land Haskett Smith as by
far his ablest lecturer, though worst financial spec, last
year. Mark Twain produced most cash.
Lecturer Charles Clarke is next on R. S. Smythe’s list,
and he will arrive at Easter. His new discourse is on St.
Paul’s Cathedral.
Bernard Shaw disputes the prevalent theory that people
go to the theatre to be amused and pleased. Says he :
“ They want to be excited, and upset, and made miser-
able, to have their flesh set creeping, to gloat and quake
over scenes of misfortune, injustice, violence, and cruelty,
with the discomfiture and punishment of somebody to
make the ending ‘ happy.’ The only sort of horror they
dislike is the horror that they cannot fasten on some
individual whom they can hate, dread, and finally torture
after revelling in his crimes.”

to Harry Rickards. Godfrey began at 15s. a week at London.
Elephant and Castle, playing innumerable parts weekly.
Now, the music-hall business brings him about £l2O a-
week.

Rignold has a notion of acting Joseph in London.
The Duke of Clarence, afterwards William IV., settled 1
£lOOO a year on Dora Jordan, the actress, Duke of Fife’s-
ancestress. He wrote to her, asking leave to reduce it to-
£soo. In response, she only tore off the last line of the-
Drury Lane playbill, and enclosed it to him : “ No money
returned after the rising of the curtain.”
Chance for the understudy. Miss Vanbrugh was put
out by being understudy for an eminent London actress,,
having to sit in the wings night after night looking at
her, without a chance to go on. Bourchier was the lead-
ing man. Suspirating over the leading lady’s shoulder,,
he fell in love with the understudy and married her.
Mrs. Keeley’s two daughters, Mrs. Albert Smith and!
Mrs. Montagu Williams, were very much alike. After'
Mrs. Smith had been left a widow, a gentleman came up.
to her laughing, at a party, with, “ Your husband wishes
me to apologise, because he is detained in a dreadful!
place.” He meant that Montagu Williams had not been*
able to get away from the Old Bailey.

I he Bank of Victoria, whose net profits have
oeen swindling every previous half-year since December
93, has apparently turned the corner. The net profits
*? r JQ® half ended 3i s t Dec., ’95, are £20,631, which is
it than its last record, and though this is done
solely by reduction of expenses, and not by increased
earnings, still it amounts to a lot. A dividend at
the rate of 5 per cent, per annum is paid on the prefer-
ence shares, absorbing £10,419, and the other £10,200 is
arried to the credit of profit and loss, making a balance
to the good of £26,761 accumulated during the last 21
years. The eternal piling up of cash still continues, the
Victoria having now £1,666,000 of coin and Government
securities, or £250,000 more than it had six months
ago, with no liabilities worth speaking of to meet
until June 1898. During the half-year the paid-up
capital has increased by £45,000 ; the note circulation has
gone up £24,000 ; the deposits have increased by £BO,OOO ;
and there has been a general and cheerful improvement
allround. There is not, and never has been, any infor-
mation from this bank as to the amount of its recon-
structed deposits, so it is impossible to tell how they are
getting on, but, judging from such appearances as there
are, it ought to be able to make a general release shortly.
Also, the statement that “ bills receivable and other
advances are £6,075,829 is a great deal more remarkable
for the energetic way the big row of figures is planked
down than for the information they convey.
The Royal Bank of Queensland still lives up to
its good reputation of the past. It has made a net profit for
“ ™ontlis e ? ded 31st Dec -> ’95, of £6033, against
£5946 last half, and £6476 the one previous. A dividend
of 2b per cent, per annum absorbs £4218 ; £l5OO is added
co reserve, making it .130,000, and, after paying dividend
tax, there is £lO3 (.allied to credit of profit and loss.
The ‘ deposits and otner liabilities ” have been reduced
from £741.,000 to £719,000, by the repayment of another
eighth of the reconstructed funds, which makes one-
fourth of the old fixed deposits paid off. How much
remains can t be ascertained, for the Itoyal lumps together
reconstructed deposits, new fixed deposits, c irrent accounts
and other liabilities ” in one big item, • nus conveying
the smallest possible minimum of inforn at ion. Also, it
lumps together bills payable, mortgage s, and miscel-
laneous advances in the same fashion. The coin, bullion,
and Government securities have increased during the
half-year by £3200, and now stand at £241.671, and, with
this great amount of cash on hand, it . urns surprising
that the Royal doesn’t release its re-constructed deposits
bodily. Under the scheme of arrangement, the Royal is
bound to add one-fourth of its profits to the reserve-fund
every half-year, and, if it adheres to this wise provision,
it must become, some day, one of the strangest banks in
Australia. Also, it wisely keeps its reserve separately in-
vested in Government securities, so that it is a genuine
affair, and not a mere book-entry, as is 100 often the case
with such things.
♦ * * *
The Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. (Mel-
bourne) publishes another unhappy-loc vug balance-sheet.
The receipts from the co.’s regular bm mess of caring for
the estate of the widow and orphan and the deceased have
amounted during the half-year to 14175, and against this
it has paid £4499 in ordinary working expenses and £250
under the head of allowance to the widow of the deceased
manager. Therefore, according to the figures in the
balance-sheet, the agency business has been a serious-loss,
the best feature about it being that it isn’t so large a loss
as that of last year. The receipts have fallen off by £2OO,
but a saving of £6OO has been made in working
expenses—hence the improvement, such as it is. The co.
sets down the value of its freehold property as 65,000,
and the amount received from it in net rents (after
deducting rates and repairs) is just £44 13s. 4d. A lease-
hold property in Queen-street, Melbourne, costs for the
half-year £315 more than the property produces. The
co.’s offices are mortgaged for £25,000, and it holds
£35,000 invested with which to pay off the mortgage
when it falls due ; but it apparently pays £lBl
more interest than it receives, so that the
loss on this item is at the rate of. £376 per
annum. The said investment of £35,000, by the way, is
represented by £lOOO deposited in the Bank of Victoria ;
£20,000 deposited in the Australian Deposit and Mort-
gage Bank (worth about 15s. in the £ on present quota-
tions) ; and £14,000 worth (nominal) of preference shares
in the same disastrous institution. The Australian
Deposit and Mortgage Bank was in a very bad way when
last heard from, and its preference shares, so far as can
be judged from the absence of quotations, seem to have
no present value at all. Yet, as the same “ 435,000 set
aside to meet mortgage ” has appeared in the balance-
sheet for a long time past without alteration, there would
seem to have been no writing down of this alarming
“ security.”
The Trustees, Executors, and Agency Co. has
just written £45,000 off its reserve, to cover depreciation,
apparently under the heading of “ loans on mortgage,”
“foreclosed mortgage,” and “fixed deposits,” for the
last two amounts have disappeared from among the
assets, and the first has been written down 60 per cent.
But the freehold property and the £34,000 located in the
Australian Mortgage and Deposit Bank, both look as if
they might want writing down badly, and the £40,000,
which are all that remain of the reserve, may need
to be applied in this direction. During the year the
co. seems to have sold or got rid of £3300 of deben-
tures, and the only asset remaining which looks really
liquid is £lO,OOO worth of inscribed stock. The interest
received is £450 less than last half, and equals £4 7s. per
cent, per annum on the investments, but whether this
has all been collected, or whether the outstanding por-
tion is included in the “amounts due to the c 0.,” which
is £llOO more than it was last half, and, if so, how much
this outstanding portion amounts to, don’t appear. The
net profits for the six months are set down at £256, equal
to 17s. per cent, per annum on the paid-up capital, and,
when the writing-off of £45,000 is set against this, the
result isn’t cheering, and dividends are naturally sus-
pended. Said £45,000 is alleged to have been “ trans-
ferred to a contingent account ” to make “ full provision,
not only for the fall in the present value of securities, but
for other contingent losses which may arise,” but this
seems to be merely a gentle way of putting it, for no con-
tingent account appears in the balance-sheet, so the
amount would seem to have gone to write off losses
already ascertained. Also the statement about “ losses
which may arise ” calls up the ghost of the £14,000 worth
of preference shares, which don’t appear to be provided
for in any way.
