The New Zealand Insurance Co. publishes an
announcement of its dividend in another column. The
full balance-sheet now to hand shows that the net lire
and marine premiums were £267,916 for the year, being
nearly £3OOO more than last year, and the profit is
£32,953. Against this, however, £27,500 goes in dividends
and bonus ; £6500 to cover depreciation account (and as
the actual depreciation ascertained during 1895 was £8523
this account is somewhat worse off at the end of the year
than it was at the beginning); and £5OOO towards the
sinking-fund on the co.’s Auckland city leasehold; so it was
necessary to take £6OOO from last year’s balance to make
things level. Last year it was necessary to take £6OO
from the previous year’s balance for similar reasons.
Under these circumstances the 12£ per cent, dividend
may be justifiable, but the 1 j per cent, bonus certainly
isn’t. The N.Z. Insurance Co. has a part of its £235,000
reserves in shares, and if these have any call liability upon
them they may make this big concern sit up violently
some day. Also an item of £2647 in Argentine Govern-
ment securities is more or less interesting. Argentine
securities are a lot safer than Confederate bonds certainly,
but beyond that there is little to he said for them.
* * * *
The Universal Permanent Building Society
(Melb.) announces that it has lost £3557 in a year’s work,
whereas last year it lost only £2967. This quaint institu-
tion has alleged assets to the amount of £287,000. Its
office property is mortgaged for £29,000. It has two over-
drafts to ballast down its other assets. It has nominally
£158,000 worth of house and land property, which yielded
last year, after paying expenses, £413, or a little over ss.
per cent., and, as people don’t buy property at a price
which only yields ss. per cent., the present sale
value is something worth thinking over. Its list
of assets, as published in the Record, doesn’t
include one penny in cash, or even one postage-stamp. It
owes £134,000 to shareholders, £72,000 to debenture-
holders, and £20,000 to depositors. Also, during the
year, the amount owing by borrowers has declined from
£97,000 to £67,000, and, when this liquid asset is ex-
hausted, there will apparently lie nothing for the Uni-
versal Permanent to do save sit down hard and look at
its alleged £158,000 worth of property. Meanwhile, it is
a fine example of the modern Australian building society
which lives and moulders in the hope of another boom
where there is no boom on the furthest horizon.
* * * *
Bendigo Mutual Permanent Land and Building
Society announces a profit, after transferring £2OOO to
contingency fund, of £4015 for the year, and pays a 9 per
cent, dividend. Fourth Victorian Permanent Building
Society (Melb.) has closed the vear with a profit of £2711.
The very small and obscure Town and Country Building
Society (Melb.) reckons its year’s profits at £l3B—equal to
about £1 17s. per cent, on its capital of £7524. Which
items suggest that the Victorian building societies outside
the boom-stricken capital are much better off than those
within it ; that the Melbourne societies which didn’t go
booming but stuck religiously to legitimate business are
doing reasonably well even now ; and that part of the
damage done to the building society business has been
through the existence of too many microscopic institu-
tions which aren’t big enough to justify the outlay on
office-rent.
« * * *
The National Trustees, Executors, and Agency
Co. (Melb.) presents a balance very unlike that of
one of its rivals in the same line of business. It
has £30,000 capital and £23,400 of reserve fund, and it
reports the handsome profit of £3OlB for the year 1895.
And with that beautiful sense of the fitness of things
which characterises so many companies, 'it spends £3OOO
in paying a 10 per cent, dividend, and, after writing £l7
off its office furniture, it has exactly £1 left towards pos-
sible depreciations on £43,000 worth of mortgages and
other investments, at a time when depreciation is at its
very worst, and very few securities can stand too close
inspection. In 1894, the same co. earned £3003, and
when it had written £l9O off its furniture account it
made up its 10 per cent, dividend by taking £lB7 out of
its previous year’s balance. And, in 1893, it made a profit
of £4466, and cheerfully paid away £4500 in a 15 per cent,
dividend which, with allowance for depreciation, necessi-
tated taking £5B out of the funds of 1892. A co. with a
good business and good chances which goes on in this
dreadful fashion deserves the worst that can happen to it.
The eighteenth annual report of the City
Mutual Fire Insurance Co. (Sydney) is an eminently
pleasing document for the shareholders. The net pre-
miums, after allowing for returns and reinsurances, were
£13,531, and the fire losses were only £2263. This result
the management ascribes solely to the persistent rejection
of all proposals coming from people with names ending
in “stein” and “berg” and “hoff”and “ sky,” and
similar, suspicious terminations. The premiums are a
trifle more than last year ; the losses are 13333 less ; and
the profit (£6875) are £2BOO better. The City Mutual
gives back £1536 of its profits as a bonus to insurers who
haven’t combusted during the year; pays a 10 per cent,
dividend (£1500) ; adds £2OOO to reserve-fund, making it
£12,000 ; makes sundry allowances for depreciation, and
carries £1478 forward. The only feature which hasn’t
improved is the investments ; last year £25,000 invested
yielded £1165 interest, and this year £27,500 yield only
£1029, equal to 3J per cent.

Since the publication of the last balance-sheet
of the flourishing Swan Brewery, Perth, W.A., that city
and Fremantle have been quietly visited by agents of big
eastern breweries, with the result that several of the latter
have determined to open branch bungeries at Perth.
Furthermore, it is possible that an attempt will be made
to wipe out the concern at the foot of Mt. Elisa.

Vic. Leg. Council has adopted the Usury Com-
mittee’s report and recommends legislation to protect poor
wretches who are admittedly charged 100 to 500 per cent.
Attention was called to D. Henry’s extensive advts. on
the railway-stations, and the Govt, undertook not to
renew the contract. Softgoods Sargood regretted the
committee, its report, and the advt. given to Usurer
Henry—all in good unctuous Tory style—but the Coun-
cil, for once, would not listen to the voice of its charmer.

The balance-sheet of Cairns (N.Q.) Municipal
Council for half-year ended June 30, ’95 (published in the
local papers Dec. 31, ’95) is a t.vpioal illustration of the
way they manage things in Q.N. Bankland. Cairns has a
population of something under 2000, and the municipality
owes Govt. £9025 and the Q.N. Bank 15336—total',
114,361. It collected during the half-year £402 in rates ;
spent £262 in salaries, £265 in street-repairs, owed the
Govt, interest, and added the Bank’s interest to
the overdraft. Its assets consist of £2441 arrears
of rates mostly bad ; £8 9s. 4d. worth of
“ goat-collars and dog-tablets,” and other plant and
materials (including a dishonored cheque for £l4
12s. Bd.), totalling a trifle over 1 900. It has a cash
balance of 3s. 9d., and a balance of liabilities over assets
of £ll,OOO 9s. 6d., and doesn’t feel in the least alarmed
about it. It has the Bank at its back, and Govt, is
at the back of the Bank, and Cairns backs the Govt.
And this cheerful three-cornered fashion of backscratching
goes on all over Kanakaland.

* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — Re the question mooted as to
whether a debt due to two members of (say) a mining co-
adventure can be attached, there is an English decision
to the effect that a debt due to the judgment debtor and
another jointly cannot be attached to answer the debts of
the judgment debtor alone. See 13 Q. B.D. 535. —D.F.
* * * *
The Wentworth mine (N.S.W.) produced last
year gold to the value of £233,475, of which was paid in
divs. £175,000, and £24,000 carried forward The Alad-
din's Lamp, an offshoot, also during the year paid in divs.
£55,000. Together, they probably paid more than all the
W.A. mines put together.
* * * »
The chairman of the Smelting Co. of Australia
told a meeting of shareholders in London that, on the
advice of Mr. Howell, they had bought the White Rock
mine, the lode of which, according to Govt, reports, was
500 ft. wide, worked by open quarry, and they were well-
advised that, in this mine, they had a most valuable
asset. By-the-by, there are two or three items which are
sure to be mentioned at some future meeting—that the
White Rock was floated for a million, and, at that price,
was the greatest “ wild cat ” in N.S.W.; that over £70,000
was spent on it in plant alone ; and, with all, the mine
could not pay working-expenses, with silver 40 per cent,
above its present price. It is a gigantic lode, but to call
it 500 ft. wide is a gigantic cram.
♦ * * *
Garibaldi G.M. Co., Hillgrove, said to have
been floated in London, is about to make another start.
Money has arrived to clear off a mortgage and to pay
directors’ fees. It never paid a div., but was worked at a
total loss of at least £20,000. Poor old Bull!
* * * *
The Eleanora, Hillgrove, is steadily progressing.
The present manager is giving more attention to concen-
tration than to the erection of additional stamps, 50 of
which are lying on the ground—which is better than being
erected—and idle, as they undoubtedly would be. The
next move will be to concentrate the vast heap of tailings
of former years. They are known to contain payable
quantities of gold, besides antimony, while the quick-
silver thus saved should go a good way towards cost.
* * * *
Broken Hill exports last year were valued at
£1,960,980. Silver ore was valued at £587,265 ; bullion at
£959,562; silver ingots at £57,804; copper matte at
£21,230. These, with £ll2 for copper, comprised the
metal exports-aggregate, £1,625,963. In 1894 these
exports were valued at £2,249,628—£500 for copper matte,
£644,896 for ore, £1,543,038 for bullion, and £61,194 for
silver ingots. The figures are not, however, conclusive,
chiefly on account of the new basis of valuing the ore
that has been adopted. Thus it is stated that 189,133
tons of ore were exported. Much of this was concentrates,
one ton representing, say, four or five tons of ore. Then
purchasers of ore return its value net, or, at the most,
put the full value upon only that proportion of the metal
which they expect to extract; whereas, in the past, when
companies exported on their own account, the gross value
was returned upon the assay value of the ore.
The Broken Hill official figures relating to
mineral exports—figures which N.S. Wales Statistician
Coghlan and the Mines Department take over—
have never been satisfactory ; in some cases, through
credit for the’ value of metal being taken twice
over—once on export at Cockburn and again on export at
Sydney—the figures in the blue-books are tens of thou-
sands out. At present there is nothing to distinguish ore
from concentrates, and the value is returned on half-a-
dozen different bases.
While =£1,625,963 is declared as the value of
Broken Hill mineral exports for ’95, the dividends paid
out of profits by B.H. co’s amounted to £732,588 ; and
the Junction, Block 10, Block 14, South, and others,
besides adding to their reserves, have spent very largely
on new machinery. On the figures, the actual working of
the mines has not, even in this comparatively bad year,
absorbed 50 per cent, of the gross receipts ; and for every
pound paid in wages to miners and mine workmen at
least a pound has been paid to non-resident shareholders.
By the removal of the duties on rough timber,
dynamite and blasting powder, salt, chemicals, chaff,
feed, grains, oils, and candles (in part), the B.H. Proprie-
tary Co. expects to save about £20,000 a year. Last year
the B.H. co’s—which get almost all their supplies from
S. A.—paid £9957 duty on rough timber, £4lOO on salt,
£1350 on explosives, and £l5OO bn candles.
The extension of the open cuts and still more
recently the working of the larger and generally more
compact sulphide bodies has led to a big reduction in the
B.H. Co.’s timber-bill. In ’93 the imports amounted to
19,442,471 super, ft., valued at £121,969 ; in ’94 they were
17,524,942 ft., valued at £104,727 ; arid last year 13,280,171 ft.,
valued at £77,902. Still, it is a mistake to suppose that
all the sulphide bodies are compact and require little
timbering. There may be trouble in this direction. The
Junction’s new body, for instance, requires a forest of
wood, and even then it is none too safe.
In 1893 the 155,099 tons of ore exported from
Broken Hill was valued at £924,290. The 189,133 tons of
ore (and concentrated ore) exported in 1895 was valued at
£587,255.
B. South looks particularly well again— i.e., it
has tremendous bodies of from low and medium grade lead
sulphide ore in sight. The weekly output to the concen-
trators is 1200 tons.

A local correspondent tells The Bulletin that
the largest amount of gold for many months, 3600 z., left
Hill End per escort on Bth Jan. This includes the
amount forwarded by the A.J.S. Bank, and includes the
results of all crushings of interest, but does not account
for all the gold won in and around Hill End and Tamba-
roora, as the Chinese storekeeper and others are con-
siderable buyers. Wren and party crashed 80 tons for
16oz. (six weeks’ work for four men). Then came Paxton’s
Tributors, 37J tons for 87ioz. (12 men, eight weeks three
days). Burns and Graham’s Tributors, 7 tons for 340 z.
(20 weeks, one man and two boys). Carver and party, 12
tons for 27ioz. (six men, five weeks). Star of Peace
Tributors, 30 tons for 430 z. (five men, 14 weeks). By none
of the parties mentioned was more than £2 ss. per
week made, while 15s. was the lowest.

Under the heading of “A Return of Sixty
Ounces to the Ton,” Sydney D.T. reports the assay made
by N.S.W. Mines Dept, of a little bit of stone sent from
some show at Harden*-as if it made no difference in
valuing a mine whether 60oz. of gold is obtained from the
treatment of one ton or less than 6grs. from the assay of
a gold specimen. This game will only stop when news-
papers have sense enough to keep such rubbish out of
their columns.

Brave efforts are being made in London to
finance a co. to work Allison’s Wyalong estate between
Sydney and Newcastle, but money is rather slack for such
ventures. Australian coal companies, writes The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent, are not favorites with English
investors, owing to the repeated strikes.
* * * *
The exodus of miners from Newcastle (N.S.W.)
districts to Westralia averages 200 per week. A populous
street in Merewether now boasts but one able-bodied man;
all the rest are grass-widows.
* » » *
C. G. Warnford Lock, manager of the Went-
worth mines, N.S.W., has written another book, this time
on “ Economic Mining.” It is worthy of the attention of
mining novices and amateurs : mining men will find little
in it that they did not know before, but can find in it a
mistake or two to laugh at. Here is one : “ The only two
sources of precious opal are Hungary and Mexico, the
product of the former being far the more valuable.”
The writer had an idea that precious opals were found
in Queensland many years ago, and since then at White
Cliffs and other places.
* * * *
Wattle Flat (N.S.W.) goldfield, though never a
very astounding place, has supported many miners for
many years. Quite a model village settlement has been
formed round it; the inhabitants now number about
1000 souls. Wattle Flat’s reputation was spoilt by wild
cats in the first rash, but the field might now repay a
little attention from outside. The other day, Cox and
Peak struck on the surface an inch leader, which at 10ft.
was nearly 6in. thick, and from which they took £3OO
worth of gold in three days. Two dishes picked from the
vein, and washed in the presence of a Bulletin corres-
pondent, gave about lOoz. gold. The leader threatens to
grow into a decent reef.
* * » «
The Great Boulder employs 280 men, nearly all
from the Barrier.
* * * *
The Gresham syndicate (of London, of course)
has paid up for the Loudon mine, at the 25-Mile, Cool-
gardie, price £35,000. “ The London is a fine property
having a well-developed reef carrying plenty of gold.” It
is left for The Bulletin to add that £35,000 is also a fine
price to pay for any mine in a- country where mines are
rich one day and poor the next. “ Find a mine, watch,
but do not touch it,” is the great W.A. motto.

Another Coolgardie battery—the Premier—at
the 25-Mile. “There are immense heaps of quartz at
grass, and plenty of gold was visible. It is estimated
that the heaps will go Soz. to the ton.” So runs the
description (which was what the big heap of milky stuff
at Bayley’s was to go). Not a word of the fact that the
Premier mine has been working for over two years, that
it has always had a small crushing plant, but never a div.
That it may pay now is the writer’s hope, although the
lode will need to be better than when he saw it.
* ♦ * «
At Woolgangie, the present terminus of the
Coolgardie railway, the water has given out. The Govern-
ment promised to send 13,000 gals. daily by train from Nor-
tham, but, though the inhabitants are willing to pay for it
at high rates, barely 6000 gals. are forthcoming—and it is
taken from the festering waterhole where the people of
Northam wash themselves. At the Woolgangie Hotel
recently they gave up washing dishes, and a dry polish
became the order of the day in that part of the Great
Dirt Land.
* * * *
Railway building has got down to such a fine
point now in Westralia that contractors are willing to
build the line from Esperance Bay to Coolgardie gratis if
they are allowed to work it for their own sole benefit for
two or three years before handing it over. And even on
these terms the unprogressive Government doesn’t see it,
because the Esperance Bay line would take business away
from the dank and festering city of Perth and might
create a rival city on the south coast.
* * * *
The outrageously bogus character of some of the
financial companies floated in London to deal with W.A.
mmes, &c., is already causing trouble. One corporation
is described by a Perth newspaper as “ this huge public
robbery.” and it is demanded that the W.A. directors,
comprising some notable personages, immediately resign.
It is said that they are not responsible for the statements
in the prospectus, and that they declare they have been
deceived. Be that as it may, they should have insisted
upon having everything before them before consenting to
become directors. Whatever the W.A. fields may turn
out this year —and big returns will cover up a mountain
of corruption—they won’t be big enough to stop a revela-
tion of public robbery in London, the obtaining of heaps
of money by false pretences, of great crimes in which pro-
minent men have lent a helping hand, but for which the
law provides no punishment. No wonder Westralia is
bubbling over with loyalty.
* * * *
Life at Menzies (W.A.)Flour, £*6s a ton ;
chaff, fid. per lb.; bread, Is. 3d. a small loaf; mutton,
when obtainable, Is. 3d. ; fowls, £2 2s. a pair ; eggs, 12s.
to 18s. a dozen ; water, Bd. per gallon ; board, £3 3s. to
£i 4s. a week ; fever, very cheap and common. In the
same paper we read of the good time coming when diva
will be paid from W.A. mines going lOdwt. to the ton.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —A friend in Westralia writes
me that he has been given a job on the lease of a British
syndicate with five or six others who are fulfilling the
labor covenants by trenching for the reef. “ Not one of
us would know a reef if we ran up against it,” he says,
“ but if we do happen to collide with anything that looks
something like what we imagine a reef ought to be, we
will bring down the cook to examine and report. The
cook is accounted an expert, because, years ago, a brother
of his ran away, and is reasonably supposed to have gone
mining somewhere.” Kalgoorlie is evidently a fine
country for spud-miners.—Young Snell.
* * * *
Part of the business of one London co., with a
capital of £250,000, is to erect an 80-head stamp mill at
Northam—the nearest permanent water to Coolgardie.
The W.A. rail freights will have to be greatly reduced to
enable ore to stand the trainage.
* » * «
Chief-Secretary Gordon’s trip to England is in
the interest of Fraser’s South mine, Southern Cross. He
leaves for Perth during the first week in February, and
will inspect the property before finally deciding to go
home.
• * • «
The deepest workings on the Murchison, so far,
are those of the Day Dawn mine (230 ft.), where they have
a large body of payable stone. The management intends
increasing the plant to 50 heads, besides erecting a large
cyanide plant to reduce the tailings. The New Chum
mine at Mount Magnet is down 340 ft. This mine has
yielded about £60,000 worth of gold during last year with
10 head of stamps. The Morning Star, also at Mount
Magnet, has very extensive workings, and is crushing
from a formation 50ft. wide in places. This mine has
turned out £22,000 worth of gold in six months with 10
head of stamps. The co., a Ballarat one, is also increas-
ing its plant and purchasing other mines in and around
Mount Magnet.
* * * *
Both T Lenzies and the Norseman now feel the
power of the Archimedean liar.
* * ♦ •
Out of 32,000 Vic. miners, only about 12,000
belong to the A.M.A. Coming annual conference will
consider the appointment of a paid organiser.
* * * *
British syndicate said to have its buying-eye on
the Democrat, Glen Wills (Vic.), which from a 6 to 9in.
reef last year milled 676 tons for 34180 z., and paid six fid.
divs.
* * * *
As reported last week, the Highland Chief mine,
Omeo district (Victoria), lately crushed 36 tons for 2570 z.
gold. Since then another find at Queenstown, close to
the Hume Range, and less than 30 miles from Melbourne,
has been estimated by Govt. Geologist Murray at lOOoz.
gold from 121bs. stone ; 25 tons of stone from the same
claim lately panned out 900 oz. ; and the reef is alleged to
be 15in. thick. The Queenstown country is very patchy.
* ♦ * *
Victoria’s first quartz-mine, the Port Phillip, at
Clunes, has been reconstructed in London, but amalgama-
tion with the N. and E. Clunes is still in abeyance. The
shafts of the latter are full of water, and the chairman of
the Port Phillip says straight out that the old co. is not
going to do a lot of pumping which will have the effect of
draining its neighbors’ mines. “We hold the key of the
position and will dictate terms. . . . We are losing
neither cash nor opportunity by waiting.”
* * * *
The other day the William-street (Melb.) sewer-
trench contractor unearthed some likely-looking stuff at a
depth of 7ft. When treated, 13 dishes gave a color in
each case, while one dish produced 23 small specks. As
far back as ’56 this locality was reputedly auriferous ; in
that year all Melb. rolled up at Flagstaff Gardens with its
kitchen-utensils and turned over the place from one end
to the other.
* * * *
Ex-Sec. for Mines Howitt (now Audit Commis-
sioner) estimates that the Vic. batteries anniially lose
£BO,OOO worth of fine gold. When it is considered that
the Long Tunnel’s refuse tailings, after navigating six
checks by the finest gold-saving appliances in Australia,
stand the purchaser in £2O weekly, the aforesaid figure
is not overstated.
* * * *
North Long Tunnel, Walhalla, to date has spent
close on £120,000 in its efforts to cut the Long Tunnel
reef at a point that would give 600 ft. backs.
* » • *
Long Tunnel, Walhalla (Vic.), has taken out 25
tons gold, and paid £1,131,600 in divs. With the excep-
tion of a brief period when the second shaft was sinking
the mine has an unbroken div. record. The consistent
Australian mine. The present manager, Ramsay Thomp-
son, has held his position 28 years.

SOCIETY
An erstwhile Sydney resident returned,
the other day, to the city of the Beautiful Harbor
ere departing for England. He had been in
Westralia for under two years and had cleaned up
for some =£30,000. He was the only survivor of a
party of four—all of them well under 40 years of
age.
* * * *
Gov. Brassey told an interviewer the other
day that he knew the young German potentate
“ so far as it is possible for a humble individual to
know an emperor.” The humility of Brassey,
when discussing the refulgent greatness of crowned
heads, is alike instructive and touching. It
teaches one that the meanest Smellbourne occu-
pant of a four-roomed cottage is not further away
from Government House than Brassey is remote
from any himperial throne.
Captain Guise, the Evangelistic member of
Gov. Buxton’s staff, is letting his light shine
before Adelaide even brighter than the boss’s.
The captain is a Plymouth brother, or something
almost as dreadful. On the voyage out he quite
eclipsed Gov. B. (who always read the Lessons on
Sunday) by holding a prayer and Bible meeting
every blessed morning ‘‘ on his own. ” A few women
used to attend the meetings regularly and pour
out their beautiful “experiences ” to one another,
whilst the four clergymen aboard were exchang-
ing funny stories on the quarter-deck. Indeed, it
was generally reported that Guise and his tabby
party never groaned forth their final “ Amen ”
until they had called the attention of Heaven to
the parsons’ absence and besought Providence not
to make things very, very hot for them in the life
hereafter. Ordinary sinful passengers took a good
deal of umbrage at Captain Guise, one way and
another. They even complained of his incon-
sistency in rigging himself out as an Arab chief-

tain to attend their fancy-dress ball, when all the
time he was shaking his turbaned head at the
fleshly waltzers. After which the well-meaning
capting was called dis-Guise to his great disgust.
• * * *
The society belles of Tasmania (the same
old lot) are overjoyed at the arrival of Admiral
Bridge and his ships, and have arranged a series
of hops for the delectation of their patrons.
Meanwhile a deputation of matrons has privately
waited upon the Admiral with a long-standing
grievance, which avers that the officers of H.M.
fleet make no matrimonial proposals so far as
Tasmania is concerned. Cyprian has promised to
“speak to them about it,” although, from an
official point of view, and in the general interests
of the navy, he “ feels bound to disfavor mar-
riage. ”
Lady Clarke, who has been vice-president
of the Austral Salon since its inception, has
resigned. The attack on “Lady Lincoln,”in Miss
Murphy’s alleged novel, is the accredited cause of
her withdrawal.
* * * *
George Musgrove’s eldest daughter, Lily,
an exquisitely pretty girl, is shortly to be married
to Mr. Casimir Julian Zichy Woinarski, a Melb.
barrister, son of a Hungarian patriot who fought
under Kossuth.
* * * *
He was a Q. State school inspector, and she
a married female teacher, whose school he was
examining. He asked her for the books of record,
and on her telling him she had left them at home, he
sent her for them, and went on with his examination.
Then, dismissing the school, he, in accordance
with the instincts of his craft, took up a vantage
point—an ambush—whence he could watch the
female teacher’s return. He saw her, as she came
along, hesitate, stop, throw the books into some
bushes, and come on again. As she entered one
door he slipped out of another, secured the books,
and marched into the schoolroom with an air of
triumph. She stood cowering—all confusion. He
said, very severely: “You haven’t kept these
books properly ; you tried to deceive me ; I’ll have
to report you.” “Please don’t,” she pleaded.
“ Yes,” he said, “ I shall.” “No! no ! let me off
this once.” “I must do my duty,” he said, with
great authority. She came closer, flung an arm
round his neck, and exclaimed: “ Write what I
dictate, and sign it, else I’ll scream for my hus-
band.” He wrote and signed ; but, shortly, she
was removed from his district.
O that the commercial men of Australia would
relate to The Bulletin their best personal experi-
ences of attempted blackmail by women !
* * * »
Lord Neville, Gov. Brassey’s A.D.C., is
not of the ranks of that fearful and wonderful
body —the “gentlemen cyclists.” The truesassiety
biker never ventures on his jigger unless attired
in his wondrous suit of pleats and buckles, and
even then it is tantamount to social ostracism to
travel at a bat exceeding live miles an hour. But
Neville casts etiquette to the winds, and his
appearance on the racing-track is eagerly looked
forward to.

’ erbatim extract from a printed beggmg-
letter sent from Jerusalem to a Vic. resident
The time appointed for the marriage of my daughter
with the young and piously learned grandson of the hav-
ing been here chief Rabby is approaching, while we have
neither the least dowry nor the marriage expenses. This
my sorrowful position, repushing back my strongest
feelings of shyness, urges me to yield to the pressure of
the unresistible bitter need and to plead the generousity
of our benevolent religionists. 1 foster the undecievable
hope that your generousity will not admit you to refuse
your aid from a poor widow and her helpless orphans.
**. * *
Apparently the Adelaide Hospital troubles
have not yet ended. Superintendent nurse
McLeod has now resigned owing to further friction,
and Miss Gordon (the Chief-Secretary s sister),
whose appointment over the heads of numerous
senior nurses was the cause of the original trouble,
has been granted leave of absence prior to resigna-
tion. There is much local sympathy with Premier
Kingston, as the Tory crowd, headed by the
Register, is making political capital out of the
affair in view of the March elections.

Ballarat is importing a British parson to
fill the shoes of Dean Stretch. The saint from
England gets the fat of the land, and the local
worker vegetates out back on 255. a week. Why
does not the Church adopt those “ economic prin-
ciples ” which its unctuous shining lights so lustily
maintain, and import only cheap laborers for the
vineyard ?
“ It stinks like N.S.W.
justice.”— New adage.

A S.A. parson named
Herrington thinks that “if
suicide were generally ridi-
culed the suicidal mania
would soon be cured.”
Ridicule is the one thing a
corpse can’t stand. What a
corpse wants is to be taken
seriously, in a spirit of sub-
dued cheerfulness tempered
with respect. If when any-
body died everybody came
and guffawed at the re-
mains, death would soon
go right out of fashion !
Corpses and parsons seem
to be equally insensible to
ridicule.
Ethel Turner, in “Almost an Idyll,”
forgot the main parts of the foreground
and background—the mosquitoes. On the
Hawkesbury and its arms, to be secure from the
ravages of these little she-devils (the male
mosquito doesn’t bite), one must be provided with
an oil-cloth suit, blucher boots, rubber socks a
wide hat with curtain attached, leather gloves
and, if possible, a cwt. of the vilest tobacco and a
cannon with dust-shot, the amount to be accord-
ing to convenience and taste. At Woy Woy last
Oct., a boatman whose wife accommodated visitors
at the house, was making arrangements with a
passenger just alighted from the train for the
holidaying of his wife and children till mid-
sumnier. It was late in the afternoon, and the
mosquitoes and other insects were just starting
work. The boatman was engaged killing them or
his face legs, &c. “ I don t think, said the visitor,
“this will suit the wife. The mosquitoes are
something awful even now ; what must they be in
summer!” “ Oh,” replied the boatman, there
ain’t smack —no smack blanky skeeters—
damn !— smack —now ; but, of course smack,
smack, smack —in the — smack damn ! summer
smack, smack— there are sometimes— smack- -a few
— smack, smack —damn the — smack —I mean damn
the— smack, smack damnthblankysanflies !
In Boomfcown everyone knows Blank, at
least by sight. Blank, with the eyeglass and the
immaculate clothes; Blank, the only man in the
wool-trade who knows wool from cotton. Blank
sometimes rides into town wearing a gorgeous
military uniform ; sometimes a bicycle bears his
precious person; but by whichever means the
greatest impression can be created, by that means
does Blank convey himself to the city. One
morning, when the streets were too muddy
for horse or cycle, Blank rode in on the
front of a tram-dummy; seated beside him was
one of the dirtiest and most drink-soaked old
ladies extant. Blank sat in dignified silence with
his coat drawn in to protect it from the contamina-
tion at his side, when his face suddenly lighted up
as he nodded to a number of his business acquaint-

the lady who accompanied him on that tram.

Few persons purchase shoddy clothing
from choice. Fewer still care to buy inferior
provisions for home consumption merely because
they are ticketed “cheap.” But cheap photo-
graphy has a charm peculiarly its own to the un-
initiated, and 99 per cent, of its patrons must be
classified under that heading. Commodities in-
numerable may be cheap without being nasty, but
photographs are not in the list. CHARLIv
MONT & CO.’S business is based on the prin-
ciple of turning out the best possible work at a
fair price, and they are pleased to be able to assert
without fear of contradiction that complaints from
their customers are almost unknown. Their
studios are at 492 George Street, Sydney, and
at 114 Elizabeth Street, Melbourne, where
their work in all its branches can be inspected
and judged on its merit.*

* * * *
The other day the Melb. Deutschers held
a picnic to celebrate the unification of the German
empire. To prevent Jingo rows, in view of recent
affairs, all speeches were in German. After the
first toast of “The Queen and the Emperor,' 5
when the lager had circulated, fiery political
orations were delivered which would have roused
all Melbourne had they been printed in English.
The Sydney celebration was, somehow or other, a
little less torrid in tone.
* * * *
Memories of that grand old sportsman,
Andrew Town, of Hobartville, Windsor, N.S.W.,
were revived last week, when his eldest daughter’
Miss Emma Town, was married, at Strathfield
church, to Major George Lee, of the N.S.W.
Lancers.
* * » *
“ Much may be said,” writes ‘ Danton ’ to
The Bulletin, “in favor of a good periodical
‘ burst,’ for the nation as for the individual. The
Saturnalia, the Bacchanalia, the Carnival, the last
days of the Republican year in the French Revo-
lution, the old Christmas carousing, See., which
lasted for weeks, may have had their faults, but
they certainly tended to make the peoples con-
cerned more satisfied with themselves and one
another. The idea of a three weeks’ general and
compulsory holiday (say at end of each year), with
free railways, &c., State theatrical performances,
Olympian contests, masks and mumming, and
generaljollification isworth working out—on appro-
priate Australian lines, of course, and with proper
precautions against hardship, &c., and would tend
more than all the prayers and sermons, and thrift
movements, and general politico-economical hum-
bug, to make the people good-humored and ready
for work of any kind, and to develop in unseen
ways truly national institutions and, perhaps,
literature and arts. Wise dissipation is the
safety-valve of any decent sort of man, woman, or
nation.”
* * * *
Half-a-dozen times every day in Sydney
Police-courts the police charge persons with the
offences of cursing and swearing. Will some
stipendiary, for information or out of curiosity,
request the next similarly-attesting constable to
define the difference between cursing and swear-
ing. If there’s no difference, the charge can be
shortened ; if the constable can define a difference
on short notice, then he should be reported for
promotion.

Curio : “ I was decidedly edified the other
evening. _ A gentleman with whom I had some
business introduced me to his wife, an attractive
lady, who said, in chatting, ‘Do you know, he
won’t let me go to the theatre. I think it is so
instructive, don’t you? 5 The husband: ‘No, no.
it’s not good for you. Far better have a good,
healthy drive. 5 A few days after I was surprised
to read of the husband’s alleged real wife, another
lady, proceeding against him for maintenance.”

The “ Straits ” Settlement —Australia.
A desperate attempt is being made to get
a u ranker ” in as Vic. Chief Commissioner of
Police when Chomley retires, and the few remain-
ing non-rankers are preparing to resist it to the
death. If a man from the ranks gets the post, it
must be Superintendent “ Tom ” O’Callaghan, of
Ballarat, who is at the top of the list.
* * * *
The recent fire onboard the s.s. Cintrahas
brought to the front the usual story that there
were not on board nearly enough boats to carry
the passengers and crew, and if the Cintra had
(&Q on {HelLaml , gr
THE AUSTRALIAN SEA-BATHING QUESTION.
gone down a great loss of life must have resulted.
This sort of story comes along so often that the
public wants badly to know if there is any truth
in it. Do Australian steamers often go to sea
without enough boats to carry the passengers
and crew in case of disaster ? Or do they go with
just enough, reckoned on a calm-water basis, and
with no allowance for a rough sea, for a possible
smash in lowering one of the boats, or for the
chance of one or two being unget-at-able in case of
fire ?. Or does anybody really know how they go,
or do they go on any fixed rule whatever ?
A SUFFICIENT EXPLANATION.

Mrs. Brough.
Mrs. Brough never had a player in her family,
but her parents were singers and teachers of
singers. Her father was a bass and her mother a
soprano, and Mrs. Brough was stage-struck long
ere she donned long dresses. She made her first
appearance—as Buttercup in “ Pinafore ” —with
Mr. D’Oyly Carte’s Co. That was in ’79. In the
same auspicious year Mr. Brough, at Plymouth,
joined the co., then touring the provinces, and he
—lucky man ! —formed the nucleus of the most
brilliant of all Australian comedy companies by
marrying Mrs. Brough after 18 months of court-
ship.
Mrs. Brough’s first distinct stage success was in
’Bl, when, with Charles Collette’s Comedy Co.,
she played Mrs. Blyth in “ The Colonel. ” For
the next three years or so, while Mr. Brough was
at the Gaiety Theatre, she played at the Opera
Comique with the American actress, Lotta (meet-
ing the ill-starred Dacre there); at the Globe, in
“ The Member for Slocombe and at the Avenue
in “Our Regiment.” In ’B5, the Broughs were
engaged by Williamson, Garner, and Musgrove
to come to Australia for a 12 months’ season.
* * * *
Mrs. Brough’s first Australian appearance was
as the Fairy Queen in ‘ ‘ lolanthe ”; Mr. Brough
played the Lord High Chancellor. But she always
preferred comedy to opera, for having to sing was
always a perfect torture. “I was dreadfully
nervous when singing,” she says, “and couldn’t
make headway.” Six months after the Broughs’
arrival, the late Dion Boueicault, with Dot and Miss
Boucicault, came to Australia, and, before ’B5 was
gone, Mr. and Mrs. Brough, Mr. and Miss Bouci-
cault, Emma Chambers, Jenny Watt-Tanner, Miss
Phillips, and Herbert Flemming first produced
‘ ‘ The Magistrate, ” and ran it eight weeks at
Melbourne Bijou. Then Brough and Boucicault
conceived the excellent idea of forming the famous
co. which has enabled so many bored Australians
to stay alive, and after both had gone to England
to generally arrange all business, they returned
and began their management at Melb. Opera
House 9th Oct., ’B6. “ Turned Up ” was played,
and “ Jack Sheppard ” —the latter for nine weeks,
with Fanny Robina as Jack and Royceas Blueskin.
And soon after Mrs. Brough began realising her
life’s dream —“ the dream that was born in me ”
she began playing leading parts in comedy,
“comedy, which I was always dying to play.”
* * * *
During the last nine years she has played nearly
a hundred parts. But of all, her choice is Paula
Tanqueray. “ When on the stage, I love myself
in that character ; I live it for the time being ; I
feel it tremendously, and, when all is over, I am
quite prostrated.” She likes—and looks—her part
of Niobe very much, and Kate Verity in “The
Squire.” She says, “I never liked myself as
Kate in ‘ She Stoops to Conquer,’and I’d rather
play Lady Bellaston than Sophia. lam very fond
of Beatrice in ‘ Much Ado.’ ”
* * * *
Mrs. Brough is a quick study, as indeed she
needs to be, considering how frequently plays are
changed in Australia. She says: “It is not much
trouble to learn a new part, and the more the
practice the better your study gets ; I like to be
very quiet when tasking my memory. We some-
times have to produce a play a week after we
first see our parts, and that includes getting our
dresses made, &c. I learnt Niobe in a week. I
learnt my part in ‘ The Passport ’ also in a week.
But Mr. Brough is the quickest study I ever
knew ; he will learn a part as long as mine in less
than half my time.
* * * *
“ When I have a new part given me to play, I
study it alone, and having formed my conception
of the character, present it accordingly in rehearsal.
Mr. Brough and Mr. Boucicault watch my ex-
position and assist me with hints and suggestions.
Any character I am playing never haunts me off
the stage, and I am a good sleeper. lam very
fond of horse-races, but am seldom lucky there.
* * * *
“ Melbourne is one metropolis ; Sydney is an-
other. Very often a signal success in Sydney will be
only a moderate one in Melbourne, and vice versa.
For instance, ‘ Sophia ’ ran but three weeks in
Melbourne, whereas it went for six in Sydney.
Launceston (Tas.) audience is a particularly
bright one ; Wellington and Dunedin are bright
audiences, but Christchurch and Auckland are
nothing like so good.
“I just worship Bernhardt; she is divine in
that she stands alone. Last season, when we
were in Europe, Bernhardt was finer than ever.
Her Camille is the most exquisite thing I have
ever seen, and her make-up in ‘ La Tosca ’ is itself
the subtlest art. Sarah was grander than ever, too,
in a new play, ‘ Izeyl, 5 which gives glorious scope
for emotional acting. Duse’s Camille cannot be
compared with Bernhardt’s ; I sat an unmoved
critic studying Duse, admiring her, yet all the
time catching glimpses of the secret wires, and
hearing now and then the silent cues. But
Bernhardt, who can weep at will, worked me up
each night to tears and distraction, and sent me
away a perfect object.
* * * *
“ I heard Melba sing, for the first time, during
my European holiday, and think her a marvellous
vocalist but not a good actress. She stands and
sings without effort, pouring forth her pure and
beautiful notes just like a bird, yet with all this
richness not giving a hint of pathos. Patti has
sympathy in her voice ; over and over again while
listening to Patti in ‘ Traviata ’ the tears have
welled into my eyes. I heard Calve too, in Carmen;
she is a divine singer and splendid actress.
Ellen Terry, as Nance Oldfield, was wonder-
fully perfect, and Irving, despite his man-
nerisms, got right hold of me in ‘ The Bells. 5
But Irving was supreme in a great little piece of
Conan Doyle’s—‘A Straggler of 15.’ He plays
the old soldier, ‘whose arteries are pipes of lime,
whose heart is shrunken and flabby, who is dying
of ninety years,’ and who, when the final moment
comes, springs back out of the arms of death, and,
‘ standing erect with eagle head and glance of fire, 5
peals out passionately ‘ The Guards need powder,
and, by God, they shall have it ! ’ then falls back,
with a groan, and dies. ”
* * * *
Both Miss Terry and Mr. Irving (Mrs. Brough
says) think highly of Brenda Gibson.
* * » •
“ Mrs. Patrick Campbell, as the Notorious Mrs.
Ebbsmith, plays with a peculiar charm, but not
much power ; nevertheless, she realises the charac-
ter so perfectly that it might have been written
for her. I think we shall play ‘ Mrs. Ebbsmith 5
before very long.”
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

Sydney,
January 21, 1.890.
My Dzab Moorabinda—
Gov. Hampden in a black bowler hat, a festive
And becoming necktie of pompadour black, scarlet
and green, and race-glasses (the same, I wonder,
that Lady H. strapped on him when she “shook
him up,” as set forth in that memorable English
interview ?) on a tan-colored strap, was certainly
the best-groomed man on the first day of the Bong
Bong picnic races. His Ex. was accompanied by
the three vice-regal girls, double-bankea by some
of the A.V.R. damsels, and the dressing all round
was seasonable. Miss Brand wore a white skirt
with fawn silk blouse and scarlet poppies in a wide
hat, her two sisters wearing respectively white
with a blue chin 6 silk blouse, and pink with a
brown hat. The suite was equestrianly gotten up
in shiny boots and things, but I was glad to notiee
that British riding-breeches have moderated in
volume, for whereas once an English horseman
afoot used to resemble a fat new woman who found
it cruelly necessary to take wide, roomy pleats in
her bloomers, there is now some suggestion of leg-
of-mutton shapeliness about him, thank the gods.
The small vice-regal scion had a little baggy riding-
suit and leggings, and rode out on a smart grey
cob, but H.E. and the ladies of the party filled a
comfortable - looking, though not very stylish,
sociable—the vice-regal taste running to yellow
harness on the bay horses.
The dressing was lively on the first day when it
was cool and misty, which made it more difficult
to understand why one or two of the prettiest
society matrons on the lawn should have worn two
veils at once as if their husbands were Turks. A
very few women rode out. The second day did
not show such a good attendance, as the sex was
not there in such an awe-inspiring majority ; and
on both days a large gap was made in the attend-
ance through the two families who run that parti-
cular part of the country being more or less in
mourning and, therefore, socially hors-de-combat
for the present. The Governor turned up in
yellow riding-boots and a poultice round his grey
felt hat. By the way, I hear that his Ex. ’s hats
rather get on the nerves of some of his rural sub-
ject s ; several of whom cannot forgive the Gov. for
making his maiden appearance at Moss Vale in a
straw “ boater ” ; but I think if Lord Hampden
only succeeds in sweeping away the idiotic tall
silk hat and the equally absurd black coat from
the face of this sun-stricken land in summer he
will have done a noble work. The V.R. party
was very <Mgag6 on the second day. The weather
was flaming hot, so, instead of sitting under a
zinc-covered pavilion and gently baking like
bricks, they sat, Governor and all, on the ground
or on camp-stools in the shade of the fence, and so
kept their powder dry. Next year I expect every-
body will be on camp-stools or primitively camped
on the grass.
The day was so warm that most people aimed
at coolness in their dressing, and because of the
sun the double-veiled trick grew to formidable
proportions. I can’t say I like the new white
chiffon veils with black spots ; they make a girl
with a round face look too much like a raw cur-
rant dumpling. The most ultra-fashionable person
present was a society matron with her hair waved
over her ears k la Parisienne, and a white-and-gold
rig-out. Gov. Hampden must have admired a
tall, fair youth who wore a white mushroom hat
draped with white muslin ; though a Sydney legal
sprig topped off with a limp white hat like the
parchment top to a bottle of home-made jam was
still more original.
Society youth in the rural districts has amusing
ways of dressing itself. There was a local lawyer
present, with a black kummerbund feelingly
worked in crewels, as if he had hung a drawing-
room bell-rope round his waist; another had a
lump of horse’s tail set in a handle, and hanging
like a philibeg, and with it he brushed away the
flies—an Indian fashion set years ago on Melb.
Block by the late Captain Standish. Premier
Reid’s dignity wouldn’t permit of his coming
forth comfortably dressed, so he was the only
man on the racecourse m a tall hat and an
umbrella. A committee-man of sunburnt aspect
mitigated his eomplexion with a scarlet necktie,
but about the most variegated figure was a Guv-
mentouse youth in white flannel pants, yellow vest,
pink shirt, and gaudy hatband, who carried an
emerald-green silk umbrella.
Miss Brand handed over the prizes at the end
of the meeting, the lion’s share going to Messrs.
Knox and Stephens, who, I hear, presented their
trainer—the lengthy Tom Payten—with the hand-
somely-fitted dressing-case, first prize in the
Bachelor’s Bag Stakes. The prizes made a regu-
lar shop-front of the back of the stand. I thought
it rather a reflection on the nails of the competitors
that the first prize for the Consolation Stakes
should have been a manicure set. The ladies’
bracelet was of gold, set with diamonds and pearls
in a graceful pattern; and the Ladies’ Mile
trophy, a gold safety-pin watch on an enamel
bow. But all the women seemed to hanker most
after a set of silver-mounted combs and brushes.
The Bong Bong race-ball, held in the new hall,
Moss Yale, on Friday evening, ran until 3 a.m.,
though the vice-regal folks left much earlier—soon
after supper. It is a good thing that the invari-
able V.R. trick of going home on top of the re-
freshments doesn’t catch on to society, or else hosts
would be obliged to set up the food at 1 a.m. in
order to keep the floor full. His Ex. (I think
Lord Hampden talks like actor Titheradge, whom
he much resembles) arrived late and accompanied
by his daughters and all the suite (Lady Hampden
was not present), the eldest Miss Brand wearing
one of the prettiest dresses on the floor—cream
brocade with violet silk sleeves and draperies of
old lace. Miss Kitty Darley wore blue satin with
a white fichu, and her sister white, of course—a
debutante dies hard nowadays. The ball was
rigged up with ferns and flags and easy chairs, the
floor was smooth, and the band kept excellent
time. A tea-and-coffee buffet was in one corner
all the evening, and an excellent supper was set
on the stage. The committee left nothing undone
to ensure a great success.
The dressing was very bright, especially for
January. But everybody wasn’t a pretty picture,
of course. A tall, thin maiden, like an under-
study for a telegraph-pole, most unwisely accen-
tuated her length and slimness by wearing a
clinging black velvet gown in which she' looked
like a note of interrogation long-drawn-out; and
a damsel in yellow and violets had evidently seen
Madge Rossejl in “A Gaiety Girl.” The men
were in a dancing mood and brought two shirt-
collars in consequence—the other one in their
pockets—so, although the evening was warm, they
kept lovely to the end. The heaviest swell on the
floor and the biggest dandy of the whole tribe was,
oddly enough, a youth who adorns Parliament on
the Labor ticket.
“ The Work Girl,” at Sydney Royal, on Satur-
day) proved to be a very breezy kind of drama
indeed, for you must admit the spectacle of a
group of larrikinesses tearing the garments off a
fat Hebrew and ending by triumphantly waving
his trousers at the audience while he escapes en-
veloped in a table-cloth, is rather an unusual stage
effect. It went to shrieks of applause on the first
night, so probably the spectacle fills a long-felt
want. There is more scope for undressing than
dressing in “The Work Girl.” Harrie Ireland
wore a Trilby collar over the dull-grey gown of the
heroine who has been betrayed and apparently
wants everybody to know it; and Miss Phillips,
as a new woman, was the only smart dresser in the
piece; though even she was handicapped by the neces-
sity of dressing vulgarly. Florrie Forde was excel-
lently got up as a coster “ donah,” her tartan frock,
wildly-feathered hat, and well-oiled bang being to
the manner born, and she was well-supported by Mr.
Westmacott, as a coster, with a good song to his
score. Why Westmacott hasn’t another song on
his list, considering how deftly he manages a
comic ditty, is a mystery, seeing that he is his own
manager.
Signor “ Katherine ” Lardelli sends me three of
his characteristic compositions from London. A
toccata in F, dedicated to Henri Kowalski, is a
very flowing melody not difficult to play and hav-
ing a classical savor; a brigandon, dedicated to
Miss Lottie Hyam, is a bright, clever allegro
marcato which that gifted pianiste will make much
of; and then there is a hunting-song for piano, in
which the spirit of the hunt is so evident that you
feel that the dogs and horns are only a page ahead
of you. It is very easy to play, besides.
The great Zimmerman and his wife were hugely
gaped at travelling over from Melbourne per ex-
press the other day—Mrs. Zim. more than her
flying hubby, who looks a lanky, slack-baked kind
of youth, generally hiding behind a big cigar.
Mrs. “Zimmy,” however, was an eye-arrester, for
Australia isn’t educated up to women travelling in
garden-party costume, and Mrs. Zim., though her
groundwork was black, had so many dressy stripes
and tags and odds and ends, culminating in a
wildly-feathery hat on three hairs, and light
gloves, that women who travelled with her
couldn’t take her all in even during 600 miles.
Evidently a dashing American on her travels
carries more frou-frou to the square yard than does
her Australian prototype, who would defer a
journey any day if her shirt-front wasn’t stiff. The
Zims. left per Monowai last Monday, and I did
hear a flying rumor that Mrs. Zim. took to the
deep in cream silk and diamonds.
“The Passport,” at Sydney Lyceum is one of
the liveliest farces B. and B. ever staged—it is one
long, well-dressed laugh in three acts. Mrs.
Brough, as the lady who makes all the trouble,
wore, in the first act, a Prussian-blue travelling
coat with deep collars of brown fur, perky little
toque en suite. Miss Watt-Tanner (who is rather
heavy on the grease-paint just now, and who
shouldn’t say “ prubbably ” for “probably ”) made
an imposing figure in furry wraps which must be
rather trying wear at this season of the year; but,
as the first act moves in Russia, furs are, of course,
in the business, for you never by any chance meet
with a summery Russian on the stage.
Brough has a fur-lined overcoat—and, by the 'y,

Mr. Brough’s trousers present when he is play-

really are expressive things, and trie way Mr. • ■

The second and third acts were dressed alike,
but Mrs. Brough wore such a handsome gown that
nobody minded that. It was a fete dress of carna-
tion-pink silk with a round, white spot, and made
with a full plain skirt and large pink puff-sleeves
to the elbow on a black band. r Llie bodice had no
high collar, but a deep collar-cwm-cape of black lace
and net fell over the back and shoulders, the front
of the bodice having a pleated interval of white
silk set in bands of black and white embroidery.
Long cream Suede gloves and a rakish hat en suite
completed a very handsome turn-out, which also
had two glittering black jet buttons on the back
of the bodice. Miss Watt-Tanner loomed large in
blue. Hilda Spong hardly looked fresh enough
for a going-away bride in a sage-green skirt and a
grey velvet blouse with silver Buttons and grey
fur on the deep color, and her hat looked a trifle
scrunched. Fashionably so, I suppose, for the
latest fashion in hats is said to be something that
might have been kicked by a horse. Up-to-date
girls, when they’re buying top-knots, ask for a
little hat that looks as if it had been out all night
and got jammed in a door. Miss Mabel Hardy
had the flattest gown of all —white silk with a
Romish-altar-looking collar of purple velvet and
fold ; a violet-strewn toque and a palm-leaf fan.
)ot Boucicault, as Greenwood, reminded ine
of Signor Lardelli, and Dorrington looked like an
understudy to Secretary Gathorne Hardy.
Writes my Melb. correspondent: —
“ Florence Young never looked better than as a
boy in the panto, at Melb. Princess’s. Like one of
Diokens’s heroines, she is “a fine figure of a
woman,” and the less clothes worn the better by
an actress whose stock-in-trade consists of her
shapeliness and agreeable singing-voice. For the
same reason, Prince Eucalyptus, in stockman’s
fet-up — i.e. , short grey pants, red silk shirt, felt
at, &c. —is less impressive than in later scenes.
In the gorgeous, if scant, attire of a stage prince,
Miss Young looks splendidly “fine and large,”
and hides no charm worth mentioning. Also, she
frisks with more freedom than when hampered
by skirts, though, as usual, the effect of her facial
make-up is marred by an overdone feverish-flush
round the eyes.
“ Of all the delightful ‘ Djin-Djin ’ pictures, the
frozen forest with its dancing snow-elves wins
most applause this hot weather. Only two colors
appear in the misty raiment of the ballerinas—
pure white and the greeny-blue of moonbeams
shining on ice. The wintry scene gives place to
sunny glades, peopled by chrysanthemum sprites,
thanks to the magic of Fairy Leonore Orelli,
whose wings, by the way, produce so round-
shouldered an effect as to make observers more
than ever unsympathetic with the mental attitude
expressed in the hymn, ‘ I want to be an angel ’!
The flower-fays are quite dazzling after their
poetic predecessors, and the audience speedily
relapses into perspiration in the noon-tide glare of
their pinky heliotropes, glowing apricots and
greens, garlanded with red.
“The golden ballet, with everything (wigs
included) the color of new sovereigns, auriferous
manna dropping from heaven, and a curtain all
gilt chains and electric-lights descending in the
midst, sets everyone yearning for Coolgardie. In-
deed, the tout ensemble is almost too splendid, and
makes one think of the mansion, owned by golden-
kgged Miss Kilmansegg, which was ‘all gold,
fine gold, both yellow and red, beaten and molten,
polished and dead ! ’
“Flora Graupner, as Princess Iris, is unaffected
as ever, though voice and color seem to have
suffered during her retirement. Japanese dress,
too, is slightly trying, and a wearer needs to
possess striking contour or else sparkling vivacity
to counteract the dowdiness of shapeless draperies.
Ihe scene where the small Princess, swathed in
green tartan, or something that looks like it, sits
enthroned m a jinriksha, isn’t a bit Japanese,
somehow; suggesting rather her Britannic Majesty
m her donkey-shay at Nice, clad in the traditional
plaid shawl and mushroom hat, to the awe and
mystification of the Italian populace. Ivy Scott
also, touring the frozen forest in Rob Roy tartan’
looks own cousin to ‘ Ellen Macjones Aberdeen ’
but, though one wouldn’t have thought so, plaids
must be popular in Chrysanthemum country or
they wouldn’t appear in ‘Djin-Djin,’ which is
nothing if not up-to-date.

“Eiton’s song, ‘My old friend Sam,’ causes
much laughter, just because the lamented Sam
used to live near a dam. Owing to defective
punctuation, the said dam sounds as if meant for
a swear word, hence the excitement. A Banana-
land bishop once made the same mistake, it was
in the country, and a few local identities had been
summoned to the parsonage to commune and take
tea with the shepherd-m-chief. The schoolmistress
and organist combined, arriving late, publicly
excused herself on the ground that it was so hard
to find the dam plank in the dark. The poor
thing had a wretched evening, for the guest of the
occasion ostentatiously snubbed her and turned a
deaf ear to everything she said. _ Not until next
day, when he had left the district, did the sup-
posed culprit discover what th# bishop must have
thought.
“ Every trick mastered ensures more bones to
the performing dog. A Melb. girl, educated up
to high-water-mark, disliked teaching and turned
cook. She prospered in her new vocation, com-
manded increasing salaries, and soon felt em-
boldened to apply for the position of housekeeper
at the town residence of Victoria’s most impressive
private family. Luck again served her and she
got the post, chiefly owing to her familiarity with
French. One of her duties is to procure and study
all the new cookery-books published in Paris, so
as to keep her dinner orders up to date, and to her
falls the superintendence of the menu.
“The ways of the lady cyclist are now most
pronounced. The other day a party of three dis-
mounted in Swanston-st., because they did not
choose to dodge in and out among the traffic, ooolly
brought their wheels on the sidewalk, and trundled
them abreast till they were well past Princes
Bridge—all talking and laughing loudly as they
went. This in a busy time of day and a busy
street. As they took up the whole of the path,
pedestrians both ways had either to be jammed
against shop fronts, or turn into the gutters.
Men didn’t say anything, but they looked.
“The latest in coiffures is to allow a loose lock
to hang negligently down the back. Followers of
this fashion of careless elegance when in the street,
however, have to be prepared for the inevitable
gamin with his ‘ Please, Mum, your hair’s cornin’
down.’ ”
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Dear Bulletin, Putting the word
“minion” instead of “nonpareil” makes a big
difference in Frank Bevan’s Sydney linotype
score —which is, correctly, 1 hour, 345 lines solid
nonpareil, 17,940 ens, 12 errors (literals). — Comp.
News of the sudden death, at Bourke, of
Mr. George Loughnan will be received with deep
regret by a very wide circle. In his halcyon days
there was no more generously-hospitable home,
whether for the man who rode or for the man who
walked, than that of Mr. Loughnan, whose kindly
nature did not desert him in less prosperous times.
The women of Melbourne might very well
have petitioned for the reprieve of young man
Strange, who was “ duly ” hanged the other Mon-
day. Strange declared that he tomahawked his
mate for saying nasty things about the daughter
of one of their former employers. Bitter critics
would be disposed to argue that such extreme
chivalry in a young Australian was proof positive
of a diseased brain.
Wisdom of the S.M. H. (Same Mould
Herald). First leader, Jan. 21: —
Every man is the better for having a fairly complete
knowledge of his trade ; not merely because it has a cer-
tain educating influence, but because it may be of service
to him.
Wonderful ! How does the Herald find these
things out ? What a marvellous man Mister
James is ! Not a “ complete ” knowledge, it will
be observed, but only a “ fairly complete ” know-
ledge. Cautious, non-committal Herald ! And
he is the better ” not, it will be noticed, for one
reason only, but for two reasons !
Dear Bulletin,—This is the sort of thing
that the papers up here don’t report. The other
morning a leper, with his face (the lower part
eaten away) covered by a veil, was on Roma-street
railway-station. He was surrounded by a swarm
of Hies, settling on the matter that was running
through the veil and down his clothes. While
waitmg he sat, unmolested by the officials, on one
l ,6 public seats, and almost immediately after
he left some women sat on the same spot. In
another case, discovered only a couple of weeks
ago in a suburb, the leper had consulted several
doctors about his skin eruptions. They evidently
ldn t know what it was, and the man might have
been with his family yet had he not happened to
d C fu - ( his wife to fetch some money to pay
the Chinese vegetable-hawker. Immediately the
Chow saw the man he took his baskets and cleared
without his money, telling the man “Go way:
you no good; you money no good ; you leper.”
Ihe doctors seem to regard the disease as non-
contagious, but the Chinese evidently think other-
wise.-Query. Brisbane.

In all the Australian provinces, except Tas-
mania, the number of children to a marriage seems
to be steadily decreasing.
it is now said that Symon, Q.C., declines
to make the sacrifice of professional income neces-
sary to enable him to enter the S. A. Assembly.
At the S.A. general election, in April,
Premier Kingston will probably be opposed in his
pet district of West Adelaide by Alderman Wells,
of the City Council.
If, after that cablegram to Salisbury,
George Reid’s “ bauble ” does not come on Vic-
toria’s 77th birthday, then there is no “recipro-
city ” in the high Tory Ministry.
The difference between Age and Argus
boiled down —the one says it is Protectionist and
isn’t, the other that it is a Freetrader and isn’t;
the one spells “honor” and “ Lilly dale,” the
other “ honour ” and “ Lilydale.”
Melb. Argus affects to regard as a new
and brilliant idea old Jock MTntyre’s proposal of
a general manager for the Vic. railways, said
manager to be assisted by two sub-advisers, with
Parliament looming in the background.
Nat Thornley, M.L.C. (Vic.), orated last
week about the rabbit pest, and the urgent neces-
sity of Government’s doing something. What is
the matter here with Private Enterprise and all
the other funniments of the Tory box of tricks ?
Premier Turner took the unusual course
of ordering a speech in opposition to a Govern-
ment Bill to be reprinted for general circulation.
The speech was Styles on Railways, and consists
of an interesting comparison of all the Australian
systems.
The decisions of the Plenary Council of
Australasia came at a rather critical time in
Bananaland. On the Education Question no
genuine Democrat can tamper or parley for a
moment. The Fat Men of the North, however,
will tamper with anything. The only grain of
compensation lies in the fact that the Roman
fanatic is, to a certain extent, balanced by the
Orange fanatic, and vice-versa.

The Tasmanian remarks: “The N.S.W.
bootmaker will now be able to got ail his rnatenal
at first cost, and it will be strange if he do^nt
maintain control of his own market.
Still, as one Sydney bootseller alone "

Under the Maoriland “Animals Protec-
tion Act Amendment Act,” any person introduc-
ing any insect into the province, without the con-
sent, in writing, of the Minister for Agriculture,
may be fined from .£5 upwards. An unsavory
passenger found coming ashore with the Norfolk
Howard about him might have a bad time under
this law.
The Vic. Chamber of Manufactures objects
to a legalised Eight-hour Day. The kind old
thing says it is a mistake for the State to teach
the people to be lazy. The limitation to 10 hours’
work in one day for boys and women should be
altered to 11 hours, and there should be no fee for
suspending the limitation.
The Vic. Bootmakers’ Ass. thinks it is a
great mistake to regulate child-labor in factories.
The Assembly Bill gives the Factory Board power
to determine the number and wages of improvers
and apprentices—and this is reckoned a grievous
sin. The Vic. bootmakers labor under the im-
pression that the Vic. tariff on boots was estab-
lished to assist employers in coercing labor.
The Melbourne Single-tax organ, the
Beacon, draws attention proudly to the fact that
N.S. Wales “manufactures more goods locally”
than Victoria, as brilliant evidence in favor of
Foreign-trade. Coghlan’s latest statistics assert
that Victorian manufactured goods amount to
£22,000,000 a year, and N.S.W.’s to £16,000,000
only, so presumably the Beacon’s statistics are
manufactured on the premises, as usual.
The Factories Amendment Bill, passed by
Vic. Assembly, provides imprisonment for a third
breach of the Act. This is characterised as
“brutal” by a protected bootmaker giving evi-
dence to a Council Committee. Insanitation,
overwork and underpayment are the offences to
which imprisonment is attached, and doubtless
such trivial details in the blessed Gospel of Com-
petition really merit reward and not punishment.
Mr. Blake, Q.C., the Irish member, hasbeen
denied the use of Melb. Town Hall for the purpose
of a lecture on Home Rule, because the promulga-
tion of his views “creates dissension,” but the
local L.O.L. easily secured the use of the hall for
“ a demonstration.” The L.O.L. doesn’t create
dissension. Dissension may be defined as the
trouble that catches the loyal Fatman under the
chest.
Quite on the cards that Tasmania will get
nearer to One-man-one-vote than was thought
possible, by an unexpected side-wind. This, of
course, barring accidents which consist (as
usual) of Upper House. The Federal Enabling
Bill now going through Parliament provides that
“no person mall vote more than once ” for the
Convention delegates. It does not mean that
every man will have a vote, but it knocks plural
voting endways.

When blundering old John M‘lntyre was
Vic. Minister for Lands, he struck a fatal idea in
the way of retrenchment, and sacked the whole oO
rabbit inspectors at one fell swoop. Before his
time, through rigidly keeping the farmers and
graziers up to the mark in the matter of rabbit-
destruction, the plague had been so much
diminished that people fancied it was almost got
under. Now, it is nearly as bad as ever, and the
struggle with the long, sad, interminable bunny is
going to be started again from the beginning, and
in the agricultural districts people throw mud at
the very name of MTntyre, K.C.M.G.
A meeting was called at Coolgardie last
week to fix up a resolution stating that that corner
of the Red Dust Land would support the British
Government and see it through. Two thousand
uninterested dry-blowers turned up but flatly re-
fused to regard the business seriously, and after
two hours of cat-calling and derision the matter
fell through. Salisbury, therefore, didn’t get a
cable stating that the 45-Mile would stand by the
empire to the last, and that the 90-Mile and the
15-Mile and the half-way soak would back it up.

Premier Kingston (S.A.) thinks Ballarat
about the best site for the capital of Federated
Australia.
Those who read of Judas Chamberlain’s
repudiation of the South African Chartered Co.
probably forget that, a few weeks ago, he favored
such another brigand association exploiting the
Northern Territory of Australia.
War, pestilence, shipwreck, miraculous
deliverance from either, any event big enough to
stir the sentiment of a people, has always been
productive of sweet songs and noble poems. Note
the alleged poems so far written on Australian
Federation, and judge the size of the sentiment
behind the movement.
Circumstances and unscrupulous ambition
are forcing Chamberlain farther and farther from
his early convictions. Not only has he, the first of
English Home Rulers, turned Unionist, but now
he is the leader of the “ spirited ” policy which he
was foremost in denouncing when Beaconsfaeld
was boss Jingo. What democracy has lost by the
change can be estimated only by those who
lived in Brummagem when “Joe’ was a mere
municipal politician.
Premier Kingston is anxious to have the
S A. Federal delegates elected at the same time
as the next Parliament, so as to ensure a heavy
vote and a consequent Democratic success. A
special election, with the whole province voting as
one constituency, as provided by the Act, would
be too expensive for all but the Bakers and the
Downers to run effectively. The S.A. representa-
tives are each to receive £2 2s. daily, for their ser-
vices at the Convention.
There will be a big woman-vote at the
approaching S.A. elections. Already there has
been much organised canvassing, and it looks as
if the fight will be mainly on Tory and Demo-
cratic lines ; but the doubtful element is the
W.C.T.U. section. Whether the cold-tea women
wiil support the Ass candidate, who is a teeto-
taller but has a morganatic wife, or reject him and
vote for the Democratic candidate with one wife
and a 40-horse-power thirst, is a problem yet
unsolved.
The Sydney stipendiaries sitting in con-
clave under the auspices of a Freetrade Govern-
ment have decided to grant hawkers’ licences to
Chinese, Indians, Syrians, and other colored trash
without reference to racial distinction wher® no
valid objection is raised by the police. lhere is
plenty of money behind the Indian-Assyrian hawk-
ing trade. After Magistrate Smitherss recent
refusal to license a lot of Syrians, a very stout
Asiatic harangued a gathering of his indignant
fellow-countrymen in sound English from the
front step of Redfem Police-court, proclaiming
that he had over £BOOO invested in the hawker
and would be ruined if his travellers
were refused legal permits.
Westralian Premier Forrest has put Ms
foot down on the wrangling of the Government
departments which used to provide the Perth
papers with much spicy copy. There is also to be
no more abuse of the “system.” It was the
custom of the reporters, especially when the
Premier was away, to devote one day to each
department, which seized with alacrity the oppor-
tunity of allotting the blame for all the adminis-
trative muddling in W.A. to the other depart-
ments, placing what was left over on the
“ system,” the universal scapegoat. Ihe enforced
harmony is resented all round.
Several persons from Adelaide and Sydney,
whom no one has ever heard of, were in Melb.,
the other day, on holiday excursion tickets ; they
were picked up by several local people whom no
one ever wants to hear of, and the result was the
Australasian National League. What these un-
necessary nonentities want is Law and Order,
Restoration of Confidence, and Opposition to
dangerous Socialistic Legislation. They likewise
want advt. for their own miserable selves on the
cheap Tory tack, if possible—but, anyway, advt.

Just now there is trouble in S.A. Cabinet
because Premier Kingston declined to make a
recent insolvent a J.P. at the request of one col-
league, and because he declined to rectify an
indiscretion by which the wrong man was gazetted
a J.P. in another colleague’s district.
The only two S.A. politicians who have
definitely announced their candidature for election
to the Federal Convention are President Baker,
of the Legislative Council, and J. V. O’Loghlin,
M.L.C., “ who will carry the R.C. vote” —alas
that there should be a R.C. vote in Federation !
The members of the Ministry who will stand are
Premier Kingston, Treas. Holder, Chief-Sec.
Gordon, and Education Minister Cockbum.
Opposition-leader Downer is a doubtful starter,
and just now he is so much broken-down by the
serious illness of his wife that he can think of
nothing else.
An attempt is being made to heal the
breach between the M‘Carthy and Redmond
sections of the Sydney Irish on the ground of a
common alliance against Tim Healy. Tim is sup-
ported by a large minority of his countrymen —his
expulsion was carried by 47 votes against 40 —and
next general election may give him a majority.
As a fighting politician he is worth a hundred like
Dillon and M‘Carthy. He knows Ireland, and,
above all, his chief lieutenant, Arthur O’Connor,
is one of the ablest and least demonstrative Irish-
men sent to the Commons. Speaker Brand, Gov.
Hampden’s father, pronounced O’Connor to be the
ablest Parliamentarian of the ’Bl session, while
his fiscal knowledge even then marked him out as
the first Treasurer of a Home Rule Parliament.
The smash of the N.S.W. Civil Service
Superannuation Fund can’t be very long delayed.
The new Act permits all civil servants to stop
contributing to the fund, and as a vast proportion
of the younger ones whose pensions are very re-
mote have stopped, and a lot more will do so, the
income of the fund will probably drop one-half.
Those who stop paying are to receive back all
their contributions with three per cent, interest
when they leave the service, therefore a lot of
what money there is must be set aside to meet
their claims. Meanwhile the pensioners go on as
usual, and all the old civil servants who will
become pensioners in a few years stick to the fund,
so though the income goes down enormously, the
outgo will remain quite, or very nearly, as large
for a good many years yet. Therefore, the million
or two needed to prevent the fund going bankrupt
must be found before very long, else the pensions
will have to stop and the superannuated under-
secretaries will begin to be captured by the police
at night in the Domain and charged with sleeping
out under copies of the S.M. Herald.
