Personal Items.
“ Toby ” Barton, Q.C., entered his 47th
fear on Sunday last.
Ex-Governor Henry Barkly, of Victoria,
has entered his 81st year.
One of the leading lights of the Melb. three-
ball industry is known as “ the Pawnee Chief.”
Dean O’Hea, Melb., baptised bushranger
Ned Kelly as an infant, and visited him in gaol
before he was hanged.
A great-grandson of President Kruger,
together with several Boers, are in Melb. tram
service as gripmen and conductors.
A direct descendant, on the maternal side,
of Dr. Johnson, the lexicographer, recently took,
at the age of 21, his B. Sc. degree at Sydney
University.
Photo, by Talma.
HARRY MUSGROVE, Manager of the 1896 -
All-Australian Cricket Team.
George Bull, the Sydney pressman
matineed the other day, goes to England to
marry Madge Rossell, the sprightly little Dutch
Gaiety dancer.
Premier Kingston declares Gov. Buxton
and his flock to be the most genial, sociable, and
common-sense family who have ever inhabited the
Adelaide vice-regal mansion.
There are 605 honorary J’sP. in Tas-
mania, not including aldermen and Mayors. The
oldest living J.P. is Edward Dumaresq (now lying
at death’s door), appointed on 12th July, 1828.
On Hell-fire Monday, Dan O’Connor, at
the completion of his Turkish bath, was asked by
an attendant if he would like a plate of oysters.
“ No, thanks,” said Dan, “ Oi’ve just had an Irish
stew.”
Judging from recent newspaper correspon-
dence, it would seem as if the only State testi-
monial a number of N.S.W. ratepayers would not
begrudge poor old Parkes would be a State
funeral.
Prince Bismarck sent a pulpit Bible to the
Blyth (S.A.) German church, and it arrived early
this year. The same church was, some years ago,
presented with a bell by the present Kaiser s
grandfather.
Ac ting-Justice Cohen’s term has been ex-
tended for another six months, which simply
means that, according to precedent, Mr. Cohen
will be the next permanent N.S.W. Supreme
Court Judge.
Tasmania has 162 lawyers. Adye Douglas,
the Leg. Council President, is the oldest, having
been admitted to practise on March 2, 1839. A
member of the Douglas family is among the latest
admitted —Feb. 26, 1895.
Adelaide Society considers itself specially
favored. The Governor’s wife is a god-daughter
of Queen Victoria. The Private Secretary, Captain
the Hon. Albert Edward John Henniker-Major,
is a godson of the Prince of Wales. He is also a
bachelor and the heir to a barony.
The “Mary Dean Fund” is practically
closed, the total amounting to little over .£lOO.
Of this sum Miss “ Concord ” Walker gave .£2O ;
Pilcher, Q.C., £lO ; Salomons, £5; “Jack”
Want, £s—the balance being mostly contributed
by lawyers. However the general public may
have sympathised with Mrs. Dean, it much re-
sented the transparent attempt to whitewash
Judge Windeyer by means of an officially-pro-
moted subscription.
Melb. demise : Barrister, also called Capt.
Taylor, ex-M.L.A., a clever amateur histrion. He
cut a swanky dash in Parliament, and looked
decidedly a bigger man than Premier Turner, a
year or two ago. The boom hit him hard, after
which he didn’t seem to think life much worth
living.
When Gov. Carington figured publicly in
England, some time back, a certain Australian
notable, who, to the great grief of his many
friends, had become mentally deranged, cabled
him an invitation to dinner, and then went round
the city ordering 400 sides of bacon here, two tons
of cheese there, and enough beer to build a rampart
round the local barracks.
Mrs. Zander, a remarkable woman, died
the other day in Melb. About 30 years ago, the
death of her husband, a leading Melb. merchant,
plunged the family in distress. Mrs. Z. and her
two daughters pluckily went to the warehouse,
without any knowledge of the business, took up
all its skeins, and carried it forward even more
successfully than Zander had done.
It is fashionable amongst colonial historians
to avoid reference to Johnny Fawkner’s father
when writing up the fathers of Victoria. And
yet it is a sufficiently known fact that long before
the Indented Heads or the Yarra knew Johnny,
his pa took a trip to Van Diemen’s Land, and,
like many others, was so impressed with the
climate and scenery of the island that he decided
to stay there.
Tasmania’s new knight, P. O. Fysh, was
originally ‘‘ in the softgoods ” in the warehouse of
the Melbourne Stevensons. He it was who on
first entering office as a Minister years ago re-
ceived a communication from the then Secretary
of State for the Colonies signed “ Your most
obedient humble servant, Carnarvon,” which was
duly acknowledged by “Your most obedient
humble servant, Fysh.” Fact.
Justice Hodges (Vic.) has absolutely no
sense of humor. Once, when at the Bar, he made
to a brother barrister a slight admission to this
effect, but he added, “ I intend to take up the
subject during next vacation.” He had mapped
out for himself a course of “funny anecdotes,”
no doubt. Before the vacation arrived Hodges
was appointed to the Bench, and apparently his
court has been sitting ever since.
Tasmanian Premier Braddon is the unfor-
tunate victim of over-generosity on the part of
friends and acquaintances —and even of strangers
—for not so long ago some disconsolate widow sent
him a pair of infants, with the request that, as she
had no further use for them, and her late husband
was a Tasmanian, he (the unfortunate Premier)
would be good enough to “ provide for them.
The position was an awkward one, for Braddon
couldn’t give them away—neither had he any
immediate use for them, nor might he drown
them—and the all too generous donor had left no
address to which they might be returned.
Braddon’s sorrows are by no means over yet, for a
friend, or enemy, has just sent him a present of
three young Tasmanian tigers. For these also
the wily Premier has no immediate use.
A brother of Andrew
Lang, the English critic, died
in the prime of life at Castle-
hill, Canterbury (M.L.). Years
ago, A. C. Lang was the
Napier resident partner of
Murray, Roberts and Co., when
that firm was at its zenith.
Severing his connection there-
with, Mr. Lang bought por-
tion of the well-known El-
derslie estate, in the South
Island, where he soon made
a name as a breeder of
prize sheep and a successful
farmer.
Miss Florence Wilton, the
festive lady imported for Melb.
Royal pantomime, is one of the
most pleasing burlesque artists
The Bulletin can talk about
just now. Her energy and pluck
are severely tested at the Royal,
where there is nobody to assist
her in making a pace. Miss
Wilton needs a tandem—we
had almost slipped into the error
of writing triplet—to take her
along, as cyclists would say.
No show could travel too fast
for this enthusiastic worker,
who has fairly earned the right
to look like her onetime over-
study, Nellie Earren. Miss
Wilton is among the few legiti-
mate principal boys. Some
ladies are bom to wear tights,
some achieve those soothing
garments, whilst others merely
ave tights thrust upon them
for the sake of their legs. The
subject of this notice was born
to wear the articles in question.
She acts in obedience to the
grandest law of the theatrical
nature, likewise by the blessing
of Providence, we believe. Miss
Wilton certainly possesses the
Earren capacity for being “a
jolly little chap all round,” and
The Bulletin hopes that her
projected appearance in Sydney
will duly happen.
Interesting to John Smith : Down South
the other day, a constable sent to serve a sum-
mons on “ John Smith, Ho. 9 —— street, Colhng-
wood,” found the address given was occupied by a
church, and so reported. The paper came back
with a minute from his irate superior offioer:
“ This is the 17th man this month who has given
his name as John Smith and could not be
Arrest the next man who says- he is John Smith.’
Mr. Blake, M.P., Q.C., who acted as
arbitrator in the M.L. Midland Railway affair,
and who has come to Australia to speak on Irish
Home Rule, is in appearance a typical advocate
of the Ireland, Martin, and Molesworth type—a
man with a massive head and a face in which are
combined the firmness and severity of the monk
and the humor of the comedian. Mr. Blake wears
on his forehead the big curl which you so often
see on the head of the virile man who remains
boyish until old age.
v Justice Dodds, of Tasmania, put up some-
thing of a Parliamentary record, having attained
office as a Minister without once speaking m the
House. His solitary pre-Ministeml vote—a silent
one —settled the fate of the Giblin Government in
78, and, being the only available lawyer, he was
made Attorney-General in the Crowther Ministry.
Dodds held continuous office, either as Treasurer
or Attorney-General from 78 till lean
years. The fat years of a Puisne Judgeship have
happened along regularly ever since.
Edward John Eyre, who landed in Sydney
in 1833 and who published “ Discoveries in Central
Australia” more than 50 years ago, still lives
in England. In 1840 Eyre started on foot from
Adelaide for King George’s Sound, which he
reached on 7th July, 1841, and he was probably
the first white man to pass over the present site or
the Coolgardie diggings. In ’46 Eyre was ap-
pointed Lieut.-Governor of Maori land under Hori
Grey, with whom he was closely associated m
South Australia. He will be 81 tins year.
One of the oldest Victorian solicitors, G.
J. Pitman, died at Hawthorn (Melb.) last .week,
aged 93. Thirty years ago, when practising a
Bendigo, he “retired,” and went to live m rviei-
bourne. He appeared in court only once atter-
wards, at the age of 80, to assist in the defence ol
a relative. He was admitted an attorney in -Itup-
land in 1826, and in Victoria m 02. Robert
Wallen, merchant and journalist, who died m
Maoriland a year or two ago, and the late superin-
tendent Winch, of the Vic. police, married
daughters of Mr. Pitman.
Melbourne Argus has scored freely over
the Transvaal affair with its well-informed notes
on the cables, by “Uitlander.” Ihe writer is
little F. J. Samson, a Hebrew character of a kind
that went out of fashion about the time he was
born. Samson affects the obsolete pane of glass
in one eye, and a smallish bell-topper on an ex-
ceptionally large round head. He is only lately
from Johannesburg, where he put m about six
years of journalistic life, and he is suspected ot
bunging up one optic with a toy window m order
that he may concentrate the other on the main
chance.
Mr. Frank Gardiner, husband of the
popular Miss Carrie Swain, and well-known in
Sydney as a bold mining-speculator, is paragraphed
in London as having, with Wolf Joel (Barney
Barnato’s kinsman and factotum), won some
<£15,000 or <£16,000 at Monte Carlo in half-an-hour
recently. Mr. Gardiner, who in his career has
endured many ups and downs with an unmoved
countenance, is said to have originally been a clog-
dancer. He is a man with the impassive face oi
one of Deadwoo 1 Dick’s Red Indians, and was
obviously intended by Nature to play poker for
whole continents.
Mrs. Parnell, mother of the dead Irish
leader, is, it is cabled, found to be insane. Other
members of the family have displayed
Beyond a doubt, her illustrious son was “ looney
for years before his death. “ The unknown is
worshipped.” When forced to come into the
open, the calm, cool leader was found to be the
hottest, rashest and most foolish of men—or else
he deliberately assumed that r6le as suited to an
occasion when it was necessary to take the enemy
by storm or not at all. Could Lecky now add
another chapter to his “Irish Leaders,” how would
he sum up Parnell ?
Mrs. Fysh, the Treasuress of Tasmania, is
renowned for her soups, which are always made
Miss FLORENCE WILTON, Principal Boy in Melb.
Royal Panto.
Photo, by Talma.
by her own hand, and are worthy of Soyer, of im-
mortal fame. Mrs. Fysh likes her little joke, and
somewhat staggered Admiral Bowden Smith by a
remark made regarding the soup at one of her
husband’s official dinners. “Your soups are
simply divine creations,” said the Admiral to the
lady, adding—“ You must have a very good
cook.” “So I have,” was the lady’s reply ; “ but
I caught Phil kissing her in the kitchen just now. ”
Hereupon the consternated Admiral nearly fell off
his seat. “Oh,” said Mrs. Fysh, “you needn’t
look so shocked. I was the cook.”
Mrs. Keith Stewart, daughter of Gov.
Fitzroy (the lady who turned the first sod of the
first Australian railway at Redfern, 3rd July,
1850), lately died in England. The railway which
was intended to “ connect Parramatta with
Sydney ” was the work of a private co. of which
the late Charles Cowper was secretary. The ven-
ture was a failure and was taken over by Govern-
ment. The spade and barrow used on the occasion
were presented by Mrs. Stewart to Mr. Cowper
and recently by his son, Sheriff Cowper, to the
N.S.W. Railway AgT

Dear Bulletin, —Why, oh ! why afflict the
world with paragraphs re the authoress of “ One
Woman’s Folly”—or is it “One Woman’s Wis-
dom”? Even the giant Murphy, grown by
O’Hooligan O’Hara, of Paddybilly, had more
points of interest than this exceedingly small
literary potato. Miss Agnes Murphy is now
“ known,” and owes her fame largely to the insati-
able anxiety of 500 Melb. journalists to get six-
penny paragraphs into the papers. Any other
nonentity might be as easily set up for bom-
bardment. In fact, fame or reputation (same
thing) in man or woman nowadays appears
to be due mainly to the industry of the small
paragraphers who stand prepared to confer glory
indiscriminately for ninepence or so, by the
editor’s leave. Surely Miss Murphy’s work is not
good enough to deserve this wholesale and persis-
tent condemnation? —Yours, C.M.G.
Harry Musgrove’s appointment to the
managership of next Australian Eleven was warmly
advocated by The Bulletin, and should please
everybody—save, perhaps, the 30 odd rival candi-
dates who lost the race for the billet. Even these
must admit that a really good man got home—or,
rather, will go ’Ome in March. The original of
the photo, published herewith has the advantage
of not being local to any province. He is a citizen
of the Southern Hemisphere. Among many all-
round theatrical experiences, extending over the
past 10 years or more, Harry Musgrove’s charge
of the “ Lonnen ” Gaiety Co. tour has been well
instanced as proof of his business and social
ability. A manager who conducts an army of
stage players through a successful six months’ cam-
paign will find as little difficulty as possible
in preserving peace and goodwill among 13 healthy
cricket artists, also on tour. That the manager’s
family name will assist him in his business rela-
tions with many Britishers goes without saying.
All visiting English elevens have carried ’Ome
pleasant recollections and honorable mention of
Williamson and Musgrove, and their chief officer,
Harry. Indeed, The Bulletin fears that the
Firm is somewhat of a silken bond binding us to
the “ mother country. ” Thirteen has often proved
an unlucky member of cricketers to send abroad.
The team that travels with Harry Musgrove will
include 14 players, available for active service.
If the captain calls upon the manager to take part
in a game, it will not be his maiden effort against
the best English bowling. Musgrove bats with
the graceful science that satisfies the stalls, and
the vigor which endears a performer to the gods.
He’ll be .there if the Eleven wants him. The
Bulletin hopes, however, that the manager’s
lubricating influence will make the Eleven work
so well that he will remain a reserve force all the
time, and be chiefly occupied in smiling at an
unbroken succession of Australian victories and
an unprecedented flow of gate-money to the
treasury.

The usual Half-yearly Dividend and Bonus of
£750 was paid to Shareholders in July ; Bonuses
amounting to £1536 3s. Id. were paid to Policy-
holders ; an Investment was written down £265
17s. 9d.; Office Furniture account was reduced
£4O; and Bad Debts written off were £54 14s. 3d.,
leaving a balance of £4228 6s. 7d. for appropriation,
which the directors propose to apply as follows :
To Balance Shareholders’ Dividend

cent, per annum £750 0 0

now £12,000 2000 0 0

The conservative policy of the Board has been
continued during the past year, and holdings on
many classes of risks have been reduced.
The care exercised in the acceptance of business,
and the cancelment and refusal of risks of a doubt-
ful nature, has resulted most satisfactorily, as evi-
denced by the small amount paid for losses, the
ratio to nett premium income being less than
17 per cent.
The funds, including paid-up capital, after pro-
vision for all outstanding claims and payment of
the dividend and bonus, now amount to £28,478,
being more than 210 per cent, of the premium
income.
The amount which would be required to re-
insure all risks current is £4510.
The Dividend to Shareholders is now payable at
the offices of the Company, and the Bonus to
Policyholders is payable on renewal of policies.
JOHN SEE, Chairman.

RAZOR
Editor Bulletin, —You lately printed a
letter re the originators of the A.W.U. amalgama-
tion scheme. It matters little who originated or
organised it, for the whole affair is obviously a
farce as far as the shed laborers are concerned.
Every office in t he organisation is held by shearers.
A laborer aspiring to office i 3 simply ridiculed.
Shed laborers working with non-Union shearers at
full Union rate of wages are denounced as “scabs,”
while shearers, so far, have been perfectly satisfied
to shear with under-paid non-Union laborers so long
as j6l per 100 and Union agreement was obtained
for themselves. Shearers don’t think the poorly-
paid “ loppy ” worth consideration, and, because he
earns less, look down upon him as an inferior
kind of being ; yet, though the average shearer
regards a rouseabout as so much dirt, he
expects him (the humor of it!) to stand out
with him and battle for his (the shearer’s) =£l per
100, which seems the whole aim and object of the
A.W.U., and absorbs most of its officers’ time and
mind —forgetting the factthat laborers aremembers
and contribute to official salaries, &c., as well as
shearers. Shearers also forget or wilfully over-
look the fact that the rouseabouts, most of whom
can shear more or less, and would.have been more
useful to squatters than men got from large cities
during strikes, could have overthrown the A.S.U.
years ago had they wished. But they were loyal,
and assisted the shearers in all troubles purely on
principle. To my knowledge only two officers
(Thos. White and Donald MacDonald, of Bourke
branch A.W.U.) have ever sincerely taken up the
shed-laborer’s cause since the amalgamation has
been established. I mention these names because
the writer of above-mentioned letter demanded
accuracy in the matter, and considered it part of
Australian history.—6 x 8.
Spielmann, in his History of Punch,
notices that the thick atmosphere of hard and fast
conventionality surrounding that dreadful literary !
poultice has “ dulled even the brilliancy of Phil
May’s pencil.”
On Glenthomson, Vic., a number of sheep
have developed a fleece of a queer kind. The first
four months’ growth of the fleece is chocolate, the
next four months’ is white, and for the last four
i the colpr is black. The bands are very even
throughout, and clearly marked.
The largest calculus ever removed by
operation from the human bladder is one in a
Brussels museum, weighing 31b. ihoz., and 17in.
in circumference. In London Royal College of
Surgeons is the second biggest, weighing 21b.
lOloz., and Bin. long by 4in. diameter ; while Cam-
bridge University Museum has the third largest
weight, 21b. flgoz., length, 15in., breadth, 1.3 2 in.
This last, however, was removed after death.

A 3100-carat black diamond (2129 carats
more than the biggest hitherto known) has been

Beastly Jolly
Jolly Beastly
When school breaks up ;
When school begins again.
That’s how the English schoolboy puts it, according to Max O’Rell.
The “ jolly beastly ” period is at hand.
Boxes and trunks, hauled with reluctance out of their hiding-
places, must be packed.
The
Question arises :
WHAT ABOUT CLOTHES?
Jack cannot face the school with those shabby things he came home in.
Tlio Answer suggests itself
where economy is practised-

Lady Barkly is another Bendigo tip-topper. In
80 years it has won £261,654 ss. Id. gold, and divided
£96,316 6s. Bd. on £5783 6s. Bd. called capital. Last half-
year’s yield, 46040 z. 3dwt. ; average, 12 £awt. ; divs. paid,
£40,600 —Bs. 9d. on 24,000 shares. The old stopes promise
div. stone for a long while yet, while at 1130 ft. level there
is a large body expected to prove payable when stoped
out.

Hydraulic sluicing will have a thorough trial in
Victoria, this year. Several co.’s have a big area of splen-
did country, near the Ovens River, Bright. M.L. engi-
neers and machinery in vogue.

M'Coll (Vic.) asks significantly in Parliament
whether a large consignment of fusel oil passed through
the Custom-house in December, and to whom it was con-
signed.
* * * *
Maryborough’s (Vic.) chief quartz show is the
Sharp and Barry Amalgamated, working on the Leviathan
reef. Co. last year milled 7734 tons for 3274£0z. and paid
£3750 in divs. Now driving for the reef at a lower level
(360 ft.), and expects to cut it 40ft. in, a result upon which
hinges the development of several other claims on the
line. Since ’9O the stone taken from the two amalgams
have averaged 14dwts.
* * * *
Bendigo has yielded 440 tons gold in 44 years.
An opening for a figure crank to work out how many tons
of stone broken for the aforesaid results.
* * * ♦
Thirty years since the first Vic. bore for alluvial
was put down on the old Albion Co.’s ground, Sebastopol.
That was the origin of the now famous Band and Albion
mine.
* * * *
A vegetable Ohow in a Vic. mining township
had a big boulder in his cabbage garden, and hired a
drayman to shift it the other day. While thus engaged,
the white barbarian noticed gold in the stone which,
when put through at a neighboring battery, gave lioz.
Then a claim, “ the Chinaman’s Luck,” was pegged out in
the middle of the chagrined Chow’s asparagus-bed.
* » * •
A mining man at Sheep station (Vic.) writes
The Bulletin : “ The reports you see from the rush here
read well in the matter of returns, but, as The Bulletin
puts it, from 10 to 20oz. to the ton is very fine—when you
get the ton. Most of the best crushings, well as they
look on paper, have not paid wages for the getting of the
stone, and in most cases the gold has vanished after a
few tons of stuff have been taken out. 20 tons is a good
patch. 1 doubt if the gold raised since the rush has paid
tucker-bills. Still, I must say that the few claims in the
district that are good are working large and seemingly
permanent bodies.” The latest from the district is the
alleged discovery of a 7ft. reef looking like 20 or 30oz.
♦ » * *
The pumping-engine just made for the Duke
United, Maryborough, has the biggest lift capacity in Vic.,
vi 2., 157,980 gallons per hour.
* * * *
John Hotson, the “ hand-selled ” mutton-man,
and hero of a wicked Melb. joke, reports an increase for
1895, of about 25 per cent, on his frozen sheep shipments
of the previous year. Some 204,000 carcases passed
through the Newport freezing works, and an advance on
this substantial output is predicted for ’96. Hotson’s
report is a calm, businesslike production, which makes
the barest reference to his recent visit to England, and
says nothing at all about those flights of mutton oratory
chronicled by the cable.
* * * »
Charters Towers ’95 yield, 251,5770 z. from
249,620 tons. This is about 12,0000 z. less than ’94, which
was the record year. It is, however, greater than the
output from the whole of W.A. Divs., £248,000; calls,
£92,815. The Brilliant and St. George headed the div.
list with £57,600, followed closely by the Brilliant and
Mills United, the last-named producing the most gold,
viz., 37,8430 z. The progress of the Towers’ deepest show,
Brilliant Extended, will be closely watched during 96.
Remarks Alan Bright's Circular : “ The mine has an
enormous quantity of stone in sight.”
* * * *
Things have been lively last year (’95) on the
Etheridge and Woolgar. The great trouble has been want
of water. The Upper Percy has dwindled to nothing,
while Gilberton has attracted a mining party from the
Towers. Warden Haldane calls the Etheridge the poor
man’s Eldorado, and points with pride to the miners who
depart for other fields, and nearly always return to the
old place.
* * * *
Explorer Lindsay predicts a Northern Territory
gold-boom. Another Kimberley ?
* * * *
Apropos the alleged salting of the Angipena
(S. A.) mine. Salting in Australia is so common and prose-
cutions for it so rare that it has been regarded as one of
the customs of the country. The fact that anyone in
Australia should be hauled up on a charge of “ salting ”
really surprises the large and experienced brigade who
have practised it with impunity and success. To salt a
gold-mine is as easy as falling off a log; to convict a
“ salter ” (unless he is as big a fool as he is a knave) is the
hardest thing on earth. It was once the practice to
employ professional “salters,” some of whom were real
artists in their business, in which they took much pride.
But a black sheep, turned up in a man who received lOOoz.
of gold to salt a crushing. He was engaged because of his
known salting accomplishments, but the clean-up showed
beyond a doubt that he had stuck to the gold and the
mine was not sold. When accused of the theft he proudly
struck his chest and said: “How dare you say I was
employed to salt your mine ? ” He issued a writ and got
lots of damages for slander. His conduct administered a
death-blow to the profession, and since then the honest
miner has done his own salting with a large amount of
success.
Four miners held the claim in which they
worked. The vein looked like getting poorer, and each,
unknown to the other, determined to get out, and to so
salt the next crushing as to cause the local mugs who
held the other half of the claim to buy him out. Each,
of course, deducted the gold he put in from the total, and
even then the yield was so good that each, again unknown .
to his mates, decided to buy in instead of selling out, and
they then had the whole claim. The explanation came
with the next crushing.
A good way to salt a stream-tin mine is to run a
lot of good tin-wash over a flat, and offer for sale when
the grass has grown well over it. The greatest diamond-
swindle of the age was worked in this way in America.
There is nothing easier than to salt a mine. “ Have you
ever salted a mine?” once asked the writer of an
innocent-looking digger in N.S. W. “ Salted a mine. Have
I ever salted 40 mines ? ” was his reply. With so many
lovely mining experts in W.A., some rare salting inci-
dents must have occurred. The Bulletin invites compe-
tition in salting yarns, and offers half a trunkful of
mining-scrip as first prize.
* * * *

SPORTING NOTIONS
Harry Musgrove may find his post as manager
of the ’96 Australian team no sinecure. He will often
have to travel day and night, and to drink with at least
CO people, make about 30 speeches daily, and every morn-
ing at 3 o’clock make the round of the pubs, and gather
up the drunks of the team, and on his return be careful
not to trip over the inebriates who have accumulated on
the pub. mats during his absence. Musgrove, as manager
of an opera co., has had experience of the eccentri-
cities of the ladies with their golden hair a-hanging down
their backs, but he will find an Australian cricket-team
on the loose in England slightly different. For instance,
there is one man, who may possibly go this time, who has
a standing theory that he cannot make a score unless he
has had six rums, several long-beers, and numerous
whiskies the night before; and unless Musgrove exercises
great diplomacy that batsman will go through a window
if interfered with in his programme. The great qualities
required in a manager are discretion, a colossal thirst, and
the power to settle brawls arising from mixed liquors and
jealousy ; but a man who has managed an opera co., and
is still alive, deserves respect, anyway.
Many prominent patrons of the sport, altogether
outside the cricket-ring, don’t view the contemplated
’Ome trip in a very favorable light, for obvious reasons.
The batsmen may be trusted to shape well, but the bowl-
ing promises to be insufferably weak ; so much so, in fact,
that it will probably be treated with contempt. Under
the circumstances, a win in any of the test-matches seems
rather improbable. A few fast, rousing stump-scatterers
are required, but the cultivation of a lightning-delivery is
a lost art. Perhaps it’s just as well, for the wicket-
keeper’s sake.
* * * •
Graham beat Lyons for the last Australian
Eleven average by a fraction. Up to a certain time in
England Graham had not made a duck. But one match
the ball struck his pads and went for four. Leg-byes,
said the umpire. But at lunch Graham said to Grace :
“ Doctor, I touched that ball with my bat.” “ Allright,”
said Grace, “ I’ll have the mistake altered.” So the score
of four was given Graham. “It will get you the average,”
remarked Coningham. So it did.
* * * •
Blackham has gone with the Aus. Eleven to
England every trip since ’7B-eight visits in all. A. Ban-
nerman and Boyle have been six times, Bonnor and
Spofforth five times. Geo. Giffen has had four trips,
Turner three.
* * * *
Of Aus. Elevens that have visited England, the
most successful was Murdoch’s ’B2 team, which won 23
matches, lost 4, and drew 11. The least successful was
Murdoch’s ’9O team, which won 13, lost 16, and drew 9.
M'Donnell’s ’BB team played most matches, 40; Blackham’s
’93 team the least, 36. Altogether Aus. Elevens have
played 297 matches in England, won 139, lost 55, drawn 88.
The ’B5-6 team drew 22 matches out of 39.
* * * »
The highest score ever made in England by
an Australian Eleven man against English players was
Murdoch’s 286, in ’B2 ; he made 211 in ’B4. Massie scored
206 in ’B2, Graham 219 in ’93, and Lyons 191 in ’93. The
’7B team had only one individual century score - C. Ban-
nerman’s 133 ; the ’BO team one also, Murdoch’s 153. The
’B2 team recorded five individual centuries and two double
centuries ; the ’B4 team put up four individual centuries
and one 200 ; the 'B5 team got five individual centuries,
the ’BB three, the ’9O two, the ’93 eleven and one 200.

• * * *
Guess the brilliant Australian cricketer who,
when a certain Aus. Eleven was in England, would come
to the manager and, with pain-drawn, woe-begone face,
say : “ Oh-h-h 1 dreadful pain ! may I go and see a
doctor?” The manager would consent. Then the bril-
liant one would crawl away to the nearest hotel, and suck
down the first pint without pausing for breath.
* * * •
Australian Elevens receive in England 50 per
cent, of .the gate ; that is, of the shillings and sixpences—
they don’t get a copper of-the additional charges for
grand-stands, &c., not even when ss. a-piece is being paid
for chairs. N.S.W. Wheelman’s League should wear this
fact in its hat. Also, the fact that Australian-Eleven
attractions have pulled English counties out of debt.
* * * •
Blackham’s thumb is still so tender that a fillip
with a finger-nail striking it would cause the blood to
spurt. Turner’s right hand forefinger is in much the
same bad state, and alone, so many cricket savants say,
quite debars him from the Aus. Eleven.
* * * *
How long is Marylebone Club to dictate cricket
law to all the players of the earth ? For 100 years it has
ladled out whatever rules of the game its council decided
upon ; most of them improving, some of them childish
and illogical. Yet not one English county club has raised
its voice in protest, apparently regarding the Marylebone
club as a fetish like Queen Victoria or Westminster Abbey.
Neither has Australia so far claimed to have its say in the
manufacture of the game it plays; though 20 years have
elapsed since its teams have fairly held their own with the
best that England can produce. The Australian game of
football is subject to a thorough overhauling at stated
intervals—not by the small Melb. coterie who fathered it,
but by a council including representatives of all the pro-
vinces, even of those where the game is little played.
Why not so with cricket ? Surely Australia can claim a
voice in the making of the game it plays so much—and so
well.
* * * *
Percy M ‘Donnell is now being freely tipped in
Melb. as captain of the Australian team. Harry Boyle,
who has been watching him closely all the season, says he
was never in better form, and with Garrett unavailable
there is no man better qualified to lead a team. But
there seems to be a reason or two why M'Donnell should
not lead a representative Australian team.
* * * *
M‘G. : Somebody objects, in a recent Bulletin,
to the legs of the racing cyclist. Granted that Zimmer-
man possesses a most ugly pair of supports, and that
Martin’s are masses of shapeless muscle, what about the
other men in the first flight ? Parsons and Harris might
each stand for sculptors’ models ; they are short, but sym-
metrical. Porta’s legs are shapely, but, owing to his
habit of letting his socks fall into his shoes, do not appear
so. Pither, Kirk, Carpenter, Iredale, Walker, and a host
of others are men of almost perfect nether development.
The cyclist—the racing cyclist—has, in nine cases out of
ten, a well-developed leg. The very nature of his sport
renders such almost compulsory : though, of course, a
good man with spindle-shanks is occasionally met—for
instance, Middleton, who has calves on his heart instead
of his legs, and wins his races thereby.
* * * *
Apropos the debate as to who was the first
bicycle rider in Australia, Mr. Percy Clay, West Mait-
land’s telegraph master, declares that those who fix the
years 1868 and 1869 as the date of the introduction of the
machine into Victoria have not, metaphorically speaking,
a leg to stand on. As far back as 1866, Mr. Olay rode a
bike from Warrnambool to Famham Park— returning (tor
several reasons) with his iron steed in a potato-cart.
* * * *
All the spoils of Zimmerman’s cycling victories
have gone down the Nimrod-Zimmy Company’s sink, it is
said.
* * » *
Prominent Melb. bikists are now “riding the
high horse,” in imitation of Martin and Porta. Zim.
says it’s a mistake. “Use a high gear and you may
become a successful plugger. A low gear is required for
brilliancy in racing.”
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —I notice that cash-amateur-
cyclists’ stock is unsaleable in your paper. Well, the
cash-amateur may be a bit of a rogue now and then, but
the lily-white amateur never rises above the fool. I was
a lily-white amateur, spent £5OO in cycles, trainer s ex-
penses, &c., and I raced for cups and medals. I have
many of these gimcracks now—£lo cups, £2O ones, £5
ones, &c., worth respectively 55., 10s., and 2s. 6d ; the
&c. lot is worth nothing. These represent my scoop for
that £5OO, my time and energies. I’ll give them to you,
if you like—they’ll stand as a concrete explanation of why
the amateurs race for cash. —Key.
» * * »
Young bikers, as a rule, seem to have faith m a
glorious resurrection of Zimmerman within a few months.
The Bulletin reckons him a “goner” beyond recovery of
first-class form. He’s six-and-twenty, anyway, and
cycling glory up to now has been confined to small-headed
boys of ill-balanced physique, who break up under the
strain of love and matrimony. The present style of bike
champion is not likely to endure long at anything, nor is
it very probable that'the class will improve. Men with
good chests, necks, and brain-power might last into the
thirties, but when a certain medical theory has been
fairly proved, no brainy individual will dream of being a
racing cyclist. Also, the girls will cease to find any
attraction in the nubbly-legged heroes with the tight-
fitting pants.
» * * *
Zim. ’s last words in Australia on cycling were
that the game in N.S.W. will be a bigger success than
ever; that the Vic’s, by mismanagement, will spoil things;
that Parsons is as good as Harris ; that Martin is a clever
rider on a fast track ; that he himself was beaten by un-
suitable climate, by business worry, and by bad light and
night racing.
When Zimmerman first came to Australia, The
Bulletin pointed out that circumstances over which Zim.
had no control would stop him striking form. His Aus-
tralian tour - his honeymoon tour—was his first failure.
It was the first time he tried to make his cycle carry two.
* * * *
The immediate effect of The Bulletin’s com-
ments on Melb. Bicycle Club management was to remind
that money-making concern of its former compromise
with creditors—some of whom, by the wajq stood out for
their pound of flesh and were paid in full. Those who
had accepted 7s. in the £ two or three years ago received
the balance of their abandoned claims “as a mark of the
M.B.C.’s gratitude, as well as of its integrity” (vide the
admiring A nous), arid last Thursday they all partook of a
banquet at the club-rooms, in celebration of this joyful
event.
* * * *
The Warrnambool bike races furnished fine
sport for any local “mugs” who wagered on the heats.
Three international scratch events were bracketed
together, the prize-money being awarded on points—
five for each win, three for a second place, and one for a
third. Martin took the one-mile, just beating Harris,
with Pither third, and Porta last. Pither took the three-
miles, just beating Martin, with Porta and Harris close
behind. Then Martin captured the five-miles (and £2O
stake), 2ft. in front of Porta, with Harris third, and
Pither last this time. After which Martin, Pither, and
Porta came out for an invitation mile, and Porta, who
finished last for the previous race over the same course,
won by a length from Martin, in 7sec. longer time than
the Irish-Yank. had needed for his victorious journey. If
all this be genuine “form,” and The Bulletin doesn’t
question its honesty, bike racing among “ champions ” is
not worth intelligent discussion. The Bendigo folk came
to this way of thinking before their Xmas cycling sports
were finished.
* * * *
The League of Vic. Wheelmen will assert itself
with a two Saturdays race-meeting at the M.C.O. Ground
—Feb. Ist. and Bth. The management advertises a
liberal programme, all things considered. £l3O is given
to the principal handicap, a two-mile event. The ten
mile and five mile scratch races are worth £4O apiece,
including a gold medal (the medal to be charged by the
ounce, presumably), and some minor handicaps bring the
total amount of prize-money up to £3OO. Also, the
scratch races carry champion titles with them, which
sounds satisfactory, since the public mind has been getting
rather mixed as regards champions. Porta, Martin,
Harris, Pither and Co. will fight their battles some more.
These cycling rivals are indefatigable.
* * * *
Why don’t the police keep an eye on the scales
of the cycling shops? A Melb. man lately ordered a
machine not to exceed 251 b. weight, and was supplied
with a jigger which, when slung on the agent’s spring
balance, went just 241 b. The bargain was closed, the
deposit paid, and the cyclist rode off—only to find, three
weeks later, on placing it upon a platform scales, that it
brought down 29Jlb.
* * * *
The racing cyclist will soon have another avenue
open to him by the introduction of an invention which,
attached to the gear of an ordinary bicycle, reduces the
distance covered nearly a thousandfold. For instance,
anyone who wants a 10-mile spin on a wet night, only
needs to fix the new mechanism on his machine and ride
50ft. along his back verandah. To cover this distance
will take the average man 37 minutes, and if he
sprints the last 6 in. he will take another hour to re-
cover. But the enormous possibility that opens is the race
on the stage. Imagine what a draw a 5-mile match between
Zimmerman, Harris, and Parsons would be if conducted
on the stage of, say, Melb. Opera House. How the
triplet would scorch for the first 4ft., as the audience
would see it, and how the final inch would be battled for
by the three of them, head to head for a single five
minutes!
* * * *
Dear Bulletin,—l note that Norman Selfe
claims to have won the first bicycle race at Sydney. He
did ride a race on a bicycle, but against 3 and 4-wheel
velocipedes. Did you ever see a sulky race a tip-dray ?
The first race between bicycles was won by Wm. Good-
ridge, at the Albert Ground, about 1870. Selfe was a
competitor, and was beaten easily.—R.J.C.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —You claim the ped. as the
most symmetrically-built athlete, dismissing the pug as
being small below and the cyclist as small above the
waist. Now, take the man who is well developed both
above and below, the oarsman, and you have, I think a
far better man than either ped., cyclist, or pug. Rowing
as everybody isn’t aware, needs more body-work than
anything else, and as much leg-work as cycling ; conse-
quently, the rower is well developed all over. He misses
nothing in his exercise. It is all very well to point out
Larry Marsh as well built. He is ; but he is the exception
not the rule. The average ped. hasn’t any arms to speak
of, whereas every oarsmen anywhere near the front
rank has a strong, well-proportioned frame. The runner
is no doubt evenly built, what there is of him—so prob-
ably, were the Liliputians. But for producing a big
well-built man, commend me to the outrigger Just look
at some of them—Beach, Searle, Hanlan, Rush, and anv
other good man you like, all real manly men—men you
find yourself looking after and admiring in the street
Walter S.

got themselves top-hats and long coats. But, alas, the

f * * * *
The rumble of Turf reform is now heard in
Melb , but there are indications that the essentially con-
servative V.R.C. committee will heap obstacles in the way
on Feb. 10. The committee-men would rather have the
kudos attached to introducing desirable reforms themselves,
but it never occurred to them to take the lead. Also,
their amendments would probably have assumed a
much milder shape than those now tabled by member
Gaunson and others, so nothing but strong opposition is
likely to be offered to anything so awfully drastic and
positively offensive, doncherknow.
Why the Metropolitan Club controllers haven’t
made some effort at turf purification long ere now is a
puzzle. Sooner or later they’ll have to do so, or the
public will be pulling out its handkerchief at the very
mention of the word “ horse.” In these days of squared
juries the papers can’t afford to libel individual jockeys
or stewards every day—yet that seems the only way of
drawing attention to the huge Australian cesspit,-mis-
called the turf.
Stewards prefer discussing protests, cronk run-
ning, &c., in a comfortable tea-party way; the presence
of the press would be irksome and irritating. But the
public would be more satisfied if they knew more about
these enquiries. That stewards make small and swell
bets is notorious, and the assertion that cash doesn’t
influence them is bunkum. Yet decisions against weight
of evidence wouldn’t be likely in the presence of the
press, unless the latter happened to have its little bit on
as well as the stewards.
* * * •
At a primitive M.L. fixture once a local owner
protested against a visiting winner. The stewards’ room
also served as accident, press, drinking and smoking
apartment. So the scribes were handed a bottle and told
to take no notice. The chair : “ I propose the protest be
upheld. There’s something in it.” Someone objected.
“ What! Do you know the lodger of the protest owes me
a tenner, your -brother over there a fiver, besides £6 to
the club for entries ? And how the ’ell do you think we’re
going to get it if we don’t give him the stake ? The club
can’t afford bad debts. Those in favor say ‘ aye ’ —the
ayes have it.”

For the sake of the malodorous Australian turf,
it is to be hoped that lawyer O’Halloran will carry out his
threat of suing a Melb. weekly paper for libel. O’Halloran
seems bound to carry out his threat. He is in the legal
trade, and can get his action done comparatively on the
cheap. Tiik Bulletin hopes that Providence will fight on
the side of truth, whichever that may be, and not neces-
sarily with the biggest battalions of witnesses.
The little weekly in question ventured to men-
tion that Mr. C. D. O’Halloran, otherwise “0. Denis,”
was called before the Mentone stewards lately to
explain the running of his filly, Dowry. A good
subject for mention, inasmuch as the precious Argus
and Age had been strangely silent on the point,
notwithstanding the sporting interest which is naturally
taken ■in Mr. O’Halloran. He is the new VA T C
steward, appointed in place of Mr. Anderson, who’ ten-
dered his resignation after “J. B. Clark” had made
things unpleasant for him re the notorious Lardy-Dardy
case, so that the fierce light that beats upon a throne was
fairly entitled to illuminate the dealings of this turf
potentate. Why didn’t the “ respectable ” daily news-
papers call attention to Dowry’s allegedly unsatisfactory
performance by stating that the filly’s owner had been
requested to account for it, and had disarmed suspicion
with his explanation? Also, why wasn’t the explanation
published ?
Thus far, then, the weekly journalfin question
appears to have done Mr. O’Halloran no wrong He ought
to be and doubtless is, sorry that the daily newspapers
held their leaden tongues concerning Dowry’s running at
Mentone, and the innocent complexion he (the owner!
was at once able to put upon it. Dowry’s running seems
to have excited suspicion, but Mr. O’Halloran proved to
the Mentone stewards that said suspicion was a libel on
his honor. Anyhow, his explanation was accepted The
newspaper appears to have angered Mr. O’Halloran with
the tone of its comments on the Mentone incident
The offending journal, instead of confining itself to
a oare narrative of the gentleman’s bad luck in being con-
nected with an evil-disposed filly, preferred to treat blm
as a lucky man for having been able to so easily satisfy
the Mentone stewards. Therein, of course, lav libel for
which he threatens legal vengeance. Tiie Bulletin cor-
dially advises him to go in and win if he can. The turf
the legal profession the V.A.T.C., and the Sup-press are
ahke interested m his success. On the other hand, the
public will be glad to hear all that the other side can say
in open court. *

A New Hush.
With one exception, such an influx of people, or
“a rush,” never has taken place to equal that to
West Australia at the present time. Fresh
arrivals to the West number 3000 a week, things
are at present booming, and although a few press-
men are denouncing Westralia as a bogus field,
their opposition is mostly caused by disappoint-
ment at not being amongst the fortunate. Cer-
tain% rich gold has been discovered there, some
people have made fortunes easily, and the precious
metal again proves what a powerful magnet it is.
The exception referred to above is in connec-
tion with a rush taking place all over Australasia.
Watch any tobacconist shop and you will notice
it. Frossard’s Cavour Cigars, in packets of eight
for Is., are a delicious, mild and fragrant smoke,
and so popular that immediately a shipment
arrives they are at once bought up. Until lately
the supply has not nearly equalled the demand,
but the manufacturers, having increased their
premises and employes, and obtained contracts for
a splendid crop of leaf, hope to keep the market
regularly supplied. Frossard’s Cavour Cigars, in
packets of eight for Is., have caused the biggest
rush ever seen in the colonies, and are spoken of
throughout the world as the most marvellous weed
of the day.*
Sheridan’s hanging was on the anniver-
sary of the Mount Rennie slaughter.
Searl and Sons, the well-known Sydney
florists, supply bouquets, wreaths and decorations
in large or small lots to suit purchasers ; and sell
seeds or cuttings of nearly every flower that
bloometh and a great many trees and vegetables
that don’t bloom. Their prices are moderate and
their taste is excellent.*
England’s Famous Coloniser
and Irrigation Expert.
Probably few men who commenced at the bottom
of the ladder have become more widely and favour-
ably known or more successful than Mr. A. F.
Spawn, of 122, Pall Mall, London, for some years
a resident of Melbourne. That he is a man of
great resource cannot be doubted when we read
that he was the original inventor of the Hand
Grenade Fire Extinguisher, also many other in-
ventions of minor importance, and recently per-
fected a scientific hot air apparatus for curing and
preserving fruits and vegetables on a large,
practical, and economical scale, which is being
adopted, not only in England, but in Holland,
Australia, and America. Mr. Spawn was ap-
pointed by a Syndicate some years ago to investi-
gate the process of growing and preserving fruits
in Australia, and while there started a well-organ-
ised and successful irrigation colony in that
country. In fact, as a public lecturer and adviser
on colonisation and irrigation he probably stands
to-day unrivalled. Business has brought him in
contact with people of skill in every part of the
globe, and naturally he has been through many
adventures, some pleasing and others dangerous ;
but the most astonishing adventure he recently
experienced right here in London, and imme-
diately after his return from an extended tour of
immigration investigation on the Pacific Coast of
America. Mr. Spawn’s own words explain clearly
how he was attacked, and how he escaped. “I
had always been uncommonly strong and healthy,
and have not known a day’s illness during my
past ten years’ travels and adventures, and was
always able to scoff at disease, but a few weeks
ago, on leaving my office at Pall Mall, I was over-
come with giddiness, and this recurred frequently
during the next few days ; I began to notice a
bitter taste in my mouth, and my tongue seemed
coated and burning ; my appetite left me—could
not enjoy the greatest delicacy, everything I ate
seemed to distress me, and caused spasmodic pains
in the pit of my stomach, and flatulency; my
complexion turned sallow, and the customary
office duties required more exertion than I could
seemingly command. My ailment also seemed to
make me nervous, and tended to cause melancholy
and gloomy feelings. I tried chemists’ and doc-
tors’ medicines, and kept growing worse.
“ I had always derided medicines outside the
regular channels, and although I had heard
Warner’s Safe Cure favourably spoken of for liver
diseases, not only in England, but Australia and
America, I never thought of using it; but for-
tunately a friend who knew of my suffering
succeeded in overcoming my prejudice, and I
decided to try it. Almost from the first dose the
disagreeable symptoms began to lessen in severity.
In a few days I was able to enjoy light food, and
after using four bottles all the above described
symptoms had entirely disappeared. I know now
what to do if my liver gets out of order, or if there
are signs of an approaching bilious attack, and
oertainly can recommend this medicine to others
with candor and confidence.” There can be no
question as to the soundness of the views expressed
dr advice given by a gentleman of Mr. Spawn’s
standing, and your experience will be just as satis-
factory if you are guided thereby.*

Messrs. Myers and Solomon, who have
traded in Sydney for nearly fbrty years as
general importers and commission merchants,
have resumed business at 42 Market - street,
Sydney. The new firm starts quite free and
unencumbered from any liabilities connected
with the old establishment. Messrs. Myers
and Solomon were the original founders of
the business, and their very long experience
makes them eminently fitted to carry on the busi-
ness to the best advantage. The firm recom-
mences business just when most of the import
duties are removed, and thus gets a considerable
start of competitors who have large duty-paid
stocks on hand.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

* * * *
Y.R.C. committee regards as impracticable the
proposition that scratchings shouldn’t be allowed after
noon on the day previous to the races, unless a valid
excuse is tendered. If applied to all manner of trum-
pery meetings, of which the public take little notice, that
might be so ; but it’s a workable scheme so far as big han-
dicaps are concerned. Late scratchings through spite
against sweep-ticket-holders, or failure to work a good
stable com. at long odds, or because ‘ 1 I’m always un-
lucky on that course,” have occurred often enough.
* * * *
A special meeting of the Y.R.C. has been called
to deal with a proposed alteration in the rules, by which
owners must scratch their horses by noon on race-day,
except in special cases. Under the present style, horses,
which it is never intended to start, are brought down to
the course for double-event purposes. Backers see them
on the course and take them in doubles, only to And them
scratched just before the race.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — The obvious reason for a
sporting tipster’s invariable failure to tip a winner, or get
near to so doing, is his want of brains. That is pre-
suming he knows something of the subject and tries his
best to hit the mark. After a tipster has collected a cer-
tain amount of possible truth, derived from facts, sug-
gestions, hints, indirect evidence, or reasonable con-
clusions founded on experience, concerning certain horses
likely to start for a certain race, he has then to weigh
each bit of “ information ”in his mind. He must con-
sider, not only the horses, but the men connected with
them, and the probabilities of some men having less, or
more, good reason for their confidence than other men.
You say, “The only way to get reliable tips is to be
Platonic friend to the best girls of leading jockeys.”
Given such a Platonic friend, and supposing that he knew
the latest ideas of every stable that fancied its chance
the well-informed individual’s knowledge is dangerous
unless he can bring a wise discrimination to bear upon his
estimate of the conflicting fancies. He can do no more.
He can’t foresee good or bad luck. These remarks apply
only to big races in which “goers” are many, not to
pettifogging events that are, to a large extent, arranged
21 hours or 24 minutes before the start. To discuss them
seriously in a weekly newspaper is absurd.—Yours, Stable
Cat.

The racers in W.A. at present seem very poor.

* * * *
Mr. S. Galvayne, or whoever was responsible
for the recent shipment of Australian horses to this
market (writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent),
simply invited disaster by making a bad selection. A
more fiddle-headed, inferior-looking lot it would be hard
to get together, and though they were said to be quiet in
saddle and harness no one attempted either to back or to
drive them. That low prices were realised cannot be
wondered at. For England nothing but the best of every
kind must be sent—nothing else will do. Only the best
will sell.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —“ Hop ” makes seasonable
comment upon the phenomenon of tea-drill by old gentle-
men of the bowling temperament. Tea is obviously more
conducive to good play than three-star. I have noticed,
at many bowling-matches, that the players who stump
fattily away in occasional twos and threes are the ones
who lose. Scores of, say, 21 —20 generally mean equal
drinking capacities. Scores of, say, 31—15 mostly imply
that the losing side was inordinately drinky. But I have
never seen a rink of tea-drinking bowlers except in M.L.
prohibition districts, where adipose “ sports ” must needs
wait until they get home to their surreptitious cellars.
Under prohibition bowls would be a lost art. —Thorold W.

* * * *
Miller’s monthly Racing Chronicle is not, thus
far, the financial success anticipated by the publisher. It
must be either that Miller over-estimated the probable
number of subscribers, or else that the handy little shilling
volume is not sufficiently well-known to race-goers. Every-
body who has discovered the value of the Chronicle ought
to assist it along towards a paying circulation.
* * * *
At the recent Christchurch (M.L.) athletic
champion meeting, instead of competitors being put back
a yard for breaking, it was notified that repeated break-
ing would result in disqualification. The self-control
shown by the runners was most wonderful.
* * * *
The standards fixed at the Aust. A. A. champion
meeting in M.L. were those adopted by the Eng. Asso.,
and stamp the men who attained them as first-class
amateurs anywhere. Kingsley, of Wellington, reached
the mark (10ft.) in the pole jump, and so did Martin, of
Auckland, in the 120yds. hurdles (16 2-ssecs.). In hammer
throwing (100 ft.) Martin (104 ft. sin) and Brown (100 ft.
lin.) secured badges. Creamer, of Auckland, broke the
Australasian 3-mile walk record, doing the distance in
22min. 51 2-ssecs. Bennett, the Dunedin youngster, ran
a mile in 4min. 29 3-ssecs., going clean away from N.S.W.
Corner. Low, another Dunedinite, a great sprinter, beat
the standard (52sec.) by 2-ssec. in the 440yds. flat. Cart-
wright (Vic.) beat the standard (10 3-sth) by l-sth in the
100yds. These were on the first day. T. Roberts, of
Auckland, who hurdled for M. L. at the meeting, is the brave
man who behaved so nobly at the Wairarapa wreck. At
his own province’s recent annual champion meeting, he
won the hurdles in great - style, and he holds the world s
record time for 220yds. hurdles, but at the big Christ-
church meeting he was unable to do even a moderate per-
formance. Excepting Cartwright and Doyle (high-
jumper), the Australian amateur athletes visiting M.L.
were disappointing. t
Sydney University beat Melb. ditto by nine
wickets. Sydney put up 336 ; Melb. 189 and 176.
The Barker-bashed Placke left Sydney on Mon-
day by the Monowai for ’Murka. They say that Placke
was absolutely idolised by a well-to-do Sydney woman,
and that he could have lived on love had he chosen.
* * * *
•.The first of the Australasian swimming chain-
pionships came off on 15th inst. at Sydney Natatorium.
Arrangements were so mixed that the bath might have
been whisky instead of water. The automatic winner-
indicating machine saw double on one occasion it re-
corded that Nos. 1,2, 3,4, 5 and 6 had swum a dead heat.
And only three men were in the race ; and they didn’t
swim a dead-heat either.
* * * *
Gormly put up an Australian record of 6 min.
8 sec. in the recent 440 yards Australasian swimming
Championship, which is 15 sec. worse than the English
record ; and P. Cavill put up 13 min. 27 4-sth sec. in the
half-mile ; Tyers’ (Eng.) time is 13 min. 20 sec. Peate, a
N.S.W. fat man, did a world’s record plunge of 66 ft. 10 in.
—but he stayed under water longer than the usual minute
allowance.
* * * *
The Cavill family and following, its uncles, its
brothers, its sisters, its cousins, and its aunts, was in
uproarious evidence at Windsor swimming carnival, the
men bellowing and the ladies screeching to their repre-
sentative. At intervals, too, in the mile race, they
flopped into the water and swam beside their man,
encouraging him. Had not Gormly mistaken the course
he might have won instead of having to give up. As it
was, that fine swimmer, P. Cavill, seemed to have had a
lucky win. But by what process does the estimable
Cavill family continue to swim as amateurs ? There is a
general impression that the Cavills are out-and-out pro-
fessionals, who live by the water, in the water, on the
water, for the water.
* * * *
The Maoriland swimmers will have put in their
2600 miles journey (to Sydney and back) for nothing.
Helhngs (N.S.W.) won the 220-yards Championship;
Gormly (N.S.W.), the 440; P. Cavill (N.S.W.), the half-
mile and mile. And the only Maorilander placed in these
big events was Stratton, who got third in the mile through
Gormly giving up.
* * * *
Australian pugs, are flocking to Johannesburg,
the best “gate” locality in the world. M’Gowan,
Doherty, and Duggan are now plugging for cash there.
M‘Gowan left Melbourne to meet Jim Murphy, the erst
Australian; Doherty to engage with Heffernan, and
Duggan to take on Tut Ryan. When the mail left it was
doubtful if the police would permit the exhibitions after
the Goddard-Ryan show, which was the outcome of bad
blood, and generated into a slugging bout. However,
little obstacles of this kind are easily overcome in
Johannesburg.
* * * *
“ Denver ” Smith has challenged the victor of
the Fitzsimmons-Maher fight. According to him, the
idea of the pair battling for the championship is simply
ridiculous. When Goddard challenged Corbett the latter
didn’t accept, and so forfeited the title. That being so,
he couldn’t present to Maher what he didn’t own. Smith,
himself, beat Goddard, and Goddard beat Maher in three
rounds, Fitzsimmons following up by knocking Maher out
in 12 at New Orleans. So Smith says Fitz. and he should
battle for the belt. Anyhow, he’ll take on any man in
the world, Yank or Englisher.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — Re the sheep-killing record. A
friend of mine who was there, and is a good, old-fashioned
reliable liar, states that he saw one M‘Lean, a N.S. Welsh-
man, kill and dress 250 sheep in eight hours at Menindie
works in Oct., 1893.—E. M. Watts, Adelaide.
♦ * * *
Rae, of the Balmain Working Men’s Club, is
said to be the coming champion sculler. Lambert, of the
Nepean, the only man whom Bubear some time ago barred
in a skiting challenge to the world, has finally retired.
The knowing ones ominously shake their heads at the
probability of Stanbury’s return to his old form.
* * * *
A certain Melb. trainer owns to a strange super-
stition. The day his prads are “ having a try ” he con-
sumes more cigars than usual. If any go out his luck
departs also, and he says he might as well scratch. One
bookie’s mascotte is a cat. Unless he fondles and
feeds it regularly before leaving home he has no luck.
Sometimes puss is absent on business of her own, and
hook-nose “falls in” sure. So the cook has a standing
order to secure the feline on race-days, and attach her to
the premises with a yard of rope.
* * * *
The bicycle is a revolutionist ; it overturns one
thing after another with the ease of a young Samson.
Horses have to go, not on the streets, but from them.
Farmers find their markets for oats cut down ; new trades
are created and huge factories built; new interpretations
of the law have to be made ; ladies alter fashions that
have resisted reform for a generation ; modes of recreation
are modified ; language receives additions ; the values of
real estate are upset; new diseases are noted ; roads are
reconstructed ; and now the accident insurance companies
are rattled. They find that their old risks will not do.
They have discovered that injuries from cycle accidents
are more .serious than the average, and that riding exposes
a greater number to risk than formerly.—Amer.
P. P. Morton, the Sydney mercer and
outfitter who does business in Pitt-street, at the
corner of Sydney Strand arcade, is the man to con-
sult regarding purple and fine linen and etceteras.
His new shipment of stylish summer hats is worth
attention.*
TheA.U.S.N. Co’s Wodonga made another
record trip from Brisbane to Sydney the other day,
and the Australian coastal trade may be felicitated
on its possession of such a fine passenger steamer.
The co’s other newest boats, Arawatta and
Aramac, are quite as comfortable and nearly as
speedy. *
The two most badly-bruised men in Mel-
bourne just now are a pair of private detectives
who were out collecting evidence in a divorce-case.
Accompanied by the jealous wife, they burst in
the door of a suburban cottage late at night in the
hope of finding something of a compromising
character, and yelled “fire ” to bring the old man
out. He came out, but only to bowl over the two
intruders and prance upon them, and when he had
sated his craving for gore, he chased one of them
off the premises and tied up the other —who had
lost most of his clothes and a lot of blood in
the course of the unpleasantness —with a rope till
the police arrived. Then he gave the shattered
mass into custody for being on the premises with-
out lawful excuse, and went back to bed.
“Shark! Shark!”
A solicitor charged with stealing £1461 of trust moneys
said “he was sorry to say he had received the money,
and had mixed it up with his money, and could not find
it.” He pleaded in excuse, however, that the money had
come to hand in instalments, and he was saving it to give
to his client all together, as the client would then “be
more likely to make some good use of it.” However,
more than half would be returned out of the bankruptcy
proceedings. A dialogue between the new solicitor and
his client drifts in just here :
Client :
O, give me, I pray you, the money you owe me—
I mean my own money, which dear uncle left;
You really must fork out, for how can you show
me
That, if you retain it, it wiM not be theft ?
Solicitor :
My dear sir, with pleasure ; but first satisfy me
That if I hand over your cash on demand
You’ll spend it discreetly ; for if you don’t—blime !
It goes to my private account —understand ?
Client :
But, sir, though my lawyer, you are not my
censor;
My money’s my own, too, to spend as I choose.
Solicitor :
Alas, ’tis my duty grave cause of offence or
Of scandal to shun —so I’ll stand in your shoes.
Yet could you but see how I’m torn by my scruples
As Meagher, too, was torn in the Waverley tram,
And Rofe, the grief-stricken . . . P’raps there
are loopholes . . .
I’ll see the Chief Justice to-morrow.
Client :
0, damn !
Solicitor :
Besides, your dear uncle has died by instalments—
At all events, drafts come to hand one by one ;
And, lest you be lured into vicious enthralments,
I’ll wait till the dribbling temptation is done.
You see, sir, my conscience—my honoured pro-
fession —
Client :
Great Emu ! this impudence passes belief !
Your conscience ! your honour ! —yes, when you’re
detected
And publicly nailed —like the penitent thief.
Solicitors nowadays, fee’d, it is true, will
Defend clients while they have money to spend
Against all antagonists. . . Good ! but pray who
will
Against their solicitors’ clients defend ?
Solicitor :
If those are your sentiments, sir, you must leave
me;
Your language is blasphemy—worse, I might
say . . .
Your money ? . . . H’m ! Yes ! . . . Well,
unless I deceive me,
You’ll see when my schedule is filed. , . , Oh,
good-day !

But What’s the Use.
A DARLING RIVER SKETCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
But what’s the use o’ writin’ “Bush ”
Though editors demand it—
For city folk, an’ farmin’ folk?
They cannot understand it.
They’re blind to what the bushman sees
The best with eyes shut tightest—
Out where the sun is hottest an’
The stars are most an’ brightest.
The crows at sunrise floppin’ round
Where some poor life has run down ;
The pair of emus trottin’ from
The lonely tank at sundown—
Their snaky heads well up, an’ eyes
Well out for man’s manoeuvres,
An’ feathers bobbin’ round behind
Like fringes round improvers.
The swagman trampm cross the plain —
Good Lord, there’s nothing sadder,
Except the dog that slopes behind
His master like a shadder ;
The turkey-tail to scare the flies,
The water-bag an’ billy ;
The nose-bag gettin’ cruel light,
The trav’ler gettin’ silly.
The plain that seems to jackeroos
Like gently-slopin’ rises,
The shrubs an’ tufts that’s miles away
But magnified in sizes ;
The track that seems a-risen up
Or else seems gently slopin’,
And just a hint of kangaroos
’Way out across the open.
The joy or hope the swagman feels
R ‘turnin’, after shearin’,
Or a'ter six months’ tramp Out Back,
He strikes the final clearin’.
His weary spirit breathes again,
His achin’ legs seem limber
When to the East across the plain
He spots the Darlin’ Timber !
But what’s the use o’ writin’ bush—
Tho’ editors demand it—-
For city folk an’ cockatoos ?
They do not understand it !
They’re blind to what the whaler sees
The best with eyes shut tightest—
Out where Australyer’s widest, an’
The stars are most ’n’ brightest. *
HENRY LAWSON.
The well-known Misfit Parlor of Mr.
Nathan Herman, established for 20 years, at 9
Wynyard Square, Sydney, is now attracting more
than the ordinary crowd of customers. One line
that is contributing specially to the growth of
business is the bale system. For £2, a whole bale
of men’s useful clothing, boots, &c., can be got.
These parcels are sent by rail or steamer, free of
charge, to any part of the province. There are bar-
gains to be had all through the establishment. A
half-chest of tea, weighing nearly 401 b., can be
purchased for £1 10s., and a 121 b. tin for 10s.
When a watch and chain—the timekeeper being
guaranteed —can be had for as small a sum as 7s.
6d., hardly a schoolboy will go without those
articles. The watches are valued at £1 55., and
are despatched post-free. Brussels carpets, in the
latest designs, are offered at 2s. per yard, and the
material is valued at nearly three times the price.
All the carpeting, linoleums, and floorcloths will
be laid, free of charge, by a competent staff.
Smokers can get tobacco at 3d. per plug, and
choice Havana cigars at 6s. per box of 50, being at
the rate of under ljd. per cigar. Mr. Herman is
constantly in receipt of letters acknowledging the
great bargains his customers have made. The
suit a Cowra man got in his £2 bale, together with
a large number of articles of wearing apparel, he
said was worth at least £4. From Bathurst, a
man wrote stating he reckoned the tea he had got
at a cost of about lOd. was worth 2s. per lb.*
At Allan vale, Vic., rabbits are being got
rid of by blocking all the burrows but two. Into
one of these a syphon from a big watertank on a
dray is inserted, and the warren thus flooded. At
the other stands a man who seizes the bunnies one
by one as they rush out to escape the water.
The “patriarch” of N.S.W. liquordom is
publican Davidson, who has the monopoly of the
Sydney horse-sale yards at Camperdown, and in
whose parlor hundreds of thousands sterling
change hands annually—the horse-auction business
being conducted on a strictly spot-cash basis.
Mr. Davidson has been licensed for 35 years. J
Melb. St. Patrick’s Cathedral, after being
about 40 years in the building, is to be opened at
last on the first Sunday in November next.
Cancer is largely on the increase in Maori-
land, the number of cases having risen from 2'69
per 10,000 persons living in 1881 to over 6 per
10,000 last year. Six men die there of cancer
yearly for every five women.
Victoria’s rainfall last year was only 17in.,
or Bin. below the average. At Mount Lyell, Tas.,
however, the record was 12ft.
Cabled that Prince Henry of Battenberg,
who went with the Ashantee expedition (which
reached Coomassie safely and obtained the King’s
submission) contracted fever and was invalided to
the coast.

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir, —In the year of 1871 I was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Friday I took the first of
your Pills; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.

A well-known Melb. penlady, on a recent
Sunday, pedalled to Sorrento and back—l2o
miles—and, after tea, wrote up her trip for her
paper.
The new Westralian daily, the Morning
Herald, started with the new year. Gordon
Stronaoh, late of Town and Country Journal
and of the defunct Sydney Echo, is sub-editor.
Melb. University snake-man, Davies, was,
on 4th Jan., bitten by a savage tiger snake, which
hung on to his finger for several seconds. Davies
quietly lifted the snake off, put it back in the
cage, wiped his finger on his trouser-leg, and
calmly went on “ milking ” the rest of the animals.
That was all the treatment that snakebite got—nd
Btryohnine, no chloride of lime, no anything.
Cyclist Zimmerman visited Melb. Royal
Panto, the other evening, and sat right through
the show, evidently enjoying it immensely
until it was shown now the noble, God-fearing
British were going to wipe out the Yankees and
make the Stars and Stripes into a gridiron. Then
Zim. gathered up his soft hat and, sighing more in
sorrow than in anger, passed out into Bourke-
street, and gave his check to a small boy at the
door.
At a recent meeting of Melb. Board of
Works, a warlike member named Cody rose to
“ draw attention to the unsettled state of Europe.”
He proposed that all the men who dig out dirt
for the Board should be incorporated into a regi-
ment with EitzGibbon as “general,” and the
Board as officers, but the unsympathetic sewerage
men weren’t on. Probably they thought that two
or three hundred corporation laborers shaking a
futile mud-shovel at Germany, and F. Gibbon
blowing a military poem in the van, would leave
the state of Europe about as unsettled as it was
before.
In ’B6 Parkes opposed the Parliamentary
grant of .£lO,OOO to old Jack Robertson, on the
ground that it would establish a dangerous pre-
cedent :
I am anxious that this Parliament in this young country
should go on free—entirely free—from public grants to
politicians, and from pensions to politicians. If men
come here let them take the consequences. If they
neglect their own affairs let them pay the penalty ; but
do not reward them by grants of public money or by
pensions from the public revenue. And our institutions
will he safer, purer and more elevated, and more a pattern
to surrounding colonies, the more we steer clear of any
touching of public money for such purposes as these. I
am extremely pained, as I have said several times, at
having to utter these sentiments, but I feel I stand upon a
rock in giving utterance to them.
The tricky old man thought he was standing on a
rock, but he was really standing on a snag. As
The Bulletin has often remarked, there is
nothing under Heaven which Parkes ever advo-
cated that he did not previously or subsequently
oppose—and vice-versa.

Notice is hereby given, that the Thirty-sixth ORDINARY HALF-YEARLY MEETING of
the Shareholders of the above Company will be held in the board-room, at the Company’s Offices. No,
412 Collins-street, on THURSDAY, 23rd INST., at 11 o’clock.
BUSINESS :
1. To receive the directors’ report and balance-sheet for the half-year ended 31st December, 1895.
2. To elect two auditors in place of Messrs. Paterson and Cleveland, who retire, but who are
eligible for re-election, and offer themselves accordingly.
3. To consider any other business that may be brought before the meeting, of which due notice
shall have been given.
REPORT.
In order to make full provision, not only for the fall in the present value of securities, but for other
contingent losses which may arise, the directors have transferred .£45,000 from the reserve fund to a
contingent account. A thorough and careful examination leads the directors to believe that the
amount so transferred will be sufficient, and as there is a sum of about =012,000 commission on corpus
m estates (<£480,000) not yet dealt with in addition to the amount carried forward, not only may the
idea of a call be dismissed, but there is every reason to believe that, with business continuing as at
present, dividends may be resumed at the end of the current half-year.
