The point reserved for argument in the
® a^our case is as to whether the evidence on
which he got convicted “ was sufficient to warrant
s ® n dmg the case to a jury.” What a mad wag
the Law can be when it likes 1 Nobody denies
that the evidence was sufficient to warrant a
sentence of 14 years’ hard labor for Jabez, but the
Law is solemnly asking itself whether this red-hot
person ought to have been committed for trial.
In the case q! a certain big Australian company-
promoter, the judge said it was quite right to
commit him for trial, although it would have been
wrong to convict him of felony. Jabez, maybe,
was properly convicted when he didn’t legally
deserve to be tried at all. In the case of
Matthew Davies, The Bulletin remembers
that, although the judge said there was
enough evidence to justify a committal,
Pan ton, P.M., had previously refused to sign a
warrant for the ex-Speaker’s prosecution. The
only official notice taken of Panton’s grave error
was the order of C.M.G. conferred upon him at
the request of the late Premier Patterson, or
somebody.
Editor Bulletin. —Your recent article on
Queensland politics omitted Hamlet — i.e., the two
politico-social forces which have of late years
attained a widespread and sinister influence in the
North. I refer to Freemasonry and It. G.-ism.
These two great powers are worked for all they
are worth in Q’sland, but more especially are their
evil influences felt in the various branches of the
Civil Service. Unless you are a “child ”of either
party your chances of promotion are “ Buckley’s.”
Scores of cases have occurred within the last three
years to illustrate my statement. Within the last
twelve months several have been so glaring that
the feeble press of this unfortunate land has
remarked upon the inequality of the ‘ * justice ”
meted out. Recently a high C.S. of “ the right
color ” was found guilty his own confession of a
system of petty peculation extending over several
years, and it was his second proved offence.
Sacked and imprisoned? No fear. The thing
was hushed up, and, only after the matter had
been exposed many months later, the offender was
transferred to a similar position elsewhere. Just
after this another C.S. with an unblemished
record of 23 years was detected in a breach of
trust, but he hadn’t the luck to belong to either
crowd, so he was promptly haled before the local
P.M., fined =£so, and immediately sacked. A man
in one of the other offices was, for unbecoming
conduct, recommended by his responsible head for
transfer from Brisbane to the sultry north, but,
lucky dog! he went to chapel regularly and
was, moreover, going to marry a relative of his
reverence. At the last minute, notwithstanding
his superior’s protest, the transfer was cancelled.
Another applied for six weeks’, leave of absence
(to contest a law-suit) and was advised it could
not be granted. Next day he applied personally
to his ministerial chief (his junior in the Lodge)
and in 24 hours the applicant was away. Another
one, absent on a medical certificate, was stated in
the Assembly (without coi tradiction) to have been
carted out of a country town suffering from
d.t’s. The Govt, burked discussion on the matter
and it was quietly dropped out of sight, as he
belonged to the right crowd. Columns could be
filled with similar instances, but sufficient has
been shown. These matters stink in the nostrils
of those who ask for a fair deal. Those who are
not “ one of us ” have a poor consolation in watch-
ing the wire-pulling when men on each side are
nominated for a vacancy or promotion—a case
then of “pull devil, pull baker.’ Until the pre-
sent political gang is fired out of office there is
little hope for a straight-goer.—N.Q.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following: 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long.*
“ The morphia and cocaine habits are
largely on the increase in Australia and constitute
a most serious danger. The less we accustom
patients to the use of the hypodermic syringe the
better. For this reason I would urge the use of
ethyl chloride [which needs no syringe], where
possible, in place of cocaine.” — Dr. Daish, at
Melb. Medical Association.

PROCLAMATION.
IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE Keg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. I,’’ have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ TZHZjRZEIE CASTLES” and “ CAPSTAIsr.”
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co.. Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles’’ and “Capstan’’ Ciyarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “ Capstan.” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HETDE, TODMAN & CO. [Agents for W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Ltd.)

Three Cups of Tea.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We tumbled across him in the nor’-west
comer of N.S. Wales. He was not what you
might call a rouseabout. He seemed a decent
sort of fellow, and his name was Good ; but lie
astonished us when he said he didn’t like tea.
“Not like tea ?” spluttered old Packer. “ You’re
not fool enough to drink the soup they call water
here ? ”
“Sometimes I boil it,” replied Good. “ Some-
times, if it’s extra-smelly, T boil it and put wood-
ashes in it; then I strain it through a handker-
chief.”
“ Well,” said Jim Bride, enigmatically, “wot
doesn’t fatten fills. If I’d strained some of the
post-and-rail tea I’ve seen there wouldn’t be much
left to drink. ”
A CCIDEN TAL.
MISS LEAD : “Did you hear that last report?”
MISS FITT: “ From where?”
MISS LEAD: “ Poor young Drinker didn't know he was loaded, and went off
and killed himself.”
One evening, when a common danger drew all
our party together in human friendliness and time
weighed leaden hours, Good told us why he didn’t
drink tea.
* * * *
“I had as good a claim on Flying Pig Ilill
as a man wants, and though I lived pretty freely I
managed to put by a few notes. I reckoned to
buy a farm down in Gippsland and take things
easy. I lived in a hut right on my claim ; but I
boarded at old Ching Foo’s, because I hated cook-
ing for myself.
“ Old Ching Foo kept a straight enough house,
and he treated me real well. He would yarn away
sometimes as if I was a relative of his, and once
or twice he gave me a nip of the best brandy I
ever tasted.
‘ ‘ One night when I’d had a glass or two and was
a bit excited like because the claim was doing so
well, Ching Foo said to me, ‘You come along my
house; I got
something I
show. You
not tell any
other fellow;
I say when
ffo.’
“It was
dark when we
started, but
Ching Foo
seemed to
know the road
all right, and
I followed him.
I don’t know
which way we
went, but we
didn’t walk
more than a
mile when we
struck a house,
and Ching Foo
took me in.
He put me
down in a
snug chair in
a room where
one o r two
lights burned
softly, and
there was a
curious tea-ey
smell. Ching
Foo left me;
then the lights
wentup gradu-
ally, and in
came Ching
Foo again, but
he was dressed
di ff erent 1 y.
He had on a
blue, shiny
coat and loose
white pants,
and his pigtail
hung down
like a rope.
“We walked
into anoih :r
room, and
there we had
dinner. Not
a boarding-
house feed ;
but curried
fowl, all sorts of vegetables, and such pud-
dings as I’d never tasted before. After dinner
we had cigars; and then a young woman—a little,
slim creature with a veil over her face—brought
in some tea, and I drank it. Now, that was tea.
The smell of it seemed to send one into a kind of
dream, and the taste of it made the dream as nice
as a dream could be. I saw old Ching Foo smil-
ing away ; then he handed me the box of cigars
and said: ‘You smoke these; velly good. I
smoke pipe ’—and I knew by the old fellow’s face
that he was going to take his opium.
“ I was in a most inexpressibly comfortable mood
—perhaps the whisky I’d had in the afternoon
might have softened me a bit, but it was that tea
that seemed to set me off. The girl re-entered
with another cup. She came close against me,
and there seemed to be a sort of magnetism in her.
When she took the saucer she seemed to touch my
knee, and I declare that touch made me feel as if
I’d known her all my life and had been in love
with her since I could remember. She was
dressed in something thin and soft; something
that showed her rounded shoulders and bust.
Lord, the very sight of her was enough to make a
fellow feel drunk ! She thought I’d finished my
tea, and stooped over me to take the cup ; and
then—l swear I couldn’t help it—l slid the cup to
the floor and put my arms round her. She didn’t
draw back ; she seemed to think that I’d a right
to do it. She snuggled softly against me ; then
she somehow dropped her veil and I saw her face.
It was such a face as I’d never seen before, and I
suppose I shall never see again. It was white as
milk, and small, and her eyes were beautiful as—
as ; and they looked into mine, and I
stooped down and kissed her. She whispered
‘Are you afraid of Ching Foo?’ and I laughed
low. As if I’d be afraid of anybody then !
“ ‘Afraid of him ! ’ said I.
“ ‘0 hush,’ she said, ‘Be quiet, or he will come.’
“ I kept quiet, for I had my arms around her, and
I didn’t want to move ever again. The whole
thing seemed like a dream, but I knew it was real.
Such a dainty little figure, and her skin as soft
and white as a flower.
“ There was a slight sound, and in a moment she
was out of my arms and had disappeared.
“ Ching Foo came in, and we had a yarn. I told
him what the claim was yielding, and pretty well
all the best side of my affairs ; and he just nodded
his head.
“ Three nights after that Ching Foo asked me to
go home with him again, and I jumped at the
chance. It was just the same as before. The
room with the lights—the same sort of dinner, the
wonderful cup of tea, then Ching Foo took his
opium—then the girl coming in again. She ard I
drank the tea together. She sat by my side as
close as close could be. I kissed her soft, little
mouth and her hair—l could have kept on kissing
her all my life, and wanted nothing better to do.
We didn’t talk much, but she asked me my name,
and she got me to write it down on a bit of paper,
and when I wrote it she kissed me and slipped
away. Then Ching Foo came in.
“ I fished for another invitation, but old Ching
Foo didn’t savee my hints until one day he asked
me to lend him =£lo, and said he’d give me
security on a section of land he owned, and as he
couldn’t write he got Tom Abner and J erry Brash
to witness his mark.* As he signed it, he whis-
pered, ‘ You satisfy take tea alonga me, to-night? ’
and I said, ‘You bet I’m satisfied, and devilish
glad to get it.’
“ Old Ching Foo said, low-like, to me, ‘ You
sign paper, too.’ I was going to tell him that
there was no occasion for me to sign, but h(
seemed to know what was in my mind, and looked
sulky, so I, thinking of the chance of seeing that
girl, signed just to please him, and he laughed like
a happy boy.
It came again—room, dinner, cigars, the cup of
tea, and and my girl. She seemed to come
straight to my arms, and I held her fast.
‘ ‘ I drank the cup of tea at a gulp when I found
she would not share it. It tasted a bit strong,
being a trifle bitter. I wonder how I remember
this, for I had her in my arms before the cup was
well out of my hands. Her dark hair was loosened
and hung down in long wavy lines ; her veil lay
on the floor. I looked into her eyes, she looked
into mine ; then her lips seemed to come to mine,
her arms went round my neck Poof !
folks may talk about opium and that stuff; but
that girl was real. Could I remember it all so
well after so many years ? No, I held her, and I
don’t want heaven to be any better than that;
and it lasted—was it an hour or a second ?
“ When I came to myself I was in a stinking
little hovel that some Chinese gardeners used to
own, and when Tom Abner saw me he said,
‘ Well, I’m blamed ! Why, it’s town-talk that
you had bought Ching Foo’s section and had gone
down to Melbourne to float a big thing in mines.’
“ When I went to the township I found that
Ching Foo had presented a cheque signed by me
at the bank, and had drawn out all my money.
Tom Abner and Jerry Brash had seen me sign the
transfer, and had heard me say that it was all
right, and that I was satisfied.
♦ » * *
‘ ‘Well, and I was satisfied. Ching may have
got at me badly ; but I’d pay twice as much—aye,
a hundred times as much if I had it for another
meeting wit.li that girl.
Maori land.

The Little People .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Who are these strange small folk
That come into our homes as kings,
Asking nor leave nor grace,
Bending our necks to their yoke,
Taking the highest place,
And mastery of all things ?
Whence they come none may know,
But a wondrous land it must be ;
Angels in exile they
Seem in this dull world below ;
Creatures of sinful clay
We feel near their purity.
Clearer their young eyes are
Than the dew in the cups of flow’rs
Gleaming, when shines at dawn
Faintly, the morning’s one star—
Eyes whose still gaze, indrawn,
Sees things unseen by ours.
Deep in those orbs serene —
Little planets be-ringed and bright—
Mysteries marvellous lie :
Known unto us they might mean
Faith, without fear, to die,
All sure of the waiting light.
Dimpled their hands and small—
Would ye, therefore, their might contemn
Seem they for play designed ?
Fate, and the Future withal,
Weal, yea and Woe, of Mankind,
Lie hid in the palms of them.
Tyrants, whose terrible names
Make men pale, with delight intense,
Worshipping, kiss their feet ;
Touch of their little hands tames
Fiercest of hearts that beat—
So mighty is Innocence.
These are the children dear,
From a country unknown of cliait.
(Dim Land of Souls Unborn),
Rosy as morn they come here,
Filling with joy forlorn
Waste places in our hearts.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
My Mates.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The shed has cut out. The “ Birds o’ Freedom ”
(Maorilanders) have “took their break.” The
rest of us have been in town nearly three hours.
I sit under the lee of the empty beer-barrel on
the pub. verandah. It is ten minutes to four.
“ Frenchy ” stands on the door-step with a three-
inch scalp-wound on his head—caused casually.
He shoos the flies off as he mutters : “Trafalgar
must be r-revenge ! Waterloo must be r-revenge !”
The old boundary-rider is out in the middle of the
road, attired in pants, leggings, and grey hair.
“ Come out here, you blanky German blank!” he.
shouts.
Two or three others wander aimlessly about in
a more or less happy condition —rather more than
less. The old chap who was penning up is stroll-
ing about calling all and sundry blanky blanks
and reciting bits of Shakespeare.
Four o’clock. A dog-fight starts. Someone
kicks the old man’s dog. He breaks off in his re-
citative. “Here! the blanky blank who kicks
my blanky dog kicks me !”
Four-five. The boundary-rider and Frenchy
are out in the road clutching spasmodically at
each other. They fall. The blood from Frenchy’s
head runs into the boundary - rider’s mouth.
Curses, blood, dust, and joy. A long man with a
dog tries to pull them apart; fails ; gets annoyed.
“ Here ! you’re both blanky blanks. I’ll fight
the blanky pair of you !”
“ Oh, if I were an orator as Brutus is !” spouts
the old chap. Little Snapper: “Blank you and
Brutus, too ! you’re a blanky liar ! ” [Short en-
counter.]
4.20. The boundary-rider’s horses break away
from rail; five or six try to catch them and fall
over one another in the attempt. Pandemonium
broken loose! General atmosphere of oaths,
broken English, ditto faces, dog-fighting, dust-
flying. . . . .
4.40. Everybody singing ; general hilarity.
I fall asleep behind the beer-barrel, lulled by the
sweet sounds, and remember no more.
Q.
P. R.
Dear Bulletin, —Re Prof. Morris’ dis-
cussion of the origin of Australian words, it
often strikes me, while listening-to the mopoke —or
morepork, or mopork, or morepoke, or mopehawk,
or whatever his name may be—that a name, more
than any appropriate with reference both to the
sound of its call, and the time, as well as in asso-
ciation with Marcus Clarke’s interpretation of
Australia’s dominant note, would be—“ No hope!”
In moments of mental depression this bird’s call
has had a weird suggestiveness to me. If Poe had
heard the bird, “ the raven ” would hardly have
been written in its present form.— Danton.

A Modern Romance.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Whether it was that she was physically the least
unattractive woman in the small island community
or whether Marden really found in her that
communion of soul—which sex-novelists claim as
essential to a joyous existence--is, after all, of
little moment. But the fact was indisputable
that Mrs. Gorlier—or “ Gorlier’s old woman, as
the unsophisticated islanders termed her—claimed
far more of his time and attention than is usually
devoted by bachelors to married acquaintances of
the opposite sex—and that his attentions were
appreciated.
Gorlier himself
—a weak-eyed in-
dividual with an
irresolute hand
for ever fingering
an undecided lip
—tacitly ac-
quiesced in the
position, and
threw his hospit-
able door open
every evening.
Then, his guest
comfortably en-
sconced in the
parlor, he would
consider his du-
ties as host ended,
and would sham-
ble off down the
little worn track
in the direction
of the Travellers’
Arms, to be seen
no more before
day-break.
An enforced
business trip to
the mainland
caused a tempor-
ary break in these
friendly rela-
tions, but the
evening of Mar-
den’s return saw
him sauntering
up to the friendly
h miestead the
door of which
was, as usual,
opened by the
host himself.
Gorlier looked
even feebler and
more undecided
than usual.
“ Evening,”
s fid the guest,
with an air of
cheerful patronage (for it was impossible to respect
Gorlier), “ Wife in, I suppose?”
Gorlier’s indecision deepened into something
like confusion. “Yes—she’s in—oh, yes —she’s in,
but ”
“But what, old man?” queried Marden sharply,
for, you see, he had been absent from the friendly
parlor for nearly a week, and the night air was
beginning to strike chilly.
Gorlier paused, and then raised his hand to his
lip with the old irresolute attitude.
“She’s gone,” he said, “she’s passed away.
My right hand has been taken from me.”
Marden staggered back as if he had been struck.
“Not dead, my God; not dead ! ” he whispered.
“Yes,” said Gorlier, simply. “But come in-
come in, you know the way almost as well as
I do.”
Marden, suppressing his emotion, eyed him
keenly for a moment. The remark savored of irony,
but Gorlier was obviously innocent of suoh mental
effort.
“ She always took to you, Marden,” he went on,
“she’s inside there” (indicating the adjacent
room). “Perhaps you’d like to see her. They
are just laying her out.”
Marden made a hurried motion of dissent.
“No! Well, p’raps not —p’raps not. You’ll
take the old chair. She—l mean, I —won’t be
long.”
He was already at the door, but paused. “ Oh,
maybe you have the price of a glass of beer about
you? This watching—thanks, a bob will do nicely.”
Marden waited until his footsteps had died
away, and then sank into a chair. A strange
sound in the next room riveted his attention,
and, with nervous intensity, he found himself lis-
tening to the hushed footsteps of the friendly
neighbors, who were performing the last offices to
the woman whose society had been so much to
him.
Meanwhile, down the beaten track shambled
the bereaved husband, mentally calculating the
amount of liquid enjoyment that lay before him.
With these anticipations were mingled regrets—
keen regrets—that, distraught by the painful
memories of his recent loss, he should have
stooped to borrow a shilling from Marden—when
he might just as easily have asked for and ob-
tained two. the Laird.
This brief year’s events demonstrate the
wisdom of a step taken by the U. 5.28 years ago,
in buying Alaska for a song, from Russia, which
would not let England have it for untold gold.
Bananas are now being grown at Horsham
(Vic.) with success. One tree in the local
botanical gardens, brought from Q. two years ago
in a din. box, is now 35ft. high, and bearing lots
of fruit.
The blood of animals which have been
innoculated with extract of a cancer tumor is found
to have a beneficial effect in human cancer cases,
diminishing pain, ameliorating ulcers, reducing
the bulk of the tumor, checking the advance of the
disease, and improving the general condition.

Mrs. Fry was once summoned to pray with
a moribund Norfolk squire. He said “Thanks,
Ma’am, but would you kindly wait till these have
done.” Two cocks were fighting at the other side
of the bed.
A recent Sydney E. News story has it
that, when a man in the bush picks his tobacco
with his fingernails, “ it’s an infallible sign that
he has served a long term of imprisonment ”—in-
stead of merely being an infallible sign that he
hasn’t got a knife.
When the Ghiniquy riots occurred at
Hobart, one of the special constables sworn in was
an Irish dentist. Sez he, as one day he walked
the streets with another special, truncheon in
hand, “Look here, old man, don’t spare the
ruffians ! Just you knock their teeth down their
throats ! ” Two birds with one stone.
Reform is urgently demanded in connec-
tion with the N.S.W. detective force. The sala-
ries at present paid are utterly and ludicrously
insufficient. If the men are to do their work pro-
perly, they must run a big bill for travelling and
like expenses, which the Govt, should foot. It
obviously follows that the capable officer, who
pays his own expenses out of his small salary,
must have other sources of income, into which
close inquiry cannot be made. Hence, salaries
should be increased, and allowance made for
money out of pocket. It is also probable that no
more detectives will be recruited from the regular
police, as by the time Robert gets to the detective
office the criminal population are familiar with his
homely countenance.
